Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
10 min
The Certainty Maxim: 'Certainty is Not Removed by Doubt'
Overview of the third major maxim and its seven sub-rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' by identifying the correct presumption (istishab) in cases of doubt and distinguishing its seven sub-rules and their exceptions.

Core Idea
Certainty endures despite doubt; a default can be overturned only by a stronger certainty.

This lesson unpacks the third of the five major legal maxims in the Hanafi tradition: al-yaqin la yuzalu bi al-shakk. The maxim is a cornerstone of Hanafi fatwa methodology, providing stability in legal presumptions across all chapters of fiqh. We will explore its seven sub-rules, each a distilled principle for handling doubt, and see how they apply to real cases in worship, family law, transactions, and more.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the maxim's meaning and authority
Understand that certainty (yaqin) is a state of knowledge free from doubt, and that it cannot be removed by mere doubt (shakk). This is based on the principle of istishab (presumption of continuity), which holds that a state of affairs remains until proven otherwise.
This maxim is universally accepted in the Hanafi school and is considered a foundational principle.
2
Recite the seven sub-rules
The passage lists seven sub-rules (qawaid) that derive from the maxim: (1) what remains as it was, (2) innocence of liability, (3) non-occurrence when doubting an act, (4) non-existence, (5) attributing events to the nearest time, (6) the original ruling on things (permissibility, prohibition, or suspension), and (7) the original ruling on private parts is prohibition.
Each sub-rule addresses a specific type of doubt scenario.
3
Apply sub-rules to concrete examples
Work through the applications given in the passage: e.g., doubt in purity (sub-rule 1), doubt in prayer rak'ahs (sub-rule 3), wife's denial of maintenance (sub-rule 2), dispute over profit (sub-rule 4), finding impurity on a garment (sub-rule 5), and the ruling on slave women (sub-rule 7).
These examples show the maxim in action across different fiqh chapters.
4
Identify exceptions (istithna'at)
The passage notes that there are cases where the maxim is not applied. For instance, when a specific impurity is certain, doubt does not remove it; other maxims like 'hardship brings ease' may override. Recognize that the maxim is general but not absolute.
Exceptions are rare and require strong evidence.
5
Understand the role of istishab in fatwa
The maxim is closely tied to istishab, which is a proof (dalil) in the Hanafi school. In issuing fatwas, the mufti relies on this presumption when there is no contrary evidence. The passage concludes with a discussion of the definition and authority of istishab.
Istishab is considered a weaker proof than text or consensus but is widely used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثالثة : اليقين لا يزول بالشك ؛ وفيها قواعد الأولى : الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان وبيان ما تفرع عليها من الطهارات والعبادات والطلاق وإنكار المرأة وصول النفقة إليها واختلاف الزوجين في التمكين من الوطء والسكوت والرّدّ والرجعة في العدة وبعدها واختلاف المتبايعين في الطوع ودعوى المطلقة الحبل. الثانية : الأصل براءة الذمة؛ وفيها بيان الاختلاف في القيمة والجواب عما ورد عليها. الثالثة : مَن شكٍّ هل فعل أو لا؟ فالأصل عدمه ؛ ويدخل فيها من تيقن الفعل وشك في القليل والكثير وبيان أن ما ثبت باليقين لا يزول إلا باليقين، وبيان الشك في الوضوء والصلاة هل صلاها أو لا ؟ والشك في تعيين الفرض المتروك، وبيان ما إذا أخبره عدل بترك شيء منها، والاختلاف بين الإمام والقوم وبيان الشك في أركان الحج وفي الطلاق وعدده وفي الخارج من ذكره وفي قدر الدين وما يدعى عليه، وفي الزكاة والصوم والمنذور، وفي اليمين من كونها بالله تعالى أو بطلاق أو عتاق. الرابعة الأصل العدم، وفيها بيان الاختلاف في وصول العنين وفي ربح المشارك والمضارب، وفي أن المال قرض أو مضاربة وفي قدم العيب، وفي اشتراط الخيار والرؤية، وفي بيان الشك في وصول اللبن إلى جوف الرضيع بعدما أدخلت ثديها في فمه، وفي آخرها التنبيه على تقييد القاعدة وبيان ما خرج منها. الخامسة: الأصل إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته وبيان وجود النجاسة في الثوب والفأرة في البئر، وبيان ما إذا أقرّ بفقء عين العبد في ملك البائع وكذبه المشتري، وفي اختلاف الورثة مع المرأة في إبانتها في المرض أو الصحة وفي اختلافهم في كون الإقرار لبعضهم في الصحة أو المرض. وفيها لو اختلفوا في إسلامها بعد موت الزوج أو قبله، وفي الاختلاف بين القاضي المعزول وغيره وبيان ما خرج عن هذه القاعدة السادسة: هل الأصل في الأشياء الإباحة أو الحظر أو التوقف؟ وبيان ثمرة الاختلاف في ذلك. السابعة: الأصل في الابضاع التحريم ؛ وفيها مسائل التحري في الفروج وبيان الطلاق المبهم والعتق المبهم والمنسي وبيان ما خرج عنها وفي بيان وطء السراري اللاتي يُجْلَبن الآن من الروم والهند وأن أصحابنا احتاطوا في الفروج إلا في مسألة، وفيها قاعدة وهي : الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة، وبيان ما فرع عليها وبيان ما يشمل الصحيح والفاسد وما يختص بالصحيح وبيان ما أورد عليها مع جوابه، وفيها خاتمة فيها قواعد الأولى : يُستثنى من قولهم اليقين لا يزول بالشك مسائل. الثانية : بيان الشك والوهم والظن وغالب الظن وأكبر الرأي الثالثة في بيان حد الاستصحاب وحجيته وما فرع عليه . وسع
The third rule: Certainty is not removed by doubt. Under it are rules: First: The original rule is that what was remains as it was, and an explanation of what is derived from it regarding purifications, acts of worship, divorce, the wife's denial of receiving maintenance, the spouses' disagreement on permission for intercourse, silence, revocation, and return during and after the waiting period, the buyers' disagreement on consent, and the divorced woman's claim of pregnancy. Second: The original rule is the innocence of the liability; under it is an explanation of the disagreement on value and the response to objections against it. Third: If one doubts whether an act was done or not, the original rule is its non-occurrence; and included in it is one who is certain of the act but doubts in the small or large amount, and an explanation that what is established by certainty is only removed by certainty, and an explanation of doubt in ablution and prayer whether he performed it or not, and doubt in specifying the omitted obligatory act, and an explanation of when a reliable person informs him of omitting something thereof, and the disagreement between the imam and the congregation, and an explanation of doubt in the pillars of Hajj, in divorce and its number, in what exits from his penis, in the amount of debt and what is claimed against him, in zakat, fasting, vows, and in the oath whether it is by Allah the Exalted or by divorce or emancipation. Fourth: The original rule is non-existence; under it is an explanation of the disagreement on the arrival of the impotent man, on the profit of the partner and the mudarib, on whether the money is a loan or mudaraba, on the pre-existence of a defect, on stipulating the option of inspection, and on the doubt of milk reaching the stomach of the suckling after she inserted her nipple into his mouth, and at the end is a reminder to restrict the rule and an explanation of what is excluded from it. Fifth: The original rule is attributing an event to its nearest time, and an explanation of finding impurity on the garment and a mouse in the well, and an explanation of when someone admits to gouging out the eye of a slave while in the seller's possession and the buyer denies it, and the disagreement of the heirs with the woman regarding her being divorced during illness or health, and their disagreement on whether the admission in favor of some of them occurred in health or illness. And under it: if they disagree on her embracing Islam after the husband's death or before it, and the disagreement between the dismissed judge and others, and an explanation of what is excluded from this rule. Sixth: Is the original rule in things permissibility, prohibition, or suspension? And an explanation of the fruit of the disagreement concerning that. Seventh: The original rule regarding private parts is prohibition; under it are matters of seeking guidance regarding genitals, an explanation of ambiguous divorce, ambiguous emancipation, and forgotten, an explanation of what is excluded from it, and an explanation of intercourse with slave women who are now brought from Byzantium and India, and that our companions were cautious regarding private parts except in one case, and under it is a rule: the original rule in speech is the literal meaning, an explanation of what is derived from it, an explanation of what includes the valid and invalid, and what is specific to the valid, and an explanation of what was objected to it along with its answer, and under it is a conclusion containing rules: First: Exceptions are made from their statement 'certainty is not removed by doubt' certain cases. Second: An explanation of doubt, illusion, speculation, predominant speculation, and greater opinion. Third: An explanation of the definition of istishab, its authority, and what is derived from it. And [the discussion] expanded.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage introduces the third major legal maxim: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt.' It then lists seven sub-rules that fall under this maxim, each with its own areas of application. The first sub-rule states that what was previously established remains as it was until proven otherwise, applying to purity, worship, divorce, and financial matters. The second sub-rule is the presumption of innocence of liability. The third sub-rule deals with doubt about whether an act occurred, where the default is non-existence. The fourth sub-rule presumes non-existence in cases like whether profit accrued. The fifth sub-rule attributes events to the nearest possible time. The sixth sub-rule discusses whether the original ruling on things is permissibility, prohibition, or suspension. The seventh sub-rule states that the original ruling on private parts is prohibition. The passage concludes with a chapter on exceptions to the maxim, definitions of terms like doubt and istishab, and the authority of istishab. This is a comprehensive overview of the applications and limitations of the certainty maxim within Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
اليقين (al-yaqīn): Certain knowledge that is free from doubt; a state of mind where no possibility of error exists.
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt; when one is uncertain between two possibilities without preponderance.
الاستصحاب (al-istishāb): Presumption of continuity; the legal principle that a state of affairs continues until evidence of change arises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt,' if a person is certain that he performed wudu but later doubts whether he broke it, what is the ruling?
A) He must repeat wudu because doubt invalidates certainty.
B) He is considered to still have wudu because certainty is not removed by doubt.
C) He should perform tayammum as a precaution.
D) He must combine both actions.

Key Takeaway
Certainty is not removed by doubt; this maxim underpins many Hanafi rulings by providing stability in legal presumptions.

Fourth Major Maxim: Hardship Brings Ease (المشقة تجلب التيسير)
Introducing the fourth major legal maxim and its list of leniency causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir', list its seven causes of leniency, and apply it to derive fiqh concessions in line with Hanafi usul al-iftaa.

Core Idea
Hardship triggers ease: the Sharia relaxes its strictness when genuine difficulty arises.

This lesson introduces one of the five major maxims of Hanafi fiqh: 'Hardship brings ease.' Understanding when and how the Sharia grants concessions is essential for a mufti. The passage outlines seven recognized causes that justify leniency and highlights the balanced approach of the imams. We will also explore the four important conclusions that refine the application of this maxim.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim and its Arabic wording
Start by committing the Arabic of the maxim to memory: 'المشقة تجلب التيسير'. This is a foundational principle that appears across all chapters of fiqh.
The maxim is classified as a kubra (major) and kulliya (universal) rule.
2
Identify the seven causes of leniency
From the passage, list the seven asbab al-takhfif: travel (السفر), illness (المرض), coercion (الإكراه), forgetfulness (النسيان), ignorance (الجهل), general difficulty (العسر), and widespread affliction (عموم البلوى).
The cause 'النقص' (deficiency) appears in the Arabic text but is sometimes subsumed under other categories; note its mention.
3
Understand the scope of the maxim
The maxim applies across all areas of fiqh, including ibadat, mu'amalat, and jinayat. It operates as a principle of facilitation when specific texts do not explicitly forbid the concession.
The passage highlights that hardship is considered only in the absence of a clear text (nass).
4
Examine the four important benefits (فوائد مهمة)
The passage concludes with four points: (1) Hardships are of two types – severe and mild; (2) Alleviations are of various types (e.g., dropping obligations, reducing, postponing); (3) Hardship is only considered when no explicit text exists; (4) The balanced nature of Sharia: 'When a matter becomes constrained, it expands.'
These benefits help a mufti correctly apply the maxim without overextending it.
5
Apply the maxim to a furu' case
Take a concrete scenario: a traveler during Ramadan. Apply the maxim: due to hardship of travel, the concession to break the fast is granted. Check if any explicit text prevents it – none, so the maxim applies.
This step internalizes the use of the maxim in real fatwa work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الرابعة : المشقَّة تجلب التيسير وبيان أن أسباب التخفيف سبعة : السفر والمرض والإكراه والنسيان والجهل والعُسْر وعموم البلوى والنقص، وفيه بيان ما فيه أبو حنيفة من العبادات وغيرها على هذه الأمة، وما وسع فيه الأئمة الأربعة، وختمنا هذه بفوائد مهمة : الأولى : المشاق على قسمين وفيها تنبيه في الفرق بين مرض الزوج ومرضها . الثانية أن تخفيفات الشرع أنواع الثالثة : أن المشقة والحرج إنما يعتبران عند عدم النص الرابعة : بيان قولهم إذا ضاق الأمر اتسع، وإذا اتسع ضاق، وبيان ما جمع بينهما .
The fourth rule: Hardship brings ease, and an explanation that the causes of leniency are seven: travel, illness, coercion, forgetfulness, ignorance, difficulty, widespread affliction, and deficiency. Under it is an explanation of what Abu Hanifa facilitated in acts of worship and other matters for this ummah, and what the four imams facilitated. And we concluded this with important benefits: First: Hardships are of two types, and in it is a note on the difference between the husband's illness and her illness. Second: The alleviations of the Sharia are of various types. Third: Hardship and difficulty are only considered in the absence of a text. Fourth: An explanation of their statement: 'When a matter becomes constrained, it expands; and when it expands, it constricts,' and an explanation of what combines them.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth major legal maxim: 'Hardship brings ease.' It lists seven causes that justify leniency in rulings: travel, illness, coercion, forgetfulness, ignorance, general difficulty, and widespread need. The passage highlights that Imam Abu Hanifa and the other three imams made concessions in various areas out of mercy for the community. It then provides four important conclusions: (1) hardships are divided into categories; (2) the types of legal alleviations; (3) hardship is only considered when no explicit text exists; (4) when a matter becomes tight, it expands, and vice versa, showing the balanced nature of Sharia.

Key Terms
المشقة (al-mashaqqa): Significant hardship or difficulty that is not normally tolerable in religious obligations.
التيسير (al-taysīr): Ease or facilitation; a legal concession (rukhsa) granted to alleviate hardship.
أسباب التخفيف (asbāb al-takhfīf): The seven causes that justify leniency: travel, illness, coercion, forgetfulness, ignorance, general difficulty, and widespread affliction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the seven causes of leniency mentioned under the maxim 'Hardship brings ease'?
A) Travel
B) Anger
C) Illness
D) Forgetfulness

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir' is a foundational principle that guides the application of concessions in Hanafi fiqh, based on seven recognized causes of hardship.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
beginner

The Maxim 'Certainty is Not Removed by Doubt' and Its Sub-Topics
Outline of sub-topics for the first major maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three sub-topics of the first major qawida 'al-yaqīn lā yuzāl bi al-shakk' and explain the significance of each in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Certainty stands until definitive proof overturns it; doubt cannot break certainty.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, the maxim 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' (Certainty is not removed by doubt) is a fundamental principle that stabilizes legal rulings. This passage outlines the structure for understanding this maxim: first, exceptions where doubt does overturn certainty; second, clarifications of key terms like doubt, illusion, and conjecture; and third, the related principle of istishab (presumption of continuity). Mastering this outline will help you navigate many fiqh rulings that depend on the status of certainty and doubt.

Guided Path
1
State the Maxim and Its Basic Meaning
Begin by memorizing the maxim 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' and its English equivalent. Understand that it establishes a strong presumption in favor of existing certainty.
This maxim is one of the five major qawaid al-kulliyya in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Identify the Three Sub-Topics Listed
Read the passage and list the three sets of rules: (1) exceptions to the maxim, (2) definitions of shakk, wahm, zann, ghalabat al-zann, and akbar al-ra'y, (3) the definition and authority of istishab and rulings derived from it.
These sub-topics are the building blocks for applying the maxim in complex cases.
3
For Each Sub-Topic, Note Its Purpose
Explain why each sub-topic is necessary: exceptions prevent rigid application; definitions ensure precise legal reasoning; istishab provides a methodology for preserving certainty.
Istishab is a key tool when no direct evidence exists.
4
Recognize the Importance of Istishab as a Corollary
Understand that istishab is the practical application of the maxim: a past certainty continues until a contrary certainty is established.
Hanafis accept istishab as a hujja (proof) but with conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قواعد الأولى : يُستثنى من قولهم اليقين لا يزول بالشك مسائل. الثانية : بيان الشك والوهم والظن وغالب الظن وأكبر الرأي الثالثة في بيان حد الاستصحاب وحجيته وما فرع عليه . وسع
First rules: Exceptions to their statement 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' are issues. Second: Explanation of doubt, illusion, conjecture, prevalent conjecture, and majority opinion. Third: Explanation of the limit of istishab (presumption of continuity), its authority, and what is derived from it.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is an outline of sub-topics related to the first major maxim, 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' (Certainty is not removed by doubt). The author lists three sets of rules: first, the exceptions to this maxim—meaning there are cases where doubt does remove certainty; second, a clarification of terms such as doubt (shakk), illusion (wahm), conjecture (zann), prevalent conjecture (ghalabat al-zann), and majority opinion (akbar al-ra'y); third, a discussion of the legal principle of istishab (presumption of continuity), its definition (hadd), its authority (hujjiyya), and the rulings derived from it. The word 'وسع' likely indicates a continuation or expansion, possibly referencing further elaboration.

Key Terms
اليقين (al-yaqīn): Certainty; a firm belief with no possibility of doubt.
الاستصحاب (al-istiṣḥāb): Presumption of continuity; the principle that a state existing in the past continues until proven otherwise.
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt; equal inclination towards two possibilities.
الوهم (al-wahm): Illusion; a weak possibility that does not reach the level of doubt.
الظن (al-ẓann): Conjecture; a belief with stronger inclination towards one possibility, but not certainty.
غالب الظن (ghālib al-ẓann): Prevalent conjecture; a very strong inclination that nearly reaches certainty.
أكبر الرأي (akbar al-ra’y): Majority opinion; the view preferred by most scholars, used in mufti's tarjih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the maxim 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' mean?
A) Certainty is removed by doubt
B) Certainty is not removed by doubt
C) Doubt is preferable to certainty
D) Certainty and doubt are equal

Key Takeaway
The first major qawida has three sub-topics: exceptions, definitions of doubt-related terms, and the principle of istishab, which form the foundation for rulings on certainty and doubt.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Maxim 'Harm Must Be Removed' and Its Sub-Rules
Introducing the maxim and its related sub-rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ḍarar yuzāl' to a novel fiqh scenario, identify the governing sub‑rule (e.g., 'al‑ḍarar lā yuzāl bi al‑ḍarar'), and determine the muftā bihi ruling using the Hanafi qawāʿid hierarchy.

Core Idea
Harm must be removed, but not by causing equal harm; necessity permits only proportional relief.

This lesson unpacks the fifth major Hanafi legal maxim: harm must be removed. As a mufti, you will encounter cases where removing harm seems straightforward but is constrained by sub‑rules: necessity permits only what is necessary, harm cannot be removed by inflicting harm, and public interest may override private harm. Mastering this maxim and its exceptions is essential for issuing sound fatwas in areas from personal injury to public policy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the harm
Determine what constitutes harm (ḍarar) in the case. Harm includes physical injury, property damage, or any unlawful detriment. The maxim commands its removal.
2
Apply the general maxim
State the general rule: 'al‑ḍarar yuzāl' (harm must be removed). This is the starting point for any remedy.
3
Check for sub‑rule restrictions
Examine whether removing the harm would violate a sub‑rule: (a) 'al‑ḍarūrāt tubīḥ al‑maḥẓūrāt' – necessity permits only what is needed; (b) 'al‑ḍarar lā yuzāl bi al‑ḍarar' – you cannot remove harm by causing equal harm; (c) 'mā ubīḥa li al‑ḍarūra yataqaddar bi qadrihā' – permission is limited to the extent of necessity.
The third sub‑rule (ḍarar lā yuzāl bi al‑ḍarar) is a restrictive dabṭ on the general maxim.
4
Consider exceptional circumstances
If private harm conflicts with public harm, the maxim may be overridden by 'drā’ al‑mafāsid muqaddam' – repelling harm takes precedence over bringing benefit. Private harm may be tolerated to prevent greater public harm.
Example: demolishing a private building to stop a fire from spreading.
5
Deliver the fatwa
Weigh all factors using the Hanafi hierarchy: prefer the position that avoids greater harm. Document the ruling and note any concession, reminding that the permission ends when the necessity ends.
Always reference the relevant sub‑rule and its evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الخامسة : الضرر يزال وبيان ما ابتني عليها من أبواب الفقه. ويتعلق بها قواعد الأولى الضرورات تبيح المحظورات الثانية : ما أُبيح للضرورة يتقدر بقدرها ويقرب منها ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله. الثالثة : الضرر لا يزال بالضرر، وبيان أنها مقيدة لما ،قبلها، وفيها بيان ما يتحمل فيه الضرر الخاص لدفع ضرر عام، وبيان فرع عليها. وفيها بيان ما إذا تعارض ضرران أو مفسدتان وبيان أحكام من ابتُلي ببليتين قولهم : درء المفاسد أولى من جلب المصالح، وما يتفرع عليها.
The fifth rule: Harm must be removed, and an explanation of what chapters of jurisprudence are built upon it. Related to it are rules: first: Necessities permit prohibitions. Second: What is permitted due to necessity is limited to its extent; and close to it: what is permitted due to an excuse becomes invalid when the excuse ceases. Third: Harm is not removed by harm, and its explanation that they are restricted to what precedes them. And in it is an explanation of when private harm is tolerated to repel public harm, and an explanation of a branch upon it. And in it is an explanation of when two harms or two corruptions conflict, and the rulings on one who is afflicted by two calamities, and their statement: 'Repelling harms is preferable to bringing benefits' and what branches from it.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage covers the fifth major maxim, 'الضرر يزال' (Harm must be removed). It states that many chapters of fiqh are based on this maxim. Three related sub-rules are listed: (1) 'Necessities permit prohibitions' (الضرورات تبيح المحظورات) meaning that in dire need, forbidden things become allowed. (2) What is allowed due to necessity is only permitted to the extent needed; similarly, what is allowed due to an excuse becomes invalid when the excuse ends. (3) 'Harm is not removed by harm' (الضرر لا يزال بالضرر), meaning you cannot remove another's harm by causing harm yourself. The text notes that this rule restricts the previous ones. It also discusses when private harm is tolerated to prevent public harm, and how to resolve conflicts between two harms or two corruptions, citing the principle 'Repelling harms is preferable to bringing benefits' (درء المفاسد أولى من جلب المصالح).

Key Terms
الضرر (al-ḍarar): Harm or injury; a legal cause for action or compensation.
الضرورة (al-ḍarūra): Necessity; a situation that permits normally prohibited acts.
درء المفاسد (dar' al-mafāsid): Repelling harms; a principle that prioritises preventing harm over acquiring benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the sub‑rule 'الضرر لا يزال بالضرر' mean?
A) Harm is removed by harm
B) Harm is not removed by harm
C) Harm can be compensated by harm
D) Harm is permanent

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al‑ḍarar yuzāl' is always qualified by its sub‑rules: necessity is limited, harm cannot be removed by harm, and public interest may override private harm.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Maxim 'Custom is Authoritative' and Its Conditions
Introduction to the maxim and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'العادة محكمة', identify its rank as a major maxim, and explain the condition that custom must be consistent or prevalent to be authoritative.

Core Idea
Custom is authoritative only if consistent or prevalent — rare custom carries no legal weight.

In this lesson, we explore the sixth major maxim of Hanafi legal theory: 'Custom is authoritative.' This maxim serves as a foundational tool for deriving rulings in the absence of explicit texts. It applies across all chapters of fiqh, from purification to transactions, as long as the custom is consistently observed or widely prevalent. We will examine the precise conditions for a custom to be legally binding.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Repeat the Arabic maxim 'العادة محكمة' and its English equivalent 'Custom is authoritative'.
2
Identify its rank
Recognize that this is one of the five major maxims (al-qawaid al-kubra) in Hanafi fiqh.
It is a universal rule (kulliya) governing all chapters.
3
Explain the condition
Custom is only considered if it is consistent (muṭṭarida) or prevalent (ghāliba). Rare customs (nādira) are not authoritative.
This condition prevents arbitrary or isolated practices from becoming legal norms.
4
List initial furuat
The passage lists applications such as defining flowing water, menstruation periods, and interpreting oaths. These show the maxim's wide scope.
Detailed furuat will be covered in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السادسة : العادة محكمة، وبيان ما فرع عليها من حدّ الماء الجاري والماء الكثير والحيض والنفاس والعمل المُفسِد للصلاة، وكون الشيء مكيلاً أو الشك موزونا، وصوم يوم ويومين قبل رمضان وقبول الهدية وجواز الأكل من الطعام المُقدَّم إليه بغير إذن صريح، وبناء الأيمان والنذور والوصايا والأوقاف عليها، وبيان ما ثبتت العادة به وبيان أنها إنما تعتبر إذا اطّردت أو غلبت لا إن ندرت. وفيها بيان حكم البطالة في المدارس، وفيه بيان مسامحة الإمام في كل شهر أسبوعا للاستراحة أو لزيارة أهله وفيها بيان تعارض العُرْف والشرع وتعارض العزف مع اللغة، وبيان ما خرج عن قولهم: الأيمان مبنية على العُرف، وبيان أن العادة المطردة تنزل منزلة الشرط، وما تفرّع عليه من استحقاق الأجرة بلا شرط إذا جرت العادة بأنه يعمل بالأجرة، وفيه بيان أن العارية إذا شرط ضمانها هل يصح أو لا؟ وبيان جهاز البنات وأنه لا يجب السؤال عند الشراء من الأسواق، وبيان أن العُزف الذي تحمل عليه الألفاظ إنما هو المقارن لا المتأخر وأنه لا يعتبر في التعاليق والدعاوى والأقارير، وفيه بيان أن الواقف إذا شرط النظر لحاكم المسلمين وكان في زمنه شافعيا ثم صار الآن حنفيا، هل يكون له أو لا؟ وبيان ما إذا شرط النظر للقاضي هل يكون لقاضي بلده أو الموقوف عليه؟ وفيه بيان أن المعتبر العُرْف العام لا الخاص. وهذا آخر القواعد الكلية .
The sixth rule: Custom is authoritative, and an explanation of what is derived from it: the definition of flowing water and abundant water, menstruation and postnatal bleeding, actions that invalidate prayer, whether a thing is measured by volume or weight, fasting a day or two before Ramadan, accepting gifts, permissibility of eating from food presented without explicit permission, building oaths, vows, bequests, and endowments upon it, and an explanation of what is established by custom, and that it is only considered if it is consistent or prevalent, not if rare. And in it is an explanation of the ruling on idleness in schools, and in it is an explanation of the imam's concession of one week per month for rest or visiting his family, and in it is an explanation of the conflict between custom and Sharia, and the conflict between custom and language, and an explanation of what is excluded from their statement: 'Oaths are based on custom,' and an explanation that a consistent custom takes the place of a condition, and what branches from it regarding entitlement to wages without a condition if the custom is that one works for wages, and in it is an explanation of whether a guarantee condition in a loan of use is valid or not, and an explanation of the trousseau of daughters and that questioning is not required when buying from markets, and an explanation that the custom upon which words are interpreted is the concurrent custom, not the subsequent one, and that it is not considered in suspended conditions, lawsuits, and confessions, and in it is an explanation that if the endower stipulates supervision for the ruler of Muslims and in his time he was Shafi'i then now has become Hanafi, does he have it or not? And an explanation of if he stipulates supervision for the qadi, is it for the qadi of his town or the one over the endowment? And in it is an explanation that the general custom is considered, not the specific. And this is the end of the universal rules.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth major maxim, 'العادة محكمة' (Custom is authoritative), and provides a long list of applications. The maxim means that customary practice can determine legal rulings in the absence of explicit textual evidence. The text lists many furu: determining definitions of flowing water and large water quantities (for purity), menstruation and postnatal bleeding, actions that nullify prayer, whether a commodity is sold by volume or weight, fasting before Ramadan, accepting gifts, eating without permission, basing oaths, vows, bequests, and endowments on custom. Custom must be consistent (muṭṭarida) or prevalent (ghāliba), not rare (nādira). Other topics: idleness in schools, the imam's weekly break, conflict between custom and Sharia or language, exceptions to the maxim 'oaths are based on custom', consistent custom as a condition, wages without explicit contract, guarantee in loan of use, the bride's trousseau, no need to ask in market purchases, the relevant custom for interpreting speech is concurrent custom, not later custom, and custom is not considered in suspended conditions, lawsuits, and confessions. Also, if a waqf stipulates supervision for a ruler who was Shafi'i but later becomes Hanafi, the custom of the time is considered; likewise stipulation for a qadi. The general custom prevails over specific custom. This concludes the universal rules (القواعد الكلية).

Key Terms
العادة (al-ʿāda): Custom; a recurring practice that is accepted as normative.
العُرْف (al-ʿurf): Prevailing custom; the common practice of people.
مُطَّرِد (muṭṭarid): Consistent; a custom that is continuously observed.
غَالِب (ghālib): Prevalent; a custom that is widespread, even if not universal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a custom considered authoritative?
A) When it is rare
B) When it is consistent or prevalent
C) When it contradicts Sharia
D) When it is local

Key Takeaway
For a custom to be authoritative in Hanafi law, it must be consistent or prevalent; rare customs are not considered.

Sub-Maxims and Applications of 'Custom is Authoritative'
Detailed applications of the maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the sub-maxims 'الأيمان مبنية على العرف' and 'العادة المطردة تنزل منزلة الشرط', and apply them to real cases such as entitlement to wages without explicit contract and validity of guarantee conditions in loans.

Core Idea
Consistent custom takes the place of an explicit condition and oaths are interpreted by custom.

In this lesson, we delve into specific sub-maxims derived from the major maxim 'Custom is authoritative.' The text highlights two key principles: first, oaths are built upon custom ('الأيمان مبنية على العرف'), meaning the common understanding of words determines oath violations; second, a consistent custom takes the place of a condition ('العادة المطردة تنزل منزلة الشرط'), so that in some contexts no explicit agreement is needed. These sub-maxims have practical applications in wages, loans, and domestic matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sub-maxims
From the passage, extract the sub-maxims: 'الأيمان مبنية على العرف' and 'العادة المطردة تنزل منزلة الشرط'.
These are specific applications of the main maxim.
2
Explain 'oaths are based on custom'
When someone swears an oath, the words are interpreted according to the customary usage of the community, not literal dictionary definitions.
Example: If one swears not to 'eat', but custom considers soup as food, then drinking soup violates the oath.
3
Explain 'consistent custom takes the place of a condition'
If it is customary in a trade or profession to provide a certain service or payment, the law enforces that custom even without an explicit contract term.
Example: A laborer is entitled to wages if customarily paid for that work, even without a prior agreement.
4
Apply to cases in the passage
The passage gives examples: entitlement to wages without condition (because custom dictates payment), and the ruling on a guarantee condition in a loan of use (ʿāriyah).
Also: the trousseau of daughters and the permissibility of not asking when buying from markets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Exceptions and Limitations: When Custom is Not Considered
Exceptions and limitations of the maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify situations where custom is not considered (suspended conditions, lawsuits, confessions), distinguish between general and specific custom, and apply the rule that concurrent custom governs interpretation.

Core Idea
Custom is not considered in suspended conditions, lawsuits, and confessions; general custom overrides specific custom.

While the maxim 'Custom is authoritative' has wide application, it is not unlimited. This lesson examines key exceptions and limitations explicitly mentioned in the passage: custom is not considered in suspended conditions (taʿālīq), lawsuits (daʿāwā), and confessions (iqrārāt). Additionally, the interpretation of words in legal documents must be based on concurrent custom (al-ʿurf al-muqārin), not subsequent custom. Finally, general custom (al-ʿurf al-ʿāmm) takes precedence over specific custom (al-ʿurf al-khāṣṣ). Understanding these boundaries is crucial for a mufti to avoid misapplication.

Guided Path
1
List the exceptions
The text explicitly excludes custom from being considered in three areas: suspended conditions, lawsuits, and confessions.
These matters require explicit intent or clear wording; custom cannot override the literal requirement.
2
Explain the concurrent custom rule
When interpreting words in legal contexts, the relevant custom is the one at the time of the utterance (muqārin), not a later custom (muta'akhkhir).
Example: A bequest made 50 years ago is interpreted according to the custom of that time.
3
Distinguish general and specific custom
General custom (al-ʿurf al-ʿāmm) prevails over specific custom (al-ʿurf al-khāṣṣ).
Example: If a local custom conflicts with a widespread custom, the general custom governs.
4
Apply to the endower case
If a waqf stipulates supervision for a 'ruler of Muslims' and the ruler was Shafi'i at the time of endowment but later becomes Hanafi, the custom of the time of endowment is considered (concurrent custom).
Also, if the stipulation is for a 'qadi', it is interpreted according to the customary meaning of the term at the time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السادسة : العادة محكمة، وبيان ما فرع عليها من حدّ الماء الجاري والماء الكثير والحيض والنفاس والعمل المُفسِد للصلاة، وكون الشيء مكيلاً أو الشك موزونا، وصوم يوم ويومين قبل رمضان وقبول الهدية وجواز الأكل من الطعام المُقدَّم إليه بغير إذن صريح، وبناء الأيمان والنذور والوصايا والأوقاف عليها، وبيان ما ثبتت العادة به وبيان أنها إنما تعتبر إذا اطّردت أو غلبت لا إن ندرت. وفيها بيان حكم البطالة في المدارس، وفيه بيان مسامحة الإمام في كل شهر أسبوعا للاستراحة أو لزيارة أهله وفيها بيان تعارض العُرْف والشرع وتعارض العزف مع اللغة، وبيان ما خرج عن قولهم: الأيمان مبنية على العُرف، وبيان أن العادة المطردة تنزل منزلة الشرط، وما تفرّع عليه من استحقاق الأجرة بلا شرط إذا جرت العادة بأنه يعمل بالأجرة، وفيه بيان أن العارية إذا شرط ضمانها هل يصح أو لا؟ وبيان جهاز البنات وأنه لا يجب السؤال عند الشراء من الأسواق، وبيان أن العُزف الذي تحمل عليه الألفاظ إنما هو المقارن لا المتأخر وأنه لا يعتبر في التعاليق والدعاوى والأقارير، وفيه بيان أن الواقف إذا شرط النظر لحاكم المسلمين وكان في زمنه شافعيا ثم صار الآن حنفيا، هل يكون له أو لا؟ وبيان ما إذا شرط النظر للقاضي هل يكون لقاضي بلده أو الموقوف عليه؟ وفيه بيان أن المعتبر العُرْف العام لا الخاص. وهذا آخر القواعد الكلية .

Key Terms
التعاليق (al-taʿālīq): Suspended conditions; legal consequences made conditional on future events, e.g., 'If I do X, then Y is divorced'.
الدعاوى (al-daʿāwā): Lawsuits; judicial claims.
الأقارير (al-iqrārāt): Confessions; admissions of fact.
العُرْف العام (al-ʿurf al-ʿāmm): General custom; a custom widely practiced across regions or communities.
العُرْف الخاص (al-ʿurf al-khāṣṣ): Specific custom; a custom limited to a particular locality or group.
المقارن (al-muqārin): Concurrent; the custom existing at the time of the utterance or contract.
المتأخر (al-muta'akhkhir): Subsequent; a custom that arises after the utterance or contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, which custom is considered when interpreting words in a legal document?
A) The custom at the time of interpretation
B) The concurrent custom at the time of the document
C) The most recent custom
D) The specific custom of the parties

Key Takeaway
Custom is not considered in suspended conditions, lawsuits, and confessions; general custom prevails over specific custom, and concurrent custom governs interpretation.

beginner
12 min
The Eleven Universal Qawaid of Ibn Nujaym
Introductory listing of eleven universal qawaid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the eleven universal qawaid listed by Ibn Nujaym and state the core theme and typical applications of each.

Core Idea
Ibn Nujaym's eleven qawaid kulliya are the master keys to deriving countless fiqh rulings.

This passage introduces the second type of qawaid in Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, listing eleven major universal principles. Each qawida is a tool for the mufti to derive rulings across many chapters. In this lesson, we will survey each qawida and its scope, preparing you to apply them in fatwa and istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose of the qawaid
Recognize that these qawaid are not the rulings themselves but the underlying principles from which numerous subsidiary rulings (furu') are derived. They serve as a mufti's mental toolkit.
Ibn Nujaym explicitly says: 'from which are derived countless particular cases.'
2
List the eleven qawaid in order
Memorize the sequence: 1) Ijtihad not overturned, 2) Halal and haram conflict, 3) Is self-sacrifice in worship disliked?, 4) The subordinate is subordinate, 5) Imam's disposition tied to public interest, 6) Hadd penalties repelled by doubts, 7) Free person not under control, 8) Same type combine, 9) Employ speech over neglect, 10) Profit with liability, 11) Question repeated in answer.
Each number corresponds to a major principle. Memorize the Arabic phrasing.
3
Identify key themes and applications per qawida
For each, note the brief description in the passage. For example, qawida 1 deals with judicial rulings; qawida 2 covers many chapters; qawida 4 includes four sub-rules; qawida 5 relates to public interest; etc.
Refer to the commentary for a concise summary of each.
4
Recognize exceptions and sub-rules
Several qawaid have explicit exceptions (e.g., qawida 1: four cases; qawida 2: ten excluded issues; qawida 6: five exceptions). Others, like qawida 4, contain sub-qawaid. Note these for deeper study.
The passage often mentions 'what is excluded' or 'what is excepted'.
5
Reflect on how these qawaid serve as a mufti's toolkit
Consider that a skilled mufti can, when faced with a novel case, recall the relevant qawida and derive the ruling. For example, the rule 'al-ijtihad la yunqad' guides judges; 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' governs financial liability.
This is the essence of qawida-based fatwa.

حنفيا، هل يكون له أو لا؟ وبيان ما إذا شرط النظر للقاضي هل يكون لقاضي بلده أو الموقوف عليه؟ وفيه بيان أن المعتبر العُرْف العام لا الخاص. وهذا آخر القواعد الكلية .
النوع الثاني من القواعد
في قواعد كلية يتخرّج عليها ما لا ينحصر من الصور الجزئية القاعدة الأولى : الاجتهاد لا ينقض بمثله، وفيها بيان أن القاضي إذا ردّ شهادة فليس لغيره قبولها إلا في أربعة وأنه لو حكم بشيء ثم تغيَّر اجتهاده، وبيان ما خرج منها وبيان ما استثناه أصحابنا من قولهم وإذا رفع إليه حكم حاكم أمضاه، وبيان قولهم وحكم بموجبه وبيان قول الموثقين مستوفيا شرائطه الشرعية وحكاية شمس الأئمة الحلواني مع قاضي عنبسة وبيان عدم الفرق بين الحكم بالصحة والحكم بالموجب، وبيان ما إذا حكم بقول ضعيف في مذهبه أو برواية مرجوع عنها أو خالف مذهبه عامدًا أو ناسيا، وبيان أن القضاء على خلاف شرط الواقف كالقضاء بخلاف النص، وبيان أن فعل القاضي وأمره إنما ينفذ إذا وافق الشرع وإلا رُدَّ.
القاعدة الثانية : إذا اجتمع الحلال والحرام غلب الحرام على الحلال وبيان ما تفرع عليها ممَّن اشتبه محرمه بأجنبيات، وما إذ كان أحد أبويه مأكولاً والآخر غير مأكول، وما إذا شارك الكلب المعلم غيره أو كلب المسلم كلب المجوسي، وما إذا وضع المجوسي يده على يد المسلم الذابح، وما إذا عجز المسلم عن مد قوسه فأعانه مجوسي، ووطء الجارية المشركة وما إذا كان بعض الشجرة أو الصيد في الحلّ وبعضها في الحرم وما إذا اختلطت المذكاة بالميتة وما إذا اختلط ودك الميتة بالزيت، وما إذا اختلطت زوجته بغيرها وفيه بيان ما إذا أسلم وتحته خمس، وما إذا صيدا فوقع في ماء أو سطح ثم على الأرض، وبيان ما خرج عنها من المسائل العشر، وفي آخرها تتمة فيما إذا جمع بين حلال وحرام في عقد أو نية، وبيان دخوله في أبواب النكاح والمهر والبيع والإجارة والكفالة والإبراء والهبة والهدية والوصية والإقرار والشهادة والقضاء والعبادات والطلاق والعتاق وعارية الرهن والوقف. وفي آخرها تنبيه على ما إذا اجتمع في العبادة جانب الحضر والسفر، ثم فصل في قاعدة إذا تعارض المانع والمقتضي فإنه يقدم المانع على المقتضي إلا في مسائل.
رمی
القاعدة الثالثة : هل يُكره الإيثار بالقرب.
القاعدة الرابعة : التابع تابع، ويدخل فيها قواعد الأولى: أنه لا يفرد بحكم، وفيها بيان حمل الجارية والشرب والطريق، وخرج عنها مسائل. الثانية : التابع يسقط بسقوط المتبوع، ويقرب منها قولهم : يسقط الفرع بسقوط أصله. الثالثة : التابع لا يتقدم على المتبوع . الرابعة : يغتفر في التابع ما لا يغتفر في غيره، وفيها بيان ما يغتفر ضمنا لا قصدًا.
القاعدة الخامسة : تصرّف الإمام على الرعيَّة منوط بالمصلحة، وفيها بيان أن أمره إنما ينفذ إذا وافق الشرع، وفي آخرها تنبيه على تصرّف القاضي في أموال اليتامى والأوقاف، وفيه بيان إحداث الوظائف بغير شرط الواقف وتقريره في المرتبات في الأوقاف.
في القاعدة السادسة : الحدود تُدرَأ بالشُّبهات، وفيها بيان أن القصاص كالحدود إلا خمس مسائل وبیان مخالفة التعزير لهما .
القاعدة السابعة : الحرّ لا يدخل تحت اليد وفيها بيان ما خرج عنها. القاعدة الثامنة : إذا اجتمع أمران من جنس واحد ولم يختلف مقصودهما دخل أحدهما في الآخر غالبًا، وبيان ما يتفرع عليها من اجتماع الحدين وما يوجب الجزاء على المحرَّم وبيان ما يجزي عن تحية المسجد وركعتي الطواف وتلاوة آية السجدة، وبيان تعدّد السهو في الصلاة والفرق بين جابر الصلاة وجابر الحج وما إذا زنى مرارًا أو شرب مرارًا أو قذف مرارًا أو جماعة، وما إذا وطىء في رمضان مرارا، وتعدّد جناية المحرم والوطء بالشبهة، وما إذا زنى بأمة فقتلها أو حرَّة كذلك، وما إذا تعددت الجناية على واحد وما إذا وطئت المعتدة بشبهة .
القاعدة التاسعة : إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله متى أمكن وإلا أهمل، وفيها بيان الحقيقة إذا تعذرت أو هجرت شرعًا أو عُرفًا، وما إذا تعذرت الحقيقة والمجاز، وفيها بيان ما إذا جمع بين امرأته وغيرها في الطلاق وفيها بعض مسائل الوقف والقول بنقض القسمة وما ذكره السبكي والخصاف وفيها تنبيه : التأسيس خير من التأكيد، وبيان ما تفرع عليه من أنه لو كرر الطلاق أو اليمين بالله تعالى منجزا أو معلقا .
القاعدة العاشرة : الخراج بالضمان وبيان معناه وما دخل فيها وما خرج عنها.
القاعدة الحادية عشرة : السؤال مُعاد في الجواب وبيان كلمة نعم وبلى.

Hanafi, does he have it or not? And an explanation of if he stipulates the supervision for the judge, is it for the judge of his town or the one in charge of the endowment? And in it an explanation that the general custom is considered, not the specific. And this is the conclusion of the universal qawaid. The second type of qawaid: concerning universal qawaid from which are derived countless particular cases. The first qawida: Ijtihad is not overturned by its like. And in it an explanation that if a judge rejects a testimony, it is not permissible for another judge to accept it except in four cases; and that if he judges something then his ijtihad changes; and an explanation of what is excluded from it; and an explanation of what our companions excluded from their statement, 'And if a judge's ruling is brought to him, he enacts it'; and an explanation of their statement, 'And he judges according to its import'; and an explanation of the statement of notaries fulfilling its legal conditions; and the story of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani with the judge of 'Anbasa; and an explanation that there is no difference between a judgment of validity and a judgment according to its import; and an explanation of if he judges according to a weak opinion in his madhhab, or a superseded narration, or he deliberately or forgetfully contravenes his madhhab; and an explanation that judging contrary to the condition of the waqif is like judging contrary to the text; and an explanation that the judge's action and order are only effective if they agree with the Shari'a, otherwise they are rejected. The second qawida: If the permissible and the prohibited come together, the prohibited prevails over the permissible. And an explanation of what branches from it: regarding one whose prohibited [women?] are confused with non-related women; and regarding an animal whose one parent is edible and the other not; and if a trained dog is joined by another dog, or a Muslim's dog [joins] a Magian's dog; and if a Magian places his hand over the hand of a Muslim slaughterer; and if the Muslim is unable to draw his bow so a Magian helps him; and intercourse with a polytheist slave-girl; and if part of a tree or game is in the halal area and part in the haram; and if a slaughtered animal is mixed with a carrion; and if the fat of a carrion is mixed with oil; and if his wife is mixed with other women, and in it an explanation of if he becomes a Muslim and has five wives; and if a hunted animal falls into water or a roof then onto the ground; and an explanation of what is excluded from it among ten issues; and at its end a supplement regarding when one combines halal and haram in a contract or intention; and an explanation of its entry into the chapters of marriage, dower, sale, hire, guarantee, release, gift, present, bequest, acknowledgment, testimony, judgment, acts of worship, divorce, manumission, loan of pawn, and endowment. And at its end a notice regarding when the aspect of residence and travel combine in an act of worship; then a section on the qawida: When the preventer and the requirement conflict, the preventer is given precedence over the requirement except in certain issues. [رَمَى?]. The third qawida: Is self-sacrifice with acts of worship disliked? The fourth qawida: The subordinate is a subordinate, and it includes sub-qawaid: The first: it is not given an independent ruling; and in it an explanation of carrying a slave-girl, drinking, and the road, and issues are excluded from it. The second: The subordinate drops with the dropping of the principal; and close to it is their statement: The branch drops with the dropping of its root. The third: The subordinate does not precede the principal. The fourth: What is not tolerated in other than the subordinate is tolerated in the subordinate; and in it an explanation of what is tolerated implicitly but not intentionally. The fifth qawida: The Imam's disposition over his subjects is tied to public interest; and in it an explanation that his order is only effective if it agrees with the Shari'a; and at its end a notice regarding the judge's disposition over the wealth of orphans and endowments; and in it an explanation of creating positions without the condition of the waqif and fixing salaries in endowments. The sixth qawida: Hadd penalties are repelled by doubts; and in it an explanation that retaliation is like hudud except five issues; and an explanation of the difference of ta'zir from both. The seventh qawida: A free person does not come under control; and in it an explanation of what is excluded from it. The eighth qawida: If two matters of the same type come together and their objectives do not differ, one enters into the other generally; and an explanation of what branches from it regarding the combination of two hadd penalties and what necessitates a penalty for the prohibited; and an explanation of what suffices for greeting the mosque, two rak'ahs of tawaf, and reciting an ayah of sajda; and an explanation of the multiplicity of forgetfulness in prayer and the difference between what compensates prayer and what compensates hajj; and if one commits zina repeatedly, or drinks repeatedly, or slanders repeatedly, or a group; and if one commits intercourse repeatedly in Ramadan; and multiplicity of the prohibited person's crime and intercourse with a shubha; and if one commits zina with a slave-girl and kills her, or similarly a free woman; and if multiple crimes occur against one person; and if the waiting woman is subject to intercourse with a shubha. The ninth qawida: Employing speech is better than neglecting it whenever possible, otherwise it is neglected; and in it an explanation of the literal meaning if it is impossible or abandoned legally or customarily; and if both literal and metaphorical are impossible; and in it an explanation of if one includes his wife and others in divorce; and in it some issues of endowment and the statement regarding annulling division, and what al-Subki and al-Khassaf mentioned; and in it a notice: Establishment is better than repetition; and an explanation of what branches from it: that if he repeats divorce or an oath by Allah, unconditional or conditional. The tenth qawida: Profit is with liability; and an explanation of its meaning and what enters into it and what exits from it. The eleventh qawida: The question is repeated in the answer; and an explanation of the words 'naam' and 'bala'. 9.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the second type of universal qawaid (القواعد الكلية) in Ibn Nujaym's Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir. It lists eleven qawaid, each with a summary of the issues that will be discussed under them. The author begins by finishing a discussion from the previous section, then states 'This is the conclusion of the universal qawaid. The second type of qawaid: concerning universal qawaid from which are derived countless particular cases.' Then he enumerates each qawida in order. The first qawida is 'Ijtihad is not overturned by its like,' covering matters of judicial rulings and their modification. The second is 'If the permissible and the prohibited come together, the prohibited prevails,' with many applications across various fiqh chapters. The third is a question about self-sacrifice in acts of worship. The fourth is 'The subordinate is a subordinate,' with four sub-qawaid. The fifth is about the Imam's discretion tied to public interest. The sixth is 'Hadd penalties are repelled by doubts,' with an exception for five issues. The seventh is 'A free person does not come under control.' The eighth deals with combining two matters of the same type. The ninth is about employing speech over neglecting it. The tenth is 'Profit is with liability.' The eleventh is 'The question is repeated in the answer.' This listing serves as a roadmap for the detailed explanations later in the book, and each qawida is a major principle of Hanafi fiqh that governs numerous subsidiary rulings.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية (al-qawā‘id al-kulliyya): Universal legal maxims that encompass many subsidiary rulings.
الاجتهاد لا ينقض بمثله (al-ijtihād lā yunqaḍ bi-mithlihī): Ijtihad is not overturned by another ijtihad; a judge's ruling based on his ijtihad cannot be overturned by another judge's different ijtihad, except in limited cases.
إذا اجتمع الحلال والحرام غلب الحرام على الحلال (idhā ij‘tama al-ḥalāl wa al-ḥarām ghalaba al-ḥarām ‘alā al-ḥalāl): When permissible and prohibited combine, the prohibited prevails over the permissible.
التابع تابع (al-tābi‘ tābi‘): The subordinate follows the ruling of its principal; includes four sub-qawaid: does not get independent ruling, drops with principal, does not precede, and tolerances are granted.
الحدود تُدرَأ بالشُّبهات (al-ḥudūd tudra’u bi al-shubahāt): Hadd penalties are repelled by doubts; when there is any doubt, the penalty is not applied.
الخراج بالضمان (al-kharāj bi al-ḍamān): The profit (or yield) is with the liability (i.e., whoever bears the risk of loss is entitled to the returns).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If halal and haram combine, haram prevails
B) Ijtihad is not overturned by its like
C) The subordinate is a subordinate
D) Hadd penalties are repelled by doubts

Key Takeaway
The eleven qawaid introduced here are the fundamental principles from which countless fiqh rulings are derived, forming the backbone of Hanafi legal methodology.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
beginner
8 min
The First Major Maxim: No Reward Without Intention
Definition of the first major maxim with its proof and classifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim and identify its two types of effect, and apply it to distinguish between intended and unintended acts.

Core Idea
Intention is the key to reward, but not always to validity in Hanafi fiqh.

This lesson introduces the first of the five major legal maxims in the Hanafi school. The maxim governs all acts of worship and even some worldly actions, clarifying that reward in the afterlife is contingent upon intention. However, the school also distinguishes between intention for reward and intention for validity.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim and its Arabic
The maxim is 'لا ثواب إلا بالنية' (No reward except with intention). Write it down and repeat it until it is committed to memory. This is the foundational axiom.
The Arabic term 'ثواب' means divine reward, and 'نية' means intention.
2
Identify the two types of effect
The hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' has two possible effects: otherworldly (reward and punishment) and worldly (validity/invalidity). The scholars agree by consensus that the otherworldly effect is intended. Understand why the worldly effect is not directly derived from the hadith.
The worldly effect (validity) may be governed by other proofs, not by this maxim alone.
3
Understand the interpretation as implicit expression
The hadith is interpreted as an implicit expression (مقتضى) requiring an estimated word, because actions often occur without intention. The estimated phrase is 'حكم الأعمال' (the ruling of actions). This explains why the hadith can be true despite many actions being done without intention.
This is a linguistic principle: without the estimated word, the hadith would be false.
4
Recognize the evidence for intention in worship
The requirement of intention in all acts of worship is established by consensus (إجماع) and by the Quranic verse 'وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا الله مخلصين له الدين حنفاء'. Note that the author prefers the consensus argument because the word 'عبادة' in the verse may mean tawhid, not specific acts.
This shows the hierarchy of evidence: consensus is stronger in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول : القواعد الكلية
النوع الأول من هذه القواعد القاعدة الأولى : لا ثواب إلا بالنية
صرح به المشايخ في مواضع في الفقه أولها في الوضوء، سواء قلنا إنها
شرط الصحة كما في الصلاة والزكاة والصوم والحج أو لا، كما في الوضوء والغسل، وعلى هذا قرروا حديث إنما الأعمال بالنيات (۱) أنه من باب المقتضى، إذ لا يصح بدون تقدير لكثرة وجود الأعمال بدونها فقدروا مضافًا أي حكم الأعمال. وهو نوعان: أُخروي؛ هو الثواب واستحقاق العقاب. ودنيوي؛ وهو الصحة والفساد. وقد أُريد الأخروي بالإجماع، للإجماع على أنه لا ثواب ولا عقاب إلا بالنية، فانتفى الآخر أن يكون مرادًا، إما لأنه مشترك ولا عموم له، وإما لاندفاع الضرورة به من صحة الكلام ،به فلا حاجة إلى الآخر. والثاني أوجه، لأن الأول لا يسلمه الخصم لأنه قائل بعموم المشترك، فحينئذ لا يدل على اشتراطها في الوسائل للصحة، ولا على المقاصد أيضًا. وفي بعض الكتب أن الوضوء الذي ليس بمنوي، ليس بمأمور به ولكنه مفتاح للصلاة.
إنما شرطت في العبادات بالإجماع، أو بآية وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا الله مُخْلِصين له الدينَ حُنَفَاء [البيّنة : ٥] والأول أوجه لأن العبادة فيها بمعنى التوحيد بقرينة
(۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب بدء الوحي باب ۱ ومسلم في كتاب الإمارة حديث ١٥٥، وأبو داود في كتاب الطلاق باب ۱۱ وابن ماجه في كتاب الزهد باب ٢٦
The First Art: The General Legal Maxims.
The First Type of these Maxims: The First Maxim: No Reward except with Intention.
The scholars have stated this in various places in fiqh, the first of which is in wudu, whether we say that intention is a condition of validity as in prayer, zakat, fasting, and Hajj, or not, as in wudu and ghusl. And based on this, they explained the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' as being of the category of iqtida' (implicative expression), since it is not valid without appreciation due to the abundance of actions without it. So they appreciated an omitted word, i.e., 'the ruling of actions'. And it is of two types: otherworldly, which is reward and deserving punishment; and worldly, which is validity and invalidity. The otherworldly was intended by consensus, because of the consensus that there is no reward or punishment except with intention. Thus the other [worldly] is ruled out from being intended, either because the term is ambiguous and has no general meaning, or because the necessity of the speech is satisfied with it, so there is no need for the other. The second is more appropriate, because the opponent does not concede the first since he says the ambiguous term has general applicability, so then it does not indicate its conditionality in means for validity, nor on ends. And in some books, the wudu that is not intended is not commanded but is a key to prayer.
It is only stipulated in acts of worship by consensus, or by the verse 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, being sincere to Him in religion, inclining to the truth' (Al-Bayyinah: 5). And the first is more appropriate because 'worship' in it means tawhid by context.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage introduces the first major legal maxim of the Hanafi school: 'لا ثواب إلا بالنية' (No reward except with intention). The author explains that this maxim is applied in various places of fiqh, starting with wudu. He discusses the debate about whether intention is a condition for validity or not, noting that in some acts like wudu and ghusl, intention is not a condition for validity but still for reward. The hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' is interpreted as an implicit expression (مقتضى) requiring an estimated word, because actions often occur without intention. The hadith's effect is twofold: otherworldly (reward and punishment) and worldly (validity/invalidity). The scholars agree that the otherworldly aspect is intended. The author then discusses two explanations for why the worldly aspect is not intended, preferring the second: that meaning is satisfied without it. He also notes a view that unintended wudu is not a commanded act but serves as a key to prayer. Finally, he states that intention is required in all acts of worship by consensus or by the Quranic verse, preferring the consensus argument because the verse's word 'worship' may refer to tawhid, not specific acts.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): The intention in the heart to perform an act for the sake of Allah, or legally to distinguish acts of worship from habits.
ثواب (thawab): Divine reward in the afterlife for an action done with pure intention.
مقتضى (muqtada): An implicit expression that requires an omitted word to complete the meaning; here, the hadith requires an estimated 'حكم الأعمال' to be true.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' interpreted as an implicit expression (مقتضى)?
A) Because actions often occur without intention
B) Because the hadith is weak
C) Because the scholars disagreed
D) Because the hadith uses a general term

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'لا ثواب إلا بالنية' establishes that intention is essential for divine reward, but validity of acts depends on separate proofs.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The General Rule: Intention is a Condition for All Acts of Worship Except Islam
General rule on intention in ibadat with the exception of Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim that intention is a condition for ibadat, identify the exception for Islam, and apply the maxim to distinguish acts requiring intention.

Core Idea
Intention is required for all worship except embracing Islam — Islam is valid even under coercion.

This lesson explores a foundational Hanafi legal maxim: that intention (niyyah) is a prerequisite for the validity of all acts of worship, with one notable exception — the act of embracing Islam. The passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair establishes this principle and provides the evidence from the case of coerced conversion. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti when assessing the validity of various devotional acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
State the text of the maxim: 'النية شرط صحة في جميع العبادات إلا الإسلام'.
2
Locate the exception
Islam is valid without intention; the proof is the acceptance of coerced Islam.
3
Contrast with disbelief
Disbelief requires intention; coerced disbelief is not valid.
4
Apply the maxim
Distinguish between acts requiring intention (all ibadat) and the exception (Islam).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في العبادات كلها فهي شرط صحتها إلا الإسلام، فإنه يصح بدونها بدليل قولهم : إن إسلام المكره صحيح ولا يكون مسلما بمجرد نية الإسلام بخلاف الكفر، كما سنبينه في بحث التروك، وأما الكفر فيشترط له النيَّة لقولهم: إن كفر المكره غير وأما قولهم : إنه إذا تكلم بكلمة الكفر هازلاً يكفر. إنما هو باعتبار أن کفر، كما علم في الأصول من بحث الهزل فلا تصح صلاة مطلقا، ولو صلاة جنازة، إلا بها فرضًا أو واجبًا أو سُنَّة أو نفلاً. وإذا نوى قطعها لا يخرج عنها إلا بمناف ولو نوى الانتقال عنها إلى غيرها، فإن كانت الثانية غير الأولى وشرع بالتكبير، صار منتقلاً وإلا فلا ولا يصح اقتداء بإمام إلا بنية. وتصح الإمامة بدون نيتها خلافًا للكرخي وأبي حفص الكبير، كما في البناية إلا إذا صلى خلفه نساء، فإن اقتداءهن به بلا نيَّة الإمام للإمامة غير صحيح واستثنى بعضهم الجمعة والعيدين، وهو الصحيح كما في الخلاصة، ولو حلف ألا يؤم أحدًا فاقتدى به إنسان صح الاقتداء. وهل يحنث ؟ قال في الخانية : يحنث قضاء لا ديانة إلا إن أشهد قبل الشروع فلا يحنث قضاء وكذا لو أمّ الناس هذا الحالف في صلاة الجمعة صحت وحنث قضاء، ولا يحنث أصلاً إذا أمَّهم في صلاة الجنازة وسجدة التلاوة. ولو حلف ألاً يؤم فلانا فأمَّ الناس ناويًا ألا يؤمه ويؤم غيره فاقتدى به فلان حنث وإن لم يعلم به (انتهى). ولكن لا ثواب له على الإمامة وسجود التلاوة كالصلاة. وكذا سجدة الشكر على قول من يراها مشروعة والمعتمد أن الخلاف في نيتها لا في الجواز وكذا سجود السهو، ولا تضره نية عدمه وقت السلام.
And as for all acts of worship, it (intention) is a condition for their validity except Islam, for it is valid without it as evidence of their saying: the Islam of the coerced person is valid, and one does not become Muslim merely by the intention of Islam, unlike disbelief. As for disbelief, intention is required for it by their saying: the disbelief of the coerced is not disbelief. And their saying: if one speaks a word of disbelief jokingly, he disbelieves, that is only because it is disbelief, as known in usul from the topic of jest. So no prayer is absolutely valid, even funeral prayer, except with it, whether fard, wajib, sunna, or nafl. If one intends to cut it (prayer), he does not exit except with a contradictory act; if he intends to transfer to another prayer, if the second is different from the first and he starts with takbir, he transfers, otherwise not. And following an imam is not valid except with intention. And the imamate is valid without its intention, contrary to al-Karkhi and Abu Hafs al-Kabir, as in al-Binaya, unless women pray behind him, for their following without the imam's intention for imamate is not valid. Some made an exception for Friday and the two Eids, which is the correct view as in al-Khulasa. If one swore not to lead anyone, and a person followed him, the following is valid. Does he break the oath? It is said in al-Khaniyya: he breaks the oath judicially but not religiously, unless he gave testimony before starting, then he does not break judicially. Likewise, if this oath-taker led people in Friday prayer, it is valid and he breaks judicially; he does not break at all if he led them in funeral prayer and prostration of recitation. If he swore not to lead a certain person, and he led the people intending not to lead him and lead others, and that person followed him, he breaks the oath even if he did not know (end). But there is no reward for him for the imamate and prostration of recitation like the prayer. Likewise, the prostration of thanks according to the one who deems it prescribed, and the relied-upon is that the difference is in its intention, not its permissibility. Likewise, the prostration of forgetfulness, and the intention of its absence at the time of salutation does not harm him.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage establishes the general rule that intention is a condition for the validity of all acts of worship except for embracing Islam. The proof is that coerced Islam is valid despite lacking intention, whereas disbelief requires intention. The passage then applies this rule to prayer, stating that no prayer (fard, wajib, sunna, or nafl) is valid without intention. It discusses cutting or transferring prayer: if one intends to cut prayer, he only exits by an act that contradicts it; if he intends to transfer to another prayer, it only works if the second is different from the first and he performs takbir. Following an imam requires intention on the follower's part, but imamate itself is valid without the imam's intention according to the correct view, though al-Karkhi and Abu Hafs al-Kabir disagreed. Exceptions are made for Friday and Eid prayers in certain contexts. The passage then details oaths related to leading prayer, distinguishing between judicial and religious consequences. It also notes that though the imamate may be valid without intention, there is no reward. The rule extends to prostrations: the difference in prostration of thanks is about intention, not permissibility, and intention not to perform prostration of forgetfulness at the salutation does not harm.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention of the heart, a condition for validity of worship.
مكره (mukrah): A person coerced under threat.
هزل (hazl): Jest or joking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is evidence that intention is not required for Islam?
A) The validity of coerced Islam
B) The hadith on actions by intentions
C) The requirement of intention for prayer
D) The invalidity of coerced disbelief

Key Takeaway
Intention is a condition for all acts of worship except embracing Islam, which is valid without intention.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Intention in Salat: Prayer Validity, Imamate, and Oaths
Detailed rulings on intention in salat, imamate, and oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of intention to specific prayer scenarios: validity of prayer, following an imam, imamate without intention, and oaths related to leading prayer.

Core Idea
Every prayer requires intention; imamate does not require the imam's intention, but the follower's intention is mandatory.

This lesson applies the general rule of intention to the specific context of prayer. The passage details that no prayer is valid without intention, whether fard, wajib, sunna, or nafl. It then addresses the nuanced rulings on following an imam, the validity of imamate without the imam's intention, and the legal consequences of oaths concerning leading prayer. Understanding these details is crucial for a mufti when advising on the validity of congregational prayers and resolving disputes about oaths.

Guided Path
1
Confirm universal requirement of intention for all prayers
Note that intention is required for fard, wajib, sunna, nafl, and funeral prayer.
2
Examine cutting and transferring prayer
Intending to cut requires a contradictory act; transferring works only if the second prayer is different and takbir is said.
3
Analyze following an imam
The follower must intend to follow; the imam's intention is not required for imamate (correct view).
Dispute with al-Karkhi and Abu Hafs al-Kabir.
4
Apply rulings on oaths about leading prayer
Distinguish between judicial and religious consequences of breaking the oath.
Examples with Friday, funeral, and specific person.
5
Extend to prostrations
Prostration of thanks: difference is about intention, not permissibility; prostration of forgetfulness does not require intention at salam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في العبادات كلها فهي شرط صحتها إلا الإسلام، فإنه يصح بدونها بدليل قولهم : إن إسلام المكره صحيح ولا يكون مسلما بمجرد نية الإسلام بخلاف الكفر، كما سنبينه في بحث التروك، وأما الكفر فيشترط له النيَّة لقولهم: إن كفر المكره غير وأما قولهم : إنه إذا تكلم بكلمة الكفر هازلاً يكفر. إنما هو باعتبار أن کفر، كما علم في الأصول من بحث الهزل فلا تصح صلاة مطلقا، ولو صلاة جنازة، إلا بها فرضًا أو واجبًا أو سُنَّة أو نفلاً. وإذا نوى قطعها لا يخرج عنها إلا بمناف ولو نوى الانتقال عنها إلى غيرها، فإن كانت الثانية غير الأولى وشرع بالتكبير، صار منتقلاً وإلا فلا ولا يصح اقتداء بإمام إلا بنية. وتصح الإمامة بدون نيتها خلافًا للكرخي وأبي حفص الكبير، كما في البناية إلا إذا صلى خلفه نساء، فإن اقتداءهن به بلا نيَّة الإمام للإمامة غير صحيح واستثنى بعضهم الجمعة والعيدين، وهو الصحيح كما في الخلاصة، ولو حلف ألا يؤم أحدًا فاقتدى به إنسان صح الاقتداء. وهل يحنث ؟ قال في الخانية : يحنث قضاء لا ديانة إلا إن أشهد قبل الشروع فلا يحنث قضاء وكذا لو أمّ الناس هذا الحالف في صلاة الجمعة صحت وحنث قضاء، ولا يحنث أصلاً إذا أمَّهم في صلاة الجنازة وسجدة التلاوة. ولو حلف ألاً يؤم فلانا فأمَّ الناس ناويًا ألا يؤمه ويؤم غيره فاقتدى به فلان حنث وإن لم يعلم به (انتهى). ولكن لا ثواب له على الإمامة وسجود التلاوة كالصلاة. وكذا سجدة الشكر على قول من يراها مشروعة والمعتمد أن الخلاف في نيتها لا في الجواز وكذا سجود السهو، ولا تضره نية عدمه وقت السلام.
And as for all acts of worship, it (intention) is a condition for their validity except Islam, for it is valid without it as evidence of their saying: the Islam of the coerced person is valid, and one does not become Muslim merely by the intention of Islam, unlike disbelief. As for disbelief, intention is required for it by their saying: the disbelief of the coerced is not disbelief. And their saying: if one speaks a word of disbelief jokingly, he disbelieves, that is only because it is disbelief, as known in usul from the topic of jest. Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention of the heart, a condition for validity of worship.
اقتداء (iqtida'): Following an imam in congregational prayer.
إمامة (imamah): The role of leading prayer.
يمين (yamin): Oath.
حنث (hanath): Breaking an oath.
قضاء/ديانة (qada'an/diyanatan): Judicially versus religiously; legal vs. moral consequence.

According to the text, if an imam leads prayer without intending to lead, and women are present, what is the ruling on the women's prayer?

B) Invalid

Key Takeaway
Imamate does not require the imam's intention (correct view), but the follower's intention is essential; oaths about leading prayer have distinct judicial and religious consequences.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
