Oath Interpretation: Intention of the Oppressed vs. Oppressor
Qawida defining oath interpretation based on justice context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'al-yamin 'ala niyyat al-halif' to determine whose intention governs an oath when oppression is involved.

Core Idea
The oppressed swearer's intention governs; the oppressor's oath is interpreted by the demander's intent.

This qawida, cited from al-Khulasa, provides a crucial nuance to the general rule that oaths are interpreted by the swearer's intention. When injustice is present, the ruling shifts to protect the oppressed and hold the oppressor accountable. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti when faced with oaths made under coercion.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim in Arabic and English
Write the Arabic text 'اليمين على نيَّة الحالف إن كان مظلومًا، وعلى نيَّة المستحلف إن كان ظالمًا' and its English translation from this lesson.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish when the swearer is oppressed (mazlum) vs. when the swearer is an oppressor (zalim).
The term 'halif' means the one taking the oath; 'mustah'rif' means the one demanding the oath.
3
Determine the legal effect in each case
For mazlum swearer: oath on his own intention. For zalim swearer: oath on intention of the demander (mustah'rif).
This reverses the usual rule when the swearer is unjust.
4
Connect to the general qawida
The general maxim 'al-yamin 'ala niyyat al-halif' holds, but this qawida specifies conditions that override it.
This is a restriction (dabt) on the general rule.
5
Note the source authority
Al-Khulasa is a later Hanafi fatwa compilation; this rule is operative in the school.
It is mufta bihi in cases of oppression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة فيها أيضًا : كما في الخلاصة. اليمين على نيَّة الحالف إن كان مظلومًا، وعلى نيَّة المستحلف إن كان ظالمًا
A base in it as well, as in al-Khulasa: The oath is on the intention of the swearer if he is oppressed, and on the intention of the one who administers the oath (the demander) if he is an oppressor.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This qawida provides a rule for interpreting oaths in situations of injustice. If a person is oppressed (mazlum) and is forced to take an oath, the oath is interpreted according to his own intention, because the oppressor has no right to impose a specific meaning. Conversely, if a person is oppressive (zalim) and he administers an oath to someone, the oath is interpreted according to the intention of the one administering it (i.e., the one making the demand), because the oppressor's own intention is what matters, not that of the coerced oath-taker. This is a rule found in al-Khulasa, a Hanafi fatwa collection. It protects the oppressed and holds the oppressor accountable to his own intent.

Key Terms
مظلوم (mazlum): One who is oppressed or forced into an oath unjustly
ظالم (zalim): One who is oppressive or forces another to take an oath unjustly
مستحلف (mustah'rif): The one who demands an oath from another, often the claimant
قاعدة (qa'ida): A legal maxim or principle

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An oppressive ruler demands an oath from a victim to prove ownership. The victim swears under duress. According to this qawida, whose intention governs the interpretation of the oath?
A) The ruler's intention
B) The victim's intention
C) The court's interpretation
D) The literal meaning of the words

Key Takeaway
In oath rulings the mufti must determine whether the swearer is oppressed or oppressor—the intention that controls shifts accordingly.

The Third Major Maxim: Certainty is Not Removed by Doubt
Stating the third major maxim and its primary hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt', identify its primary hadith evidence, and apply it to a case of doubt in ritual purity.

Core Idea
Certainty is not removed by doubt — a known certainty persists unless overcome by another certainty.

This lesson introduces the third of the five major maxims (al-qawaid al-kubra) of Hanafi fiqh: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' (اليقين لا يزول بالشك). This maxim is foundational across all chapters of fiqh, especially in matters of purity and prayer. Its primary evidence is a hadith narrated by Abu Hurairah in Sahih Muslim, which instructs a person experiencing doubt during prayer not to leave the mosque until certain of impurity. Understanding this maxim is essential for the mufti when resolving cases where doubt arises in acts of worship or transactions.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim in Arabic and English
Learn the exact phrasing: اليقين لا يزول بالشك. This is a universal principle that applies to all areas of fiqh.
Also learn its translation: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt.'
2
Identify the primary textual evidence
The author cites the hadith from Sahih Muslim narrated by Abu Hurairah: 'If one of you finds something in his stomach and it becomes doubtful to him whether something came out or not, let him not leave the mosque until he hears a sound or smells an odor.' The full hadith establishes that certainty of purity is not overturned by mere doubt.
This hadith is a dalil naqli for the maxim.
3
Understand the legal principle
The maxim means that an established state (like purity) remains in effect until there is certain evidence that it has changed. Doubt, being weaker than certainty, cannot remove it. Only another certainty can override it.
This principle is derived from the hadith's implication that doubt does not necessitate action.
4
Apply the maxim to a practical case
Consider a person who is certain of having performed wudu but later doubts whether they passed wind. According to the maxim, they are still considered pure until they are certain (by hearing or smelling) that wudu was broken.
This is a common scenario in fiqh of salah and taharah.
5
Relate to the mufti's role
A mufti must apply this maxim when issuing fatwas. For example, if someone asks about the validity of their prayer after experiencing doubt, the mufti rules that the prayer is valid because certainty of purity is not removed by doubt.
This maxim often overrides subjective feelings of uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثالثة : اليقين لا يزول بالشك
ودليلها ما رواه مسلم عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه مرفوعا: «إذا وجد أحدكم في بطنه شيئًا فأشكل عليه، أخرج منه شيء أم لا؟ فلا يخرجن من المسجد حتى
The third maxim: Certainty is not removed by doubt. Its evidence is what Muslim narrated from Abu Hurairah (may Allah be pleased with him) raised to the Prophet: 'If one of you finds something in his stomach and it becomes doubtful to him whether something came out or not, let him not leave the mosque until...'
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage introduces the third major maxim of Hanafi fiqh: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' (اليقين لا يزول بالشك). This is a foundational principle that applies across all chapters of fiqh. The author provides the textual evidence from Sahih Muslim: a hadith about doubt regarding passing wind during prayer. The principle means that if a person is certain of his purity, doubt does not affect that certainty. This maxim is one of the five major maxims (القواعد الكبرى) in Hanafi legal theory. The hadith, though cut off in the text, is well-known: the complete narration states that one should not leave the mosque until he hears a sound or smells an odor. This establishes that certainty of purity is not overturned by mere doubt.

Key Terms
اليقين (al-yaqīn): Certainty, the state of being sure about a matter without doubt
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt, a state of uncertainty between two possibilities

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the evidence for the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' as provided in this passage?
A) A Quranic verse from Surah Al-Imran
B) A hadith from Muslim narrated by Abu Hurairah
C) Consensus of the Sahaba
D) Logical reasoning (qiyas)

Key Takeaway
Certainty is not removed by doubt; the mufti must uphold established certainty until overcome by certain knowledge.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Third Maxim: Certainty is not Removed by Doubt – Application and Refinement
Presentation of the third maxim, its proof, application to impurity location, and critical refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the third major maxim and its hadith proof, IDENTIFY the initial application to a hidden impurity, and EXPLAIN why the correct interpretation distinguishes between the state of certainty and its legal ruling.

Core Idea
Certainty's ruling endures despite doubt; the maxim governs the legal effect, not the psychological state.

This lesson presents the third major maxim of Hanafi jurisprudence: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt.' We begin with its foundational proof from hadith, then explore a challenging application: a garment known to be impure but with an unknown spot. Some scholars argued that washing a part suffices due to doubt about the spot's location. However, this reasoning appears to contradict the maxim. The author resolves the problem by showing that the maxim really means the ruling (hukm) of certainty persists, not the certainty itself. This refinement is crucial for sound legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim and its hadith proof
State the maxim in Arabic and English, and recall the hadith about feeling something in the stomach from Sahih Muslim.
The hadith provides a clear precedent: certainty of wudu is not removed by doubt about passing wind.
2
Examine the problematic application
Consider the case of a garment known to be impure but the exact spot is hidden. Some earlier scholars said washing a part suffices because doubt about the location allows returning to original purity.
This application was recorded in Fath al-Qadir and analogized to a dhimmi case in al-Siyar al-Kabir.
3
Identify the reasoning and its flaw
The reasoning is that doubt about the spot creates doubt about the continued presence of impurity, so the principle 'certainty is not removed by doubt' is invoked to keep the garment impure. But the author finds this problematic: if doubt arises after washing, it is doubt about removal, which does not remove the prior certainty of impurity.
The flaw lies in applying the maxim to the state of certainty rather than its ruling.
4
Distinguish between certainty and its ruling
Understand the critical refinement: the maxim means the ruling (hukm) of certainty is not removed by doubt. Thus, the prior certainty that the garment is impure carries a ruling (impermissibility of prayer) that persists despite doubt about removal.
This interpretation resolves the contradiction: partial washing does not lift the ruling of impurity.
5
Apply the refined understanding
Apply to the garment case: since the ruling of impurity remains, the entire garment must be washed. Washing only a part does not make it pure, and prayer in it is invalid.
This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثالثة : اليقين لا يزول بالشك ودليلها ما رواه مسلم عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه مرفوعا: «إذا وجد أحدكم في بطنه شيئًا فأشكل عليه، أخرج منه شيء أم لا؟ فلا يخرجن من المسجد حتى يسمع صوتًا أو يجد ريحا» في فتح القدير، من باب الأنجاس ما يوضحها فنسوق عبارته بتمامها : قوله تطهير النجاسة واجب مقيد بالإمكان، وأما إذا لم يتمكن الإزالة لخفاء خصوص المحل المصاب مع العلم بتنجيس الثوب ؛ قيل الواجب غسل طرف منه فإن غسله بتحر أو بلا تحرّ طهر وذكر الوجه يبين لا أثر للتحري وهو أن يغسل بعضه. مع أن الأصل طهارة الثوب وقع الشك في قيام النجاسة لاحتمال كون المغسول محلها فلا يقضي بالنجاسة بالشك. كذا أورده الأسبيجابي في شرح الجامع الكبير قال : وسمعت الإمام تاج الدين أحمد بن عبد العزيز يقوله ويقيسه على مسألة في السير الكبير هي : إذا فتحنا حصنًا وفيهم ذمّي لا يعرف لا يجوز قتلهم لقيام المانع بيقين، فلو قتل البعض أو أخرج حلّ قتل الباقي للشك في قيام المحرّم، كذا هنا . وفي الخلاصة بعد ما ذكره مجرَّدًا عن التعليل : فلو صلى معه صلاة ثم ظهرت النجاسة في طرف آخر تجب إعادة ما صلّى. (انتهى). وفي الظهيرية ؛ ثوب فيه نجاسة لا يدري مكانها ؛ يغسل الثوب كله. (انتهى). وهو الاحتياط. وذلك التعليل مشكل عندي فإن غسل طرف يوجب الشك في طهر الثوب بعد اليقين بنجاسته قبل ؛ وحاصله أنه شك في الإزالة بعد تيقن قيام النجاسة. والشك لا يرفع المتيقن قبله والحق أن ثبوت الشك في كون الطرف المغسول والرجل المخرج هو مكان النجاسة والمعصوم الدم يوجب البتة الشك في طهر الباقي وإباحة دم الباقين ومن ضرورة صيرورته مشكوكا فيه ارتفاع اليقين عن تنجسه و معصوميته. وإذا صار مشكوكا في نجاسته جازت الصلاة معه، إلا أن هذا إن صح لم يبق لكلمتهم المجمع عليها أعني قولهم : اليقين لا يرتفع بالشك، معنى. فإنه حينئذ لا يتصوّر أن يثبت شك في محل ثبوت اليقين ليتصور ثبوت شك فيه لا يرتفع به ذلك اليقين. فمن هذا حقق بعض المحققين أن المراد لا يرتفع به حكم اليقين. وعلى هذا التقدير يخلص الإشكال في الحكم لا الدليل. فنقول : وإن ثبت الشك في طهارة الباقي ونجاسته، لكن لا يرتفع حكم ذلك اليقين السابق بنجاسته وهو عدم جواز الصلاة فلا تصح بعد غسل الطرف لأن الشك الطارىء لا يرفع حكم اليقين السابق على ما حقَّق من أنه هو المراد من قولهم: اليقين لا يرتفع
The Third Base: Certainty is not removed by doubt. Its evidence is what Muslim narrated from Abu Huraira, may Allah be pleased with him, from the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'If one of you finds something in his stomach and it becomes unclear to him whether anything came out or not, let him not leave the mosque until he hears a sound or finds a smell.' In Fath al-Qadir, in the chapter on impurities, what clarifies it is mentioned. So we quote his full statement: 'His saying: Removing impurity is obligatory but limited to possibility. When removal is not possible due to the hiddenness of the particular affected spot, even though the garment is known to be contaminated, it is said that the obligation is to wash a part of it. If he washes it with investigation [taharr] or without investigation, it becomes pure. The explanation shows that investigation has no effect, and that is to wash some of it.' Despite the original purity of the garment, doubt arises about the continued presence of the impurity because the washed part might be the spot, so one does not rule impurity by doubt. Thus al-Aspijabi narrated it in Sharh al-Jami' al-Kabir. He said: I heard Imam Taj al-Din Ahmad ibn Abd al-Aziz say this and analogize it to a case in al-Siyar al-Kabir: If we conquer a fortress and there is a dhimmi among them who is not known, it is not permissible to kill them because the impediment is confirmed with certainty. If some are killed or removed, it becomes permissible to kill the rest due to doubt about the continued existence of the prohibition. Similarly here. In al-Khulasah, after mentioning it without the reasoning: If he prays with it and then the impurity appears on another part, he must repeat what he prayed. (End). In al-Zahiriyyah: A garment in which there is an impurity whose location is unknown: wash the entire garment. (End). That is precaution. That reasoning is problematic to me, for washing a part causes doubt about the purity of the garment after the previous certainty of its contamination. Its summary is that it is doubt about removal after certainty of the existence of impurity. Doubt does not remove what was certain before. The truth is that the existence of doubt about whether the washed part and the removed individual are the location of the impurity and the protected blood necessarily causes doubt about the purity of the remaining part and the permissibility of the blood of the remaining individuals. As a necessary consequence of its becoming doubtful, the certainty about its contamination and protectedness is lifted. If it becomes doubtful in its impurity, prayer with it becomes permissible. However, if this is correct, then their agreed-upon statement, i.e., 'certainty is not removed by doubt,' would have no meaning. For then it would be inconceivable that doubt would occur in a place where certainty is established, such that a doubt occurs that does not remove that certainty. Therefore some investigators have verified that what is meant is that the ruling (hukm) of certainty is not removed by doubt. On this basis, the problem is resolved in terms of the ruling, not the proof. So we say: Even if doubt arises about the purity of the remaining part and its impurity, the ruling of the prior certainty of its impurity, which is the impermissibility of prayer, is not removed. Thus prayer is not valid after washing the part, because the emergent doubt does not remove the ruling of prior certainty, as has been verified that this is what is meant by their statement: certainty is not removed.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents the third major maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' with its primary hadith proof (the hadith about feeling something in the stomach). The author then quotes from Fath al-Qadir an application: if a garment is known to be impure but the exact spot of impurity is hidden, some scholars say washing a part suffices because the original purity of the garment creates doubt about the continued presence of impurity. This is analogized to a case of a dhimmi in a conquered fortress. However, the author finds this reasoning problematic: washing a part creates doubt about whether the garment has become pure, when there was prior certainty of impurity. Since doubt does not remove prior certainty, the garment remains impure. He then discusses the correct interpretation: the maxim means that the 'ruling' (hukm) of certainty is not removed by doubt. Thus, even if doubt arises about the impurity's removal, the original ruling (impermissibility of prayer with that garment) remains. Therefore, after washing only a part, the prayer is not valid. This is a critical refinement distinguishing between the certainty itself and its legal ruling.

Key Terms
اليقين (al-yaqin): Certain conviction about a matter, such as the impurity of a garment or the state of wudu.
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt where two possibilities are equally likely; it does not remove prior certainty.
حكم اليقين (hukm al-yaqin): The legal ruling that results from certainty (e.g., impermissibility of prayer with impure garment). This is what the maxim means is not removed by doubt, not the certainty itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's verified interpretation, what does the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' mean?
A) The state of certainty itself cannot be affected by subsequent doubt.
B) The ruling (hukm) of certainty is not removed by doubt.
C) Doubt can never affect any prior certainty in any way.
D) Washing part of an impure garment always makes it pure.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' means that the legal ruling established by certainty persists despite subsequent doubt; it does not refer to the subjective state of certainty.

Refining the Maxim of Certainty: The Case of Unknown Impurity
Critical analysis of a specific application of اليقين لا يزول بالشك

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the refined understanding of 'certainty is not removed by doubt' to distinguish between preserving the certainty itself and preserving its ruling.

Core Idea
The maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt' preserves the ruling of certainty, not the certainty itself—a key Hanafi distinction.

In this passage from Fath al-Qadir, the author critiques an earlier Hanafi view that washing one part of a garment with unknown impurity suffices to make it pure. The debate revolves around the interpretation of the famous maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt.' We will analyze the arguments and see why the correct understanding preserves the ruling of the prior certainty, not the substance of certainty itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
Consider a garment that is certainly impure (najis), but the exact spot of impurity is unknown. The question: what must be done to purify it for prayer?
The problem involves certainty of impurity and doubt about location.
2
State the earlier Hanafi view
Some Hanafi scholars (al-Usbijabi, Taj al-Din) argue that washing any part of the garment suffices, because doubt about the location of impurity arises, and the original presumption of purity is restored. They analogize to a case in siyar where killing some inhabitants of a fort creates doubt about a dhimmi's identity, making killing the rest permissible.
This view relies on the idea that doubt removes the prior certainty of impurity.
3
Understand the critique
The author of Fath al-Qadir objects: before washing, the garment was certainly impure. After washing only a part, doubt arises whether the impurity was removed. Since the prior certainty is established, the subsequent doubt cannot remove it. The maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt' must be interpreted to preserve the ruling of the certainty (impermissibility of prayer), not the certainty itself.
The critique distinguishes between the certainty and its ruling.
4
Apply the refined maxim
Thus, after washing one part, the ruling of the prior certainty (prayer is not allowed) remains. Prayer in that garment is invalid. The only way to be sure is to wash the entire garment, which is the precautionary view.
This shows the practical difference between the two interpretations.
5
Recognize the methodological takeaway
The passage teaches that legal maxims must be applied with precision. The maxim does not mean that any doubt nullifies prior certainty; it means that the ruling derived from that certainty remains in effect unless a new certainty replaces it.
This is a critical refinement for fatwa.

He hears a sound or finds an odor (narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, etc.) In Fath al-Qadir, from the chapter on impurities that clarifies it, we quote his statement in full: His saying: Purification from impurity is obligatory limited by possibility. But if removal is not possible due to the concealment of the specific affected spot while knowing the garment is impure, it is said that the obligation is to wash a part of it. If he washes it with or without estimation (taharrin), it becomes pure. The aspect mentioned shows that estimation has no effect, meaning he washes some of it. Since the original state is purity of the garment, doubt arises about the existence of impurity due to the possibility that the washed part is its location, so impurity is not judged by doubt. Thus Al-Usbijabi mentioned it in his commentary on al-Jami' al-Kabir, saying: I heard Imam Taj al-Din Ahmad ibn Abd al-Aziz say it and analogize it to a case in al-Siyar al-Kabir: If we conquer a fort and there is a dhimmi among them who is not known, it is not permissible to kill them due to the certain existence of the preventing factor. If some are killed or taken out, killing the rest becomes permissible due to doubt about the existence of the prohibition. Similarly here. In al-Khulasa after mentioning it without reasoning: If he prays with it, then impurity appears in another spot, he must repeat the prayers he prayed. (End). In al-Zahiriyya: A garment with impurity whose location is unknown: wash the entire garment. (End). That is precaution. This reasoning is problematic to me because washing a part causes doubt about the purity of the garment after certainty of its impurity before. The gist is that it is doubt about removal after certainty of the existence of impurity. And doubt does not remove the prior certainty. The truth is that the establishment of doubt that the washed part and the man taken out are the location of impurity and the protected blood necessarily causes doubt about the purity of the rest and the permissibility of spilling the blood of the rest. From the necessity of it becoming doubtful, the certainty of its impurity and protection is lifted. If it becomes doubtful in its impurity, prayer with it is permissible. However, if this were valid, their universally accepted statement, namely 'certainty is not removed by doubt,' would have no meaning. For then it would be inconceivable for doubt to arise in a place of established certainty, such that doubt is established without that certainty being removed by it. Therefore, some verifiers established that what is meant is that its ruling is not removed by doubt. On this basis, the problem is resolved in terms of ruling, not evidence. So we say: Even if doubt is established about the purity or impurity of the rest, the ruling of the previous certainty of its impurity, which is the impermissibility of prayer, is not removed. So prayer after washing the part is not valid, because the sudden doubt does not remove the ruling of the previous certainty, as it is verified that this is what is meant by their statement: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt.' So washing the rest and ruling it pure is problematic, and God knows best. End of the statement of Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt' (اليقين لا يزول بالشك) as applied to a specific fiqh problem. The author of Fath al-Qadir critiques an earlier view from al-Usbijabi that if a garment is certainly impure but the exact spot of impurity is unknown, washing one part of it renders the whole garment pure because doubt arises about the location, and the original purity of the garment is presumed. The author argues that this application is problematic: before washing, the garment was certainly impure; after washing one part, doubt arises whether the impurity was removed. Since the prior certainty of impurity is established, the subsequent doubt cannot remove it. Therefore, the correct application of the maxim is that the ruling of the previous certainty (impermissibility of prayer) remains, so prayer in such a garment is not valid. The passage includes an analogy from siyar (warfare) about a dhimmi in a fort: once some are killed, doubt arises about the identity of the dhimmi, allowing killing the rest. The author distinguishes between the ruling and the evidence, concluding that the maxim preserves the ruling of certainty, not the certainty itself. The passage ends by noting that the precautionary view is to wash the entire garment.

Key Terms
اليقين لا يزول بالشك (al-yaqīn lā yazūlu bi-al-shakk): A foundational legal maxim: certainty is not removed by doubt. In this passage, it is debated whether its application preserves the certainty itself or its ruling.
التحري (al-taḥarrī): Estimation or reasoned guess about the direction or location of something, here the spot of impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The garment must be washed three times.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt' preserves the legal ruling of the prior certainty, not the certainty itself—a critical distinction for correct fatwa.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate

The Maxim: Certainty Not Removed by Doubt - Meaning and Scope
Introduces the maxim and its operational definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'ما ثبت بيقين لا يرتفع إلا بيقين', explain its dabt as predominant probability (غالب الظن), and identify its scope across all fiqh chapters.

Core Idea
Certainty is only overridden by certainty; predominant probability acts as practical certainty.

In Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, the author introduces the third major legal maxim governing doubts. The maxim addresses how pre-existing certainties are resolved when doubt arises. The Hanafi school, through its later authorities, defines 'certainty' as including predominant probability. This lesson unpacks the maxim's text, its operational definition, and its place in the fatwa hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim text
The Arabic text of the maxim is 'ما ثبت بيقين لا يرتفع إلا بيقين'.
It is the third of the five major maxims.
2
Understand the key terms
'اليقين' means certainty; 'الشك' means doubt. However, the intended meaning here includes predominant probability (غالب الظن), as the text clarifies.
The dabt (sub-maxim) is 'غالب الظن في معنى اليقين'.
3
Recognize the scope
This maxim applies universally in fiqh: worship, transactions, family law, criminal law, etc.
The passage focuses on worship applications.
4
Apply to an initial example
From al-Multaqat: If one has no missed prayers but suspects past invalidity due to missing a condition, he may only make up what his predominant probability suggests, not all prayers.
Anything beyond predominant probability is disliked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أعاد في الوقت شك في ركوع أو وهو فيها أعاد وإن كان بعدها فلا وإن شك أنه كم صلى فإن كان أول مرة استأنف وإن كثر تحرى وإلا أخذ بالأقل وهذا إذا شك فيها قبل الفراغ وإن كان بعده فلا شيء عليه إلا إذا تذكر بعد الفراغ أنه ترك فرضا وشك في تعيينه قالوا يسجد سجدة واحدة ثم يقعد ثم يقوم فيصلي ركعة بسجدتين ثم يقعد ثم يسجد للسهو كذا في فتح القدير ولو أخبره عدل بعد السلام أنك صليت الظهر أربعا وشك في صدقه وكذبه فإنه في الثالثة أنه في التطوع ثم شك في الرابعة أنه في الظهر قالوا يكون في الظهر والشك ليس بشيء ولو تذكر مصلي العصر أنه ترك سجدة ولم يدر هل تركها من الظهر أو العصر الذي هو فيها تحرى فإن لم يقع تحريه على شيء يتم العصر ويسجد سجدة واحدة ثم يعيد الظهر احتياطا ثم يعيد العصر فإن لم يعد فلا شيء عليه وفي المجتبى إذا شك أنه كبر للافتتاح أو لا أو هل أحدث أو لا أو هل أصابت النجاسة ثوبه أو لا أو مسح رأسه أو لا استقبل إن كان أول مرة وإلا فلا (انتهى) ولو شك أنها تكبيرة الافتتاح أو القنوت لم يصر شارعا وتمامه في الشرح من آخر سجود السهو أنه ولو شك في أركان الحج ذكر الخصاف أنه يتحرى كما في الصلاة وقال عامة مشايخنا يؤدي ثانيا لأن تكرار الركن والزيادة عليه لا يفسد الحج وزيادة الركعة تفسد الصلاة فكان التحري في باب الصلاة أحوط كذا في المحيط وفي البدائع في الحج يبني على الأقل في ظاهر الرواية وفي البزازية شك في القيام في الفجر أنها الأولى أو الثانية رفضه وقعد قدر التشهد ثم صلى ركعتين بفاتحة وسورة ثم أتم وسجد للسهو فإن شك في سجدته أنها عن الأولى أم عن الثانية يمضي فيها وإن شك في السجدة الثانية لأن إتمامها لازم على كل حال وإذا رفع رأسه من السجدة الثانية قعد ثم قام وصلى ركعة وأتم بسجدة السهو وإن شك في سجدته أنه صلى الفجر ركعتين أو ثلاثا إن كان في السجدة الثانية فسدت صلاته وإن كان في السجدة الأولى يمكن إصلاحها عند محمد رحمه الله لأن تمام الماهية بالرفع عنده فترفع السجدة بالرفض ارتفاعها بالحدث فيقوم ويقعد ويسجد للسهو إلى أن قال نوع منه تذكر أنه ترك ركنا قوليا فسدت صلاته وإن ترك فعليا يحمل على ترك الركوع فيسجد ثم يقعد ثم يقوم ويصلي ركعة بسجدتين صلى صلاة يوم وليلة ثم تذكر أنه ترك القراءة في ركعة ولم يعلم نية صلاة أعاد الفجر والوتر وإن تذكر أنه ترك في ركعتين فكذلك وإن تذكر الترك في الأربع فذوات الأربع كلها (انتهى)
The liability is attached to the default, so it is not discharged except with certainty. This exception refers to a third maxim: what is established with certainty is not removed except with certainty. It is intended to mean predominant probability. Hence, the author of al-Multaqat said: If one has not missed any prayer and wishes to pray all the prayers of his life since he attained maturity?… No, it is not recommended for that unless his predominant opinion is that they were invalid due to lack of purity or omission of a condition; then he prays what he thinks is likely, and anything beyond that is disliked due to the prohibition. (End). Prostration of forgetfulness. Doubt about whether one prayed a prayer: if still in time, repeat. Doubt about bowing or while in the prayer: repeat if still in it, if after, no. If he doubts how many rak'ahs he prayed, if it is the first time, restart; if frequent, he acts on his conjecture; otherwise, he takes the minimum. This is if the doubt occurs before finishing. If after finishing, nothing is required unless he remembers after finishing that he left an obligatory act and doubts its identity; they said: he prostrates one prostration, then sits, then stands and prays one rak'ah with two prostrations, then sits, then prostrates for forgetfulness. This is in Fath al-Qadir. If a reliable person informs him after the salam that you prayed zuhr four rak'ahs and he doubts his truthfulness or falsehood, he repeats as a precaution because doubting his truthfulness is a doubt about the prayer. If there is a dispute between the imam and the congregation, if the imam is certain, he does not repeat; otherwise, he repeats based on their statement, as in al-Khulasa. If one prays one rak'ah with the intention of zuhr, then doubts in the second whether it is 'asr, then doubts in the third whether it is supererogatory, then doubts in the fourth whether it is zuhr, they said: he is in zuhr and the doubt is nothing. If a person praying 'asr remembers that he left a prostration and does not know whether he left it from zuhr or from the 'asr he is in, he should try to determine; if his conjecture does not settle on anything, he completes 'asr and prostrates one prostration, then repeats zuhr as a precaution, then repeats 'asr; if he does not repeat, nothing is upon him. In al-Mujtaba: If he doubts whether he said the opening takbir or not, or whether he had an event or not, or whether impurity touched his garment or not, or whether he wiped his head or not, he starts over if it is the first time, otherwise not. (End). And if he doubts whether it is the opening takbir or the qunut takbir, he does not become a prayer entrant; its completion is in the commentary from the end of the prostration of forgetfulness. And if he doubts about the pillars of Hajj, al-Khassaf said he should act on his conjecture like in prayer. Majority of our scholars said he performs a second time because repeating the pillar and adding to it does not invalidate Hajj, whereas an extra rak'ah invalidates prayer, so conjecture in the chapter of prayer is more precautionary, as in al-Muhit. In al-Bada'i, regarding Hajj, he builds on the minimum in the apparent narration. In al-Bazzaziyyah: Doubt about the standing in Fajr whether it is the first or second: he abandons it and sits for the amount of tashahhud, then prays two rak'ahs with Fatiha and a sura, then completes and prostrates for forgetfulness. If he doubts about his prostration whether it is for the first or second, he continues in it; if he doubts about the second prostration because its completion is necessary in any case… When he raises his head from the second prostration, he sits, then stands and prays one rak'ah and completes with a prostration of forgetfulness. If he doubts about his prostration whether he prayed Fajr two rak'ahs or three: if it is the second sajdah, his prayer becomes invalid; if it is the first, it can be rectified according to Muhammad (RAH) because the completion of the essence for him is with the raising, so he raises the prostration with abandonment, its raising is with an event… then he stands, sits, and prostrates for forgetfulness… until he said: a type of it: he remembers that he left a verbal pillar, his prayer invalidates; if he left an action pillar, it is treated like leaving the bowing, so he prostrates, then sits, then stands and prays one rak'ah with two prostrations. (If) he prayed a day and night prayer then remembered that he left the recitation in one rak'ah and did not know the intention of the prayer, he repeats Fajr and Witr. If he remembers that he left it in two rak'ahs, likewise. If he remembers the leaving in the four, then he repeats all four-rak'ah prayers. (End).
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the third major legal maxim, 'What is established with certainty is not removed except by certainty' (ما ثبت بيقين لا يرتفع إلا بيقين). The maxim is from the five major maxims of Islamic jurisprudence. The author clarifies that the intended meaning here is 'predominant probability' (غالب الظن), which is treated practically as certainty for legal purposes. The first application is from Al-Multaqat: It is not recommended to make up all prayers of one's life based on mere doubt, but if one's predominant opinion is that past prayers were invalid due to lack of purity or missing a condition, then one may make up only those that are likely deficient. Anything beyond is disliked due to prohibition of unnecessary worship. Then the text proceeds to numerous detailed rulings about doubt in prayer (sajdah al-sahw). These rulings demonstrate how the maxim is applied: when a doubt occurs during prayer, the default (origin) is considered, and one either acts on the likely true state or takes the minimum certain amount. For example, if a person doubts whether he prayed a certain prayer while still in the time, he repeats it (because the original liability remains certain). If the doubt arises after the time has expired, he does not repeat (since the original obligation was presumably fulfilled). In cases of doubt about the number of rak'ahs, if it is the first occurrence of such doubt, he restarts the prayer; if it occurs frequently, he acts on his best conjecture; otherwise, he takes the minimum number (الأقل). Forgetting a fard (obligatory part) and later doubting which one, the prescribed remedy is specific steps including an extra rak'ah and sujood al-sahw. The passage also includes a reference to Hajj rituals, where the same principle applies but with a different effect because repeating a pillar in Hajj does not invalidate the act unlike an extra rak'ah in prayer. Overall, this section thoroughly illustrates how the maxim governs resolutions of doubt in worship.

Key Terms
اليقين (al-yaqīn): Certainty, the opposite of doubt. In this maxim, it includes predominant probability (ghalib al-dhann) according to Hanafis.
غالب الظن (ghālib al-ẓann): Predominant probability; a strong conjecture that is practically certain.
الذمة (al-dhimma): Legal responsibility or liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi interpretation in this passage, what does 'اليقين' in the maxim 'ما ثبت بيقين لا يرتفع إلا بيقين' primarily refer to?
A) Absolute mathematical certainty
B) Predominant probability (غالب الظن)
C) Eyewitness testimony
D) Any doubt

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school implements the maxim using predominant probability as the standard for certainty, enabling practical resolution of doubts in worship and transactions.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
10 min
Applications of the Maxim in Prayer Doubts
Applies the maxim to specific prayer doubt cases with detailed rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'ما ثبت بيقين لا يرتفع إلا بيقين' to various prayer doubt scenarios by identifying the original certainty and determining whether to repeat, act on conjecture, or take the minimum.

Core Idea
In prayer doubts, the original state of obligation or correctness governs: repeat if in doubt of performance, act on conjecture if frequent doubter, else take the minimum.

This lesson applies the major maxim to a wide range of doubt cases in prayer. The passage presents detailed rulings from Hanafi sources, illustrating how the maxim resolves questions of missed rak'ahs, forgotten pillars, and conflicts between imam and congregation. Understanding these applications equips the future mufti to advise the laity on common worship doubts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of doubt
Determine whether the doubt is about performing an entire prayer, about a specific rak'ah, about a pillar (rukn), or about the intention (niyyah).
2
Determine the timing
Check if the doubt occurs before, during, or after the prayer. For doubts after prayer, usually no action unless a pillar is known to be missing.
3
Assess the doubter's frequency
Distinguish between a first-time doubter (مبتدئ) and a frequent doubter (كثير الشك). First-time doubter restarts; frequent doubter acts on conjecture (تحرى) or takes the minimum (الأقل).
4
Apply the maxim
The original certainty (e.g., that the prayer was due, or that a rak'ah was performed correctly) is not removed by doubt. Either repeat with certainty, act on predominant probability, or build on the minimum.
5
Handle forgotten pillars
If a pillar is forgotten and the person remembers after prayer, specific steps including sujood al-sahw and sometimes an extra rak'ah are prescribed, as detailed in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشتغل الذمة بالأصل فلا يبرأ إلا باليقين وهذا الاستثناء راجع إلى قاعدة ثالثة هي: ما ثبت بيقين لا يرتفع إلا بيقين والمراد به غالب الظن ولذا قال في الملتقط : ولو لم يفته من الصلاة شيء وأحب أن يقضي صلاة عمره منذ أدرك لا أنه منذ أدرك لا يستحب لذلك إلا إذا كان أكبر ظنه فسادها بسلب الطهارة أو ترك شرط فحينئذ يقضي ما غلب على ظنه وما زاد عليه يكره لورود النهي عنه (انتهى). سجود شك في صلاة هل صلاها أم لا أعاد في الوقت شك في ركوع أو وهو فيها أعاد وإن كان بعدها فلا وإن شك أنه كم صلى فإن كان أول مرة استأنف وإن كثر تحرى وإلا أخذ بالأقل وهذا إذا شك فيها قبل الفراغ وإن كان بعده فلا شيء عليه إلا إذا تذكر بعد الفراغ أنه ترك فرضا وشك في تعيينه قالوا يسجد سجدة واحدة ثم يقعد ثم يقوم فيصلي ركعة بسجدتين ثم يقعد ثم يسجد للسهو كذا في فتح القدير ولو أخبره عدل بعد السلام أنك صليت الظهر أربعا وشك في صدقه وكذبه فإنه يعيد احتياطا لأن الشك في صدقه شك في الصلاة ولو وقع الخلاف بين الإمام والقوم فإن كان الإمام على يقين لا يعيد وإلا أعاد بقولهم كذا في الخلاصة ولو صلى ركعة بنية الظهر ثم شك في الثانية أنه في العصر ثم شك في الثالثة أنه في التطوع ثم شك في الرابعة أنه في الظهر قالوا يكون في الظهر والشك ليس بشيء ولو تذكر مصلي العصر أنه ترك سجدة ولم يدر هل تركها من الظهر أو العصر الذي هو فيها تحرى فإن لم يقع تحريه على شيء Commentary
This passage presents the third major legal maxim, 'What is established with certainty is not removed except by certainty' (ما ثبت بيقين لا يرتفع إلا بيقين). The maxim is from the five major maxims of Islamic jurisprudence. Anything beyond is disliked due to prohibition of unnecessary worship. Then the text proceeds to numerous detailed rulings about doubt in prayer (sajdah al-sahw). These rulings demonstrate how the maxim is applied: when a doubt occurs during prayer, the default (origin) is considered, and one either acts on the likely true state or takes the minimum certain amount. For example, if a person doubts whether he prayed a certain prayer while still in the time, he repeats it (because the original liability remains certain). Overall, this section thoroughly illustrates how the maxim governs resolutions of doubt in worship.

Key Terms
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt; uncertainty between two possibilities with neither outweighing the other.
التحري (al-taḥarrī): To exert effort to determine the correct ruling by seeking the most likely truth, used when exact knowledge is lacking.
الأقل (al-aqall): The minimum; the smaller number when doubt exists about quantity.
كثير الشك (kathīr al-shakk): A frequent doubter; one who experiences doubt in most of his actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rulings in this passage, what should a frequent doubter (كثير الشك) do when he doubts the number of rak'ahs during prayer?
A) Restart the prayer from the beginning.
B) Act on his predominant conjecture; if no conjecture, take the minimum.
C) Always take the maximum number to be safe.
D) Continue and ignore the doubt.

Key Takeaway
The maxim is applied flexibly in prayer doubts: first-time doubters restart, frequent doubters use conjecture or minimum, and forgotten pillars require specific sujood al-sahw procedures.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate

Applying the Certainty Maxim to Talaq, Debt, Zakat, Fasting, and Iddah
Continues the maxim's applications to talaq, debt, zakat, siyam, and 'iddah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt' to five distinct fiqh scenarios (talaq, debt, zakat, fasting, iddah) and state the correct ruling in each.

Core Idea
When in doubt, build on the minimum certainty — unless precaution demands acting on the maximum.

This lesson walks through five practical applications of the third grand maxim (ما ثبت بيقين لا يرتفع إلا بيقين). Each case tests your ability to identify the original certainty and decide whether to follow the minimum or the precautionary maximum. You will see how the same principle yields different outcomes depending on context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
Name the specific uncertainty (e.g., number of divorces, amount of debt).
2
Determine the original certainty
What state existed before the doubt? (e.g., marriage is certain, no debt owed is certain).
3
Apply the rule: certainty remains
If the doubt does not reach certainty, the original state persists.
Shakk does not remove yaqin.
4
Check for predominant opinion or precaution
If the doubt is equal and the matter involves rights/worship, the stricter option may be taken (ihtiyat).
Ihtiyat can override the minimum.
5
State the final ruling
Combine the above to issue the fatwa.

ومنها شك هل طلق أم لا لم يقع شك أنه طلق واحدة أو أكثر بنى على الأقل كما ذكر الأسبيجابي إلا أن يستيقن بالأكثر أو يكون أكبر ظنه على خلافه وإن قال الزوج عزمت على أنه ثلاث يتركها وإن أخبره عدول حضروا ذلك المجلس بأنها واحدة وصدقهم أخذ بقولهم إن كانوا عدولاً وعن الإمام الثاني حلف بطلاقها ولا يدري أثلاث أم أقل يتحرى وإن استويا عمل بأشد ذلك عليه كذا في البزازية ومنها شك في الخارج أمني أو مذي وكان في النوم فإن تذكر احتلاما وجب الغسل اتفاقا وإلا لم يجب عند أبي يوسف رحمه الله عملا بالأقل وهو المذي ووجب عندهما احتياطا كقولهما بالنقض بالمباشرة الفاحشة وكقول الإمام في الفأرة الميتة إذا وجدت في بئر ولم يدر متى وقعت وهنا فروع لم أرها الآن الأول لو كان عليه دين وشك في قدره ينبغي لزوم إخراج القدر المتيقن وفي البزازية من القضاء إذا شك فيما يدعي عليه ينبغي أن يرضى خصمه ولا يحلف احترازا عن الوقوع في الحرام وإن أبي خصمه إلا حلفه إن كان أكبر رأيه أن المدعي محق لا يحلف وإن كان أكبر رأيه أنه مبطل ساغ له الحلف (انتهى) الثاني له إبل وبقر وغنم سائمة وشك في أن عليه زكاة كلها أو بعضها ينبغي أن تلزمه زكاة الكل الثالث شك فيما عليه من الصيام الرابع شكت فيما عليها من العدة هل هي عدة طلاق أو وفاة ينبغي أن يلزم الأكثر عليها وعلى الصائم أخذا من قولهم لو ترك صلاة وشك أنها أية صلاة تلزمه صلاة يوم وليلة عملا بالاحتياط
And among them: Doubt whether he divorced or not: it does not occur. Doubt whether he divorced once or more: he builds on the minimum, as al-Asbijabi mentioned, unless he is certain of the more or his predominant opinion is contrary. And if the husband said, 'I resolved on three,' he leaves her. If upright persons who attended that session inform him that it was one, and he believes them, he takes their statement if they are upright. From Imam al-Thani: He swore by divorcing her and does not know whether three or less, he tries to determine. If they are equal, he acts on the more severe upon him, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And among them: Doubt about the emitted substance: whether it is mani or madhi; if it was during sleep, then if he remembers a wet dream, ghusl is obligatory by consensus. Otherwise, according to Abu Yusuf (RAH), it is not obligatory, acting on the minimum (madhi), and according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), it is obligatory as a precaution, like their view regarding nullification by excessive touching, and like the Imam's view regarding the dead mouse found in a well without knowing when it fell. And here are branches that I have not seen now: First: If one owes a debt and doubts its amount, he should pay the certain amount. In al-Bazzaziyyah from the section of judging: if he doubts what he is being sued for, he should satisfy his opponent and not take an oath to avoid falling into the forbidden. If his opponent refuses except his oath: if his predominant opinion is that the plaintiff is truthful, he does not take the oath; if his predominant opinion is that he is false, it is permissible for him to take the oath. (End). Second: He has camels, cows, and sheep that are grazing (sa'imah) and doubts whether zakat is due on all or some: he should pay zakat on all. Third: Doubt about how much fasting is due on him. Fourth: A woman doubts about her 'iddah: whether it is 'iddah of divorce or death. She should observe the longer period. And for the one fasting, from their statement: if one leaves a prayer and doubts which prayer it is, he owes a full day-and-night prayer, as a precaution.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage continues applying the same third maxim (ما ثبت بيقين لا يرتفع إلا بيقين) to other fiqh topics. Starting with divorce (talaq): if a husband doubts whether he issued a divorce, it is not considered to have occurred (since the original marriage is certain). If he doubts the number, he builds on the minimum (one) unless certainty or predominant opinion indicates more. If he consciously resolves on three divorces, that action is valid. The testimony of reliable witnesses regarding the number is accepted if he trusts them. In case of an oath regarding divorce where he is uncertain of the number, he seeks conjecture; if both possibilities are equal, he must act on the most severe option (i.e., three divorces) because that is precautionary regarding the rights of the woman. Next, regarding ritual impurity: if a person wakes up with wetness and doubts whether it is maniy (requiring ghusl) or madhiy (requiring only wudu), if he remembers a wet dream, ghusl is obligatory by consensus. Without memory of a dream, Imam Abu Yusuf says ghusl is not obligatory, acting on the minimal probability (madhiy), while the two Imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) require ghusl as a precaution, citing analogy with cases like a dead mouse in a well (where the ruling depends on when it fell, but precaution is taken). This shows a difference within the Hanafi school on applying the maxim: Abu Yusuf strictly adheres to the original certain state (no impurity), while the sahihayn lean toward precaution when doubt involves potential major impurity. Lastly, the author mentions additional branches he did not see elsewhere: (1) Debt: pay the certain minimum. (2) Zakat on grazing livestock: if in doubt, pay zakat on all (precaution). (3) Fasting: act on the certain minimum. (4) 'Iddah (waiting period): if unsure whether it is for divorce or death, observe the longer period (precaution). Similarly, if one misses a prayer and doubts which one, he must make up a full day and night's worth of prayers as a precaution. These examples demonstrate the consistent principle: when certainty is absent, one either builds on the certain minimum or adopts the precautionary (more burdensome) course to ensure compliance.

Key Terms
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt, uncertainty between two possibilities where neither outweighs the other.
الأقل (al-aqall): The minimum certain number when a larger number is in doubt.
الاحتياط (al-ihtiyāt): Precautionary measure; adopting the more demanding ruling to ensure compliance with the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman is unsure whether her 'iddah is for divorce (three months) or death (four months and ten days). According to the Hanafi maxim, what should she do?
A) Observe three months (minimum)
B) Observe four months and ten days (maximum)
C) Observe until she is certain
D) Consult a scholar and follow their opinion

Key Takeaway
When doubt arises, apply the certainty maxim by either building on the certain minimum or adopting the precautionary maximum, depending on whether the matter involves individual rights (minimum) or obligations to Allah (precaution).

The Internal Hanafi Dispute on Ghusl: Certainty vs. Precaution
Continues the maxim's applications; presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ghusl on waking with wetness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the views of Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn on the requirement of ghusl when waking up with wetness without remembering a dream, and apply the tarjih principles to determine the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Ihtiyat (precaution) can override the minimum-certainty rule when the doubt involves a major act of worship or potential impurity.

This lesson focuses on a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: is ghusl obligatory when a person wakes up with wetness but no dream? Abu Yusuf holds that original purity remains (no ghusl), while the two companions require ghusl as a precaution. The debate reveals how the same maxim can be applied differently depending on whether one prioritizes strict certainty or cautionary fulfillment of obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt and the original state
Original state: purity (no janabah). Doubt: is the wetness mani or madhi?
2
Recall Abu Yusuf's position
He builds on the original certainty (madhi, which does not require ghusl).
Acts on the minimum certain possibility.
3
Recall the Sahibayn's position
They require ghusl as precaution (ihtiyat).
They consider that the majority of such emissions are maniy.
4
Understand the analogy
Like the dead mouse in a well, where the hukm is precautionary when time of fall is unknown.
Analogy from Imam (Abu Hanifa).
5
Apply tarjih — which view carries weight?
Later Hanafi scholars often prefer precaution in matters of worship, so the Sahibayn's view is mufta bihi for general practice.
Consult fatwa collections.

Key Terms
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt, uncertainty between two possibilities where neither outweighs the other.

المني (al-maniy): Semen; its emission requires ghusl (major ablution).
المذي (al-madhiy): Pre-seminal fluid; its emission requires wudu only, not ghusl.

Key Takeaway
In a Hanafi internal dispute, the precautionary view (ihtiyat) is often preferred for matters of worship, as in the case of ghusl upon waking with wetness without a dream.

The Basic Operation of the Maxim 'al-Asl al-'Adam'
Direct applications of the maxim 'al-asl al-'adam'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-asl al-'adam' to resolve disputes over obligations, profits, and marital rights by determining which party's statement is accepted.

Core Idea
The original state is non-existence: the one who denies an obligation or claim has the stronger position.

This lesson introduces one of the most frequently invoked maxims in Hanafi fatwa: al-asl al-'adam. Derived from the principle 'certainty is not removed by doubt,' it sets the default presumption in disputes. When a claim is made, the party denying the existence of the obligation, relationship, or profit is favored—unless evidence proves otherwise. The mufti relies on this maxim to decide whose statement is accepted in countless daily disputes.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its basis
Understand that 'al-asl al-'adam' means the original state of a thing is non-existence until proven. It is derived from the greater maxim 'al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk' (certainty is not removed by doubt).
This is a wusta (intermediate) maxim governing many furuat.
2
Identify the disputed fact
In each case, determine what fact is being denied. The party who denies the existence of a right, obligation, or event relies on the original non-existence.
3
Apply the maxim to the dispute
Decide whose statement is accepted based on the presumption. Typically, the denier's statement is accepted because the original state is non-existence.
If both parties have a plausible 'original,' the stronger one prevails (see Lesson 2).
4
Confirm with textual evidence
Review the examples from the passage: the impotent man case, the divorce dispute, the mudarib's profit claim, the wife's maintenance claim. Each illustrates the maxim in action.
5
Note the exception (preview)
The passage hints at an exception when another original (e.g., qawl al-qabid) overrides. This will be covered in Lesson 2.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة : الأصل العدم وفيها فروع منها، أخذا من القاعدة : القول على قولنا في الوطء لأن الأصل العدم، لكن قالوا في العنين : لو ادعى الوطء وأنكرت وقلن بكر، خيرت. وإن قلن ثيب فالقول له لكونه منكرًا استحقاق الفرقة عليه. والأصل السلامة من وفي القنية : افترقا وقالت افترقنا بعد الدخول، وقال الزوج قبله. فالقول قولها لأنها تنكر سقوط نصف المهر . (انتهى). ومنها القول : قول الشريك والمضارب إنه لم يربح لأن الأصل عدمه، وكذا لو قال لم أربح إلا كذا لأن الأصل عدم الزائد وفي المجمع من الإقرار وجعلنا القول للمضارب إذا أتى باليقين وقال هما أصل وربح لا لرب المال. (انتهى). لأن الأصل، وإن كان عدم الربح، لكن عارضه أصل آخر، وهو أن القول قول القابض في مقدار ما قبضه ولو ادعت المرأة النفقة على الزوج بعد فرضها فادعى الوصول إليها وأنكرت فالقول لها كالدائن إذا أنكر وصول الدين. ولو ادعت المرأة نفقة أولادها الصغار بعد فرضها وادعى الأب الإنفاق، فالقول له مع اليمين كما في الخانية والثانية خرجت عن القاعدة فليتأمل. وكذا في قدر رأس المال لأن الأصل عدم الزيادة. وكذا في أنه ما نهاه عن شراء كذا لأن الأصل عدم النهي. ولو ادعى المالك أنها قرض والآخذ أنها مضاربة فالقول فيها قول الآخذ لأنهما اتفقا على جواز التصرّف له والأصل عدم الضمان، ولذا قال في الكنز : وإن قال أخذت
Rule: The original state is non-existence. And within it are branches, among them, taken from the rule: The statement is on our side regarding intercourse because the original state is non-existence. But they said regarding the impotent man: If he claims intercourse and she denies and they say she is a virgin, she is given choice. If they say she is a non-virgin, then the statement is his because he is denying the entitlement of separation from him. And the original state is safety from... In al-Qunyah: They separated and she said we separated after consummation, and the husband said before it. So the statement is hers because she denies the fall of half the mahr. (End). And among them: The statement of the partner and the mudarib that he did not make profit because the original state is its non-existence. Similarly, if he said I did not profit except such-and-such, because the original state is the non-existence of extra. And in al-Majma' from al-Iqrar: We made the statement for the mudarib if he brought certainty and said both are principal and profit, not for the owner of the capital. (End). Because the original, even though it is non-existence of profit, was contradicted by another original, which is that the statement of the receiver is accepted regarding the amount he received. And if a woman claims maintenance from her husband after its determination, and he claims delivery to her and she denies, then the statement is hers like the creditor when he denies receipt of the debt. And if a woman claims maintenance for her minor children after its determination, and the father claims spending, then the statement is his with an oath as in al-Khaniyya; the second [case?] came out of the rule, so let it be pondered. Similarly regarding the amount of capital because the original state is no increase. Similarly regarding that he did not forbid him from buying such-and-such because the original state is no prohibition. And if the owner claims it is a loan and the taker claims it is a mudaraba, then the statement is that of the taker because they agreed on the permissibility of disposal for him and the original state is no liability. Hence it is said in al-Kanz: If he said I took...
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the fundamental maxim 'al-asl al-'adam' (the original state is non-existence) and its numerous applications in Hanafi fiqh. The maxim is a corollary of the principle of certainty: what is not proven to exist is presumed absent. The text demonstrates how this maxim governs disputes in marriage, partnership, agency, maintenance, and contracts. Notably, it also shows that when two contradictory originals apply (e.g., original non-existence of profit vs. original acceptance of the receiver's statement), the stronger or more specific original prevails. The mufti must carefully balance competing maxims in such cases. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing with more examples.

Key Terms
الأصل العدم (al-asl al-'adam): The legal presumption that a state of non-existence is the original condition until proven otherwise
القول قول القابض (al-qawl qawl al-qābiḍ): The statement of the receiver (of money or goods) is accepted regarding what he received

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A mudarib (investment manager) claims he made no profit. The capital owner claims there was profit but has no proof. According to Hanafi fiqh, whose statement is accepted?
A) The owner's, because the capital is his.
B) The mudarib's, because the original is no profit.
C) The court's decision.
D) Both must bring evidence.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-asl al-'adam' presumes non-existence; always identify the party denying the existence of a right or obligation to apply it.

When the Maxim is Overridden: Conflicts and Exceptions
Conflicts and exceptions to the maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of resolving conflicts between legal originals and identify when 'al-asl al-'adam' is overridden by stronger presumptions.

Core Idea
When two originals conflict, the one based on stronger evidence or specificity prevails.

The previous lesson showed the straightforward application of al-asl al-'adam. However, the Hanafi system recognizes that multiple legal presumptions may apply simultaneously. The mufti must then determine which original is stronger. This lesson examines the conflicts and exceptions explicit in the passage, including the famous override by the maxim 'al-qawl qawl al-qabid' and the exceptional case of a mother claiming child maintenance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict of originals
In the mudarib case where he brings certainty ('atā bi al-yaqīn'), the original non-existence of profit competes with the original that the receiver's statement about what he received is accepted. The text says the mudarib's statement is accepted because he brought certainty, and the owner's claim of extra profit is overridden.
Certainty removes the doubt that gives rise to the opponent's original.
2
Analyze the loan vs. mudaraba case
Owner claims loan; receiver claims mudaraba. The receiver's statement is accepted because both agreed that he had the right to transact, and the original is absence of liability. Here, al-asl al-'adam of liability competes with the owner's claim; but the agreement on permissibility of disposal tips the scale.
3
Examine the exception to al-asl al-'adam
In the case of a mother claiming child maintenance after determination, the father's statement (that he spent) is accepted with an oath, even though the mother denies receipt. This is an exception; the text says 'the second came out of the rule'.
The reason is the child's need and the likelihood that the father would spend.
4
Formulate the rule for conflicting originals
The Hanafi approach: when two originals apply, the stronger one (more specific, based on certainty, or serving a higher purpose) overrides the weaker. The mufti must justify the choice.
5
Apply to a new conflict
Practice with a scenario where a creditor claims repayment and debtor denies. Which original applies? (Answer: al-asl al-'adam of payment favors debtor, but if creditor has a document, that changes the presumption.)

قاعدة : الأصل العدم وفيها فروع منها، أخذا من القاعدة : القول على قولنا في الوطء لأن الأصل العدم، لكن قالوا في العنين : لو ادعى الوطء وأنكرت وقلن بكر، خيرت. وإن قلن ثيب فالقول له لكونه منكرًا استحقاق الفرقة عليه. والأصل السلامة من وفي القنية : افترقا وقالت افترقنا بعد الدخول، وقال الزوج قبله. فالقول قولها لأنها تنكر سقوط نصف المهر . Rule: The original state is non-existence. And within it are branches, among them, taken from the rule: The statement is on our side regarding intercourse because the original state is non-existence. And among them: The statement of the partner and the mudarib that he did not make profit because the original state is its non-existence. And in al-Majma' from al-Iqrar: We made the statement for the mudarib if he brought certainty and said both are principal and profit, not for the owner of the capital. (End). Because the original, even though it is non-existence of profit, was contradicted by another original, which is that the statement of the receiver is accepted regarding the amount he received. And if a woman claims maintenance from her husband after its determination, and he claims delivery to her and she denies, then the statement is hers like the creditor when he denies receipt of the debt. And if a woman claims maintenance for her minor children after its determination, and the father claims spending, then the statement is his with an oath as in al-Khaniyya; the second [case?] came out of the rule, so let it be pondered. Similarly regarding the amount of capital because the original state is no increase. Similarly regarding that he did not forbid him from buying such-and-such because the original state is no prohibition. Commentary
This passage presents the fundamental maxim 'al-asl al-'adam' (the original state is non-existence) and its numerous applications in Hanafi fiqh. The maxim is a corollary of the principle of certainty: what is not proven to exist is presumed absent. The text demonstrates how this maxim governs disputes in marriage, partnership, agency, maintenance, and contracts. Notably, it also shows that when two contradictory originals apply (e.g., original non-existence of profit vs. original acceptance of the receiver's statement), the stronger or more specific original prevails. The mufti must carefully balance competing maxims in such cases. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing with more examples.

Key Terms
الأصل العدم (al-asl al-'adam): The legal presumption that a state of non-existence is the original condition until proven otherwise
القول قول القابض (al-qawl qawl al-qābiḍ): The statement of the receiver (of money or goods) is accepted regarding what he received

Key Takeaway

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
15 min
Maxim: Original Absence (al-Asl al-'Adam) with Furuat
Defining the maxim 'original absence' and listing its many applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim al-asl al-‘adam (original non-existence) to resolve common fiqh disputes and identify the exception for essential attributes (al-sifat al-asliyyah).

Core Idea
Where doubt arises, original absence is presumed—except for essential attributes, where existence is the base.

This lesson introduces one of the most frequently invoked legal presumptions in Hanafi fiqh: al-asl al-‘adam. You will see how this maxim governs the burden of proof in dozens of cases—from marital disputes to commercial transactions—and learn the critical refinement that distinguishes accidental attributes from essential ones.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim clearly
Define 'original absence' in legal context: the default assumption that a claimed fact (right, event, obligation) did not occur until proven.
This shifts the burden of proof to the claimant.
2
Identify the furu' in the text
Go through the applications listed: impotence, timing of divorce, profit, maintenance, loan/mudarabah, etc. Note the ruling in each.
Focus on who is the denier (munkir) and why their word is taken.
3
Determine claimant and denier
For each case, use the maxim to decide which party is asserting a new fact (claimant) and which denies it (denier). The denier's word is taken.
In some cases, conflicting originals may apply (e.g., mudarib's word on amount received).
4
Note exceptions and refinements
The tanbih: the maxim applies only to accidental attributes (e.g., profession); for essential attributes (e.g., virginity) the original is existence. Also note the child maintenance exception.
These exceptions prevent absurd presumptions.
5
Apply to a new case
Take a modern dispute (e.g., credit card agreement) and apply the maxim to determine whose word is taken.
Always check if the disputed fact concerns an accidental or essential attribute.

Rule: The original is non-existence, and within it are branches, including: taken from the rule: the statement is the word of our school regarding intercourse because the original is non-existence. But they said regarding the impotent man: if he claims intercourse and she denies and she is said to be a virgin, she is given the choice. If she is said to be a non-virgin, then the statement is his because he is denying liability to separation upon him. And the original is freedom from defect. And in al-Qunyah: they separated and she said we separated after consummation, and the husband said before it. Then the statement is her statement because she denies the fall of half the dower. (End). And among them: the statement of the partner and the mudarib that he did not profit because the original is its non-existence, and likewise if he said I did not profit except such-and-such because the original is non-existence of excess. And in al-Majma' from al-Iqrar: and we made the statement for the mudarib if he brings certainty and says they are capital and profit, not for the owner of capital. (End). Because the original, even though it is non-existence of profit, but another original opposed it, which is that the statement is the word of the recipient regarding the amount he received. And if the woman claims maintenance from the husband after it was fixed and he claims delivery and she denies, then the statement is hers like the creditor if he denies receipt of the debt. And if the woman claims maintenance for her young children after it was fixed and the father claims he provided it, then the statement is his with an oath as in al-Khaniyyah, and the second case departed from the rule, so let it be considered. And likewise regarding the amount of capital because the original is non-existence of increase. And likewise regarding that he did not prohibit him from buying such-and-such because the original is non-existence of prohibition. And if the owner claims that it is a loan and the recipient claims it is mudarabah, then the statement is of the recipient because they agreed on the permissibility of disposal for him and the original is non-existence of liability. Hence it is said in al-Kanz: and if he said I took from you one thousand as a deposit and it perished, and he said I took it as usurpation, then he is liable; and if he said you gave it to me as a deposit and he said I usurped it, no. (End). And in al-Bazzaziyyah: he gave to another an 'ayn then they differed; the giver said it was a loan, and the other said it was a gift, then the word is of the giver. (End). Because the claimant of gift claims discharge from value while the 'ayn is itself valuable. And among them: if the woman inserted the nipple of her breast into the mouth of the infant and it is not known whether milk entered his throat or not, marriage is not prohibited because there is doubt about the prohibition; thus in al-Walwalijiyyah, and its completion will come in the rule that the original regarding private parts is prohibition. And among them: if they differ regarding receipt of the sold item and the leased object, then the statement is of the denier as in Ijazah al-Tahdhib. And among them: if a debt is established against him by acknowledgment or evidence, and he claims payment or discharge, then the statement is for the creditor because the original is non-existence. And among them: if they differ regarding the pre-existence of a defect and the seller denies it, then the statement is his. And there is disagreement in its reasoning: it is said because the original is its non-existence, and it is said because the original is the binding of the contract. And among them: if they differ regarding stipulating the option, then it is said the statement is for him who negates it in operation because the original is its non-existence, and it is said for him who claims it because he denies the binding of the contract. And we have related the two statements in the commentary. And the relied-upon is the first. And among them: if he said I usurped from you one thousand and made profit in it ten thousand, and the usurped-from said but I commanded you to trade with it, then the statement is for the owner as in Iqrar al-Bazzaziyyah; meaning by the original which is non-existence of usurpation. And among them: if they differ regarding seeing the sold item, then the statement is for the buyer because the original is its non-existence; and if they differ regarding a change in the sold item after seeing it, then the statement is for the seller because the original is non-existence of change. Note: The original non-existence is not absolute, but rather it is in accidental attributes. As for essential attributes, the original is existence. And derived from that: if he bought it on the condition that it is a baker or a scribe and he denies the existence of that attribute, then the statement is his because the original is its non-existence since they are accidental attributes. And if he bought it on condition that it is a virgin and he denies the existence of virginity and the seller claims it, then the statement is for the seller because the original is
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the major Hanafi maxim 'al-asl al-‘adam' (the original state is non-existence) with an extensive list of applications (furu'). The author begins by stating the rule, then immediately provides examples. The maxim means that in cases of dispute, the default presumption is that a claimed new fact (e.g., a payment, a prohibition, a profit) did not occur. The burden of proof is on the one asserting the new fact. The first application concerns a claim of sexual intercourse in cases of impotence ( 'inan): if the wife denies intercourse and is a virgin, she gets the choice; if she is a non-virgin, the husband's word is taken because he denies liability to divorce. Then a case from al-Qunyah: if the wife claims divorce occurred after consummation (so full dower due) and the husband denies, her word is taken because she denies reduction of dower. The next set involves partners and mudarib (silent partnership): the default is no profit, so the partner's word is taken. But in the Majma', a nuance: the mudarib's word is accepted if he claims the amount is capital and profit (not all for the investor), because a default principle of recipient's word on amount received opposes the profit default. In maintenance claims: if wife claims maintenance after it was fixed and husband claims he gave it, her word is taken (like a creditor denying receipt). But if she claims for children, the father's word is taken with oath, because the father is denying liability for future payments. This second case departs from the general rule, noted for reflection. Further furu': disputes about capital amount (original is no increase), prohibition of purchase (original is no prohibition), classification of transaction (if one says loan, other says mudarabah, the recipient's word is taken because both agree on permissibility of disposal and original is no liability). Then a case from al-Kanz: if someone says 'I took as deposit and it perished' and the owner says 'you took as usurpation', he is liable; but if owner says 'I gave as deposit' and the taker says 'I usurped it', then not liable. Because the taker's claim of deposit is an acknowledgment of trust, so no liability. Another case: gift vs loan – the giver's word is taken because claiming gift requires discharge from value. Then cases on breastfeeding causing prohibition: doubt about milk ingestion does not create prohibition because the prohibition is doubted. Also disputes about receipt of sold/rented item: denier's word. Debt claim: if debtor claims payment, creditor's word is taken (original is non-payment). Defect disputes: if buyer claims defect existed prior to sale and seller denies, the seller's word is taken; reasoning differs but both point to original non-existence or binding of contract. Option stipulation: the one who negates it has the stronger claim (relying on original absence of option). Usurpation with profit: if the usurper claims the owner authorized trade, the owner's word is taken because original is non-existence of authorization. Sight of sold item: buyer's word on non-sight (original no sight); after sight, seller's word on change (original no change). The passage concludes with an important note (tanbih): the maxim of non-existence applies only to accidental attributes, not essential ones. For essential attributes, the original is existence. Applications: if a sale is conditioned on the slave being a baker (accidental), the buyer's denial of that attribute is accepted (original non-existence). But if sale is conditioned on virginity (a default existence in a young woman), the seller's claim of virginity is accepted (original existence). This nuance refines the rule.

Key Terms
الأصل العدم (al-asl al-'adam): The legal presumption that a claimed fact (right, event, obligation) did not occur until proven otherwise.
الصفات العارضة (al-sifat al-'aridah): Accidental or non-essential attributes (e.g., profession, literacy) – original is non-existence.
الصفات الأصلية (al-sifat al-asliyyah): Essential or inherent attributes (e.g., virginity, freedom) – original is existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over whether a sold slave is a virgin, the seller claims virginity and the buyer denies. According to the tanbih, whose word is accepted?
A) The buyer's, because original is non-existence.
B) The seller's, because virginity is an essential attribute and original is existence.
C) The buyer's, because he is the one claiming a deficiency.
D) The seller's, because he is the defendant.

Key Takeaway
Al-asl al-‘adam is a powerful legal presumption that shifts the burden of proof, but it must be restricted to accidental attributes; for essential attributes, the original is existence.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
beginner
