The Default Ruling for Things: Permissibility or Prohibition?
Raises the dispute over the default ruling for things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two competing views on the default ruling for things and explain the Hanafi position that distinguishes between acts of worship and worldly matters.

Core Idea
The default for things is debated: Shafi'i says permissibility; Hanafis distinguish worship (prohibition) from transactions (permissibility).

This passage introduces a foundational legal maxim: what is the original ruling for actions and objects not addressed by texts? Imam al-Shafi'i held that everything is permissible until evidence proves otherwise. Another view holds the opposite: prohibition until evidence permits. The Hanafi school, however, adopts a nuanced position that distinguishes between acts of worship and worldly interactions, as we will explore.

Guided Path
1
Read the maxim
The text presents two possibilities: 'Is the original status of things permissibility or prohibition?'
2
Identify the first view
The first view is that the default is permissibility (ibahah). The text explicitly says this is the school of Imam al-Shafi'i.
Imam al-Shafi'i based this on the Quranic ayah: 'He created for you all that is in the earth' (2:29).
3
Identify the second view
The second view is that the default is prohibition (tahrim). The text says 'or prohibition until evidence indicates permissibility?' and mentions it is attributed (to someone, possibly Abu Hanifa or others).
This view is held by some scholars who prioritize caution.
4
Understand the Hanafi distinction
Although the text does not resolve the dispute, the Hanafi school's relied-upon position is a middle ground: for acts of worship (ibadat), the default is prohibition – nothing is to be done without evidence; for worldly transactions (mu'amalat), the default is permissibility.
This is a key nuance to remember when applying the maxim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة هل الأصل في الأشياء الإباحة حتى يدلّ الدليل على عدم الإباحة ـ وهو مذهب الشافعي رحمه الله - أو التحريم حتى يدلّ الدليل على الإباحة؟ ونسبه
Rule: Is the original status of things permissibility until evidence indicates non-permissibility – and this is the school of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him – or prohibition until evidence indicates permissibility? And he attributed it...
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental debate in usul al-fiqh: what is the default ruling for things (الأصل في الأشياء) that are not addressed by divine texts? The Shafi'i school holds that the default is permissibility (الإباحة), meaning that everything is considered permissible unless there is evidence (from Quran, Sunnah, etc.) to show it is prohibited. This is based on a Quranic verse (2:29) and the principle that Allah created things for human benefit. The other view, attributed here to an unnamed source, holds that the default is prohibition (التحريم), meaning that everything is considered forbidden unless there is evidence to permit it. This view is often associated with some Hanbali and Zahiri scholars. The text here does not explicitly state which view is preferred in the Hanafi school; it only mentions that the permissibility view is the Shafi'i madhhab. The phrase 'ونسبه' (attributed to) suggests that the prohibition view is attributed to someone, possibly Imam Abu Hanifa or others. In later Hanafi works, the relied-upon position is that the default in matters of worship is prohibition (nothing is to be done without evidence), while in worldly matters (mu'amalat) the default is permissibility. The passage functions as a memorization anchor for this crucial usuli rule.

Key Terms
الأصل في الأشياء الإباحة (al-aṣl fī al-ashyā’ al-ibāḥah): The default ruling for things is permissibility; a principle that everything is allowed unless proven otherwise.
التحريم (al-taḥrīm): Prohibition; the opposite of ibahah. In this context, it is the view that the default ruling is prohibition until evidence shows permissibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which school holds that the original status of things is permissibility (ibahah)?
A) The Hanafi school
B) The Shafi'i school
C) The Maliki school
D) The Hanbali school

Key Takeaway
The default ruling for things is a contested maxim; the Hanafi school resolves it by distinguishing ibadat (default prohibition) from mu'amalat (default permissibility).

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
8 min
Original Ruling of Things: Permissibility, Prohibition, or Suspension?
Compares positions on original ruling of things before revelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions on the original ruling of things before revelation and apply the Hanafi position of suspension (tawaqquf) to resolve ambiguous cases.

Core Idea
Before revelation, actions have no ruling — suspension (tawaqquf) is the Hanafi default, not prohibition.

This lesson examines a foundational qawida in Hanafi legal theory: what is the original ruling of things before revelation? The author compares three positions — permissibility (Shafi'i), prohibition (attributed to Abu Hanifa), and suspension (Hanafi majority). The practical effect emerges in cases where no clear text exists, such as ambiguous animals or unknown plants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three views: permissibility (ibaha) until evidence of prohibition (Shafi'i), prohibition (tahrim) until evidence of permissibility (attributed to Abu Hanifa by Shafi'is), and suspension (tawaqquf) — no ruling until evidence (Hanafi majority).
Also note the view of some Hanafis like al-Karkhi who hold permissibility.
2
Understand the Hanafi view of tawaqquf
According to al-Bada'i, actions before revelation have no legal ruling because the ruling's attachment is negated due to lack of benefit. This is tawaqquf: we suspend judgment.
This does not mean things are prohibited; they are simply not yet ruled upon.
3
Apply the qawida to furuat (cases)
Examine the cases given: ambiguous animals, unknown plants, river ownership, stray pigeon, giraffe. For each, determine the ruling using the Hanafi principle: since no evidence exists, the ruling is permissibility based on the qawida of ibaha (as in al-Hidayah) or suspend?
The author indicates that the Hanafi qawaid lead to permissibility for the giraffe, even though not explicitly mentioned.
4
Distinguish between ibaha, tahrim, and tawaqquf
Compare the epistemological foundations: Shafi'i ibaha derives from istishab (presumption of continuation of original permissibility); Hanafi tawaqquf avoids assigning a ruling where there is no benefit; prohibition is a minority view.
In fatwa, the relied-upon Hanafi view is permissibility (ibaha) as stated in al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة
هل الأصل في الأشياء الإباحة حتى يدلّ الدليل على عدم الإباحة ـ وهو مذهب الشافعي رحمه الله - أو التحريم حتى يدلّ الدليل على الإباحة؟ ونسبه الشافعية إلى أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وفي البدائع المختار أن لا حكم للأفعال قبل الشرع، والحكم عندنا وإن كان أزليا، فالمراد به هنا عدم تعلقه بالفعل قبل الشرع، فانتفى التعلّق لعدم فائدته (انتهى). وفي شرح المنار للمصنف : الأصل في الأشياء الإباحة عند بعض الحنفية ومنهم الكرخي، وقال بعض أصحاب الحديث : الأصل فيها الحظر. وقال أصحابنا الأصل فيها التوقف بمعنى أنه لا بد لها من حكم، لكنَّا لم نقف عليه بالفعل. (انتهى). وفي الهداية من فصل الحداد؛ أن الإباحة أصل. .(انتهى). ويظهر أثر هذا الاختلاف في المسكوت عنه، ويتخرج عليها ما أشكل حاله .
الكل .
فمنها الحيوان المشكل أمره والنبات المجهول سميته .
ومنها : إذا لم يعرف حال النهر شل هو مباح أو مملوك. ومنها : لو دخل برجه حمام وشك هل هو مباح أو مملوك.
ومنها: مسألة الزرافة؛ فمذهب الشافعي رحمه الله القائل بالإباحة، الحلّ في
وأما مسألة الزرافة فالمختار عندهم حلّ .أكلها وقال السيوطي ولم يذكرها أحد من المالكية والحنفية ؛ وقواعدهم تقتضي حلّها والله تعالى أعلم.
Rule: Is the original rule regarding things permissibility until evidence indicates otherwise – which is the school of al-Shafi'i – or prohibition until evidence indicates permissibility? The Shafi'is attribute the latter to Abu Hanifa. In al-Bada'i, the chosen view is that actions have no ruling before revelation; although the ruling with us is eternal, what is meant here is non-attachment of ruling to the action before revelation, so attachment is negated due to lack of benefit. (End). In Sharh al-Manar by the author: The original rule regarding things is permissibility according to some Hanafis, including al-Karkhi. Some hadith scholars said the original is prohibition. And our companions said the original is suspension (tawaqquf), meaning they must have a ruling, but we have not determined it yet. (End). In al-Hidayah, from the chapter of hadd, that permissibility is the original. (End). The effect of this difference appears in matters not mentioned (maskut 'anh), and from it are derived cases whose ruling is problematic. All of them. Among them: animals of ambiguous nature and plants of unknown name. Among them: if the status of a river is unknown – is it permissible or owned? Among them: if a pigeon enters his tower and he doubts whether it is permissible or owned. Among them: the issue of the giraffe; the Shafi'i school says permissibility, so it is halal. As for the giraffe, the chosen view in our school is that eating it is halal. Al-Suyuti said none of the Malikis or Hanafis mentioned it, but their principles dictate its permissibility. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines a well-known qawida: the original ruling of things (pre-revelation or in the absence of revealed evidence) is a matter of dispute. The author presents three main positions: 1) Imam al-Shafi'i holds that things are originally permissible until evidence of prohibition; 2) some attribute to Abu Hanifa the view that things are originally prohibited until evidence of permissibility; 3) the Hanafi view according to al-Bada'i is that there is no ruling for actions before revelation (the ruling is suspended). In Sharh al-Manar, it is mentioned that some Hanafis (like al-Karkhi) hold permissibility, while some hadith scholars hold prohibition, and the majority of Hanafis (our companions) hold suspension (tawaqquf). Then he quotes al-Hidayah that permissibility is the original. The practical difference emerges in cases where the ruling is unclear, such as ambiguous animals, unknown plants, whether a river is public or private, whether a pigeon in one's tower is owned or free, and the famous case of eating giraffe. The Shafi'is rule it permissible, and although the Hanafis did not explicitly mention it, their principles (qawaid) indicate its permissibility. This lesson is a qawida_definition, clarifying the scope and application of the maxim about the original ruling of things.

Key Terms
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permissibility: the ruling that an action may be done without reward or punishment.
التحريم (al-tahrim): Prohibition: the ruling that an action is forbidden.
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Suspension: refraining from assigning a ruling until evidence is found.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view attributed to Abu Hanifa by the Shafi'is?
A) Original permissibility
B) Original prohibition
C) Original suspension
D) No original ruling

Key Takeaway
The foundational qawida in Hanafi fatwa is that the original ruling of things is permissibility, not prohibition, unless textual evidence indicates otherwise.

Original Prohibition in Sexual Matters (Qawida: al-Asl fi al-Abda' al-Tahrim)
Establishes a qawida and applies it to furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi qawida on original prohibition in sexual matters and apply it to cases of uncertainty, such as forgotten manumission or divorce.

Core Idea
In sexual matters, prohibition is the default; uncertainty maintains it.

This lesson introduces a key legal maxim: 'al-asl fi al-abda' al-tahrim' — the original rule regarding private parts (sexual relations) is prohibition. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti when dealing with cases where permissibility and prohibition conflict. The passage from Ashbah wa al-Nazair explains the principle and applies it to specific scenarios involving manumission and divorce, illustrating why personal reasoning (taharri) is not allowed in such matters.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its meaning
Begin by articulating the qawida: 'al-asl fi al-abda' al-tahrim.' Explain that 'abda'' refers to the private parts and by extension all sexual relations. The original ruling is prohibition, meaning any sexual contact is presumed unlawful unless there is clear evidence from the Shari'a to permit it (e.g., valid marriage or concubinage).
Note the quote from Kashf al-Asrar that even marriage is originally prohibited but permitted by necessity.
2
Identify the dabt (restriction) and its implication
The passage states that when permissibility and prohibition coincide, prohibition overrides. This means that even a mere possibility of prohibition blocks access. Consequently, ijtihad (personal reasoning or taharri) is not allowed in sexual matters; one must avoid until certainty is achieved.
This contrasts with areas like direction of qibla where taharri is allowed.
3
Apply the qawida to the first furu': forgotten manumission
Consider a man with four slave girls who frees one but forgets which. Because the original rule is prohibition, he cannot make ijtihad to determine which is free. He must refrain from all of them until the freed one is identified, and the judge also cannot permit him.
The judge's role is to prevent until clarity.
4
Apply the qawida to the second furu': forgotten triple divorce
Similarly, if a man divorces one of his wives with three talaq and forgets which, he cannot approach any of them. Even if he distinguishes all but one, he cannot approach that one until he knows for sure she is not the divorced one. The judge administers an oath before allowing.
The oath does not substitute for certainty; if he later becomes uncertain, he must still refrain.
5
Analyze the return case and the persistent prohibition
If in the manumission case the man sells three slave girls and the judge rules that the remaining one is the freed woman, and then some of what he sold returns to him, he still cannot have intercourse with her because the original prohibition remains due to the prior uncertainty that was never resolved with certainty.
This shows that the maxim is applied strictly, and even a judicial determination based on probability does not override the original prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة :
الأصل في الأبضاع التحريم ولذا قال في كشف الأسرار شرح فخر الإسلام: الأصل في النكاح الحظر، وأبيح للضرورة (انتهى). فإذا تقابل في المرأة حلّ وحرمة غلبت الحرمة، ولهذا لا يجوز التحرِّي في الفروج. وفي كافي الحاكم الشهيد من باب التحري ولو أن رجلاً له أربع جواري أعتق واحدة منهنَّ بعينها، ثم نسيها فلم يدر أيتهنَّ أعتق لم يسعه أن يتحرى للوطء ولا للبيع، ولا يسع الحاكم أن يخلي بينه وبينهنَّ حتى يبين المعتقة من غيرها، وكذلك إذا طلق إحدى نسائه بعينها ثلاثا ثم نسيها، وكذلك إن ميَّز كلهنَّ إلا واحدة؛ لم يسعه أن يقربها حتى يعلم أنها غير المطلقة وكذلك يمنعه القاضي عنها حتى يخبر أنها غير المطلقة، فإذا أخبر بذلك استحلفه ألبتة أنه ما طلق هذه بعينها ثلاثا ثم خلى بينهما ؛ فإن كان حلف وهو جاهل بها فلا ينبغي له أن يقربها فإن باع في المسألة الأولى ثلاثا من الجواري فحكم الحاكم؛ فإن أجاز بيعهنَّ وكان ذلك من رأيه وجعل الباقية المعتقة، ثم رجع إليه بعض ما باع بشراء أو هبة أو ميراث لم ينبغ له أن يطأها هي
Rule: The original rule regarding private parts (sexual relations) is prohibition. Hence it is said in Kashf al-Asrar, commentary on Fakhral-Islam: The original ruling in marriage is prohibition, and it becomes permissible by necessity. (End). Therefore, if for a woman there coexists permissibility and prohibition, the prohibition overrides. For this reason, ijtihad (personal reasoning) is not allowed regarding private parts. In Kafi al-Hakim al-Shahid from the chapter of ijtihad: If a man has four slave girls and he manumits one specific one, then forgets which one, and does not know which he freed, he is not permitted to make ijtihad for intercourse or sale, nor may the judge allow him to consort with them until he identifies the manumitted one from the others. Similarly, if he divorces one of his wives specifically with three divorces and then forgets which, and similarly if he distinguishes all except one, he may not approach her until he knows she is not the divorced one; the judge prevents him from her until he informs that she is not the divorced one. If he so informs, the judge administers an oath that he did not divorce this specific one three times, then lets him be with her. But if he swore while ignorant of her, he should not approach her. If in the first issue he sells three of the slave girls and the judge rules, if he validates the sales and from his opinion determines the remaining one is the manumitted, then if some of what was sold returns to him by purchase, gift, or inheritance, he should not have intercourse with her.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage establishes a fundamental qawida (maxim): the original ruling regarding sexual relations (abda') is prohibition (tahrim). This means that any sexual contact is presumed forbidden unless there is clear evidence of permissibility (nikah, ownership, etc.). The author quotes from Kashf al-Asrar that even marriage (nikah) is originally prohibited but permitted out of necessity (for procreation). Therefore, when there is a conflict between permissibility and prohibition concerning a woman, prohibition prevails. Hence, one cannot resort to ijtihad (personal reasoning) in matters of sexual access when there is uncertainty. The passage then presents a detailed case from Kafi al-Hakim al-Shahid: a man has four slave girls and frees one specific one but forgets which. He cannot engage in ijtihad to determine which one is free; he must refrain from all of them until the identity is resolved. The judge also cannot allow him to associate with them. Similarly, if he divorces one of his wives with triple talaq and forgets which, he cannot approach any until he knows who is not divorced. If he later claims he knows, the judge administers an oath and then permits. If he sells three of the slave girls and the judge rules that the remaining one is the freed one, then if that sold one returns to his possession, he still cannot have intercourse with her because the prohibition is based on the original uncertainty. This case illustrates how the qawida is applied: original prohibition is not easily overridden by mere probability.

Key Terms
الأبضاع (al-abda'): Private parts; by extension, sexual relations and matters of intimacy.
الحظر (al-hazr): Prohibition; synonym of tahrim in this context.
التحري (al-taharri): Ijtihad or personal reasoning to determine the correct ruling in cases of uncertainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a man who freed one of his four slave girls and forgot which not allowed to make ijtihad (taharri) to determine which one is free?
A) Because ijtihad is only for legal experts.
B) Because the original rule in sexual matters is prohibition, and uncertainty maintains that prohibition.
C) Because the slave girls have a right to be released.
D) Because he is not a judge.

Key Takeaway
The original prohibition in sexual matters means uncertainty maintains the prohibition; ijtihad is not allowed.

The Original Ruling of Things: Suspension, Permissibility, or Prohibition
Discussion of the ikhtilaf on the default ruling for unaddressed matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on the default ruling for unaddressed matters (maskan 'anhu), apply the Hanafi qawida of tawaqquf to new cases, and evaluate the practical difference between the positions.

Core Idea
Before revelation, the default is not prohibition or permissibility, but suspension of judgment until evidence is found.

This passage addresses a foundational legal maxim: what is the original state of things that have not been addressed by divine revelation? Three positions exist: permissibility (ibaha), prohibition (hadhr), and suspension (tawaqquf). The Hanafi school predominantly holds suspension, meaning we do not assign a ruling without evidence. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its practical applications.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Understand that the maxim concerns things not addressed by revelation (al-maskan 'anhu).
This is a meta-legal principle that governs all fiqh rulings.
2
Identify the three positions
Learn the three views: ibaha (Shafi'i and some Hanafis), hadhr (some hadith scholars), and tawaqquf (majority of Hanafis).
The preferred Hanafi position is tawaqquf.
3
Note the Hanafi internal ikhtilaf
Some Hanafis (e.g., al-Karkhi) hold ibaha, but the majority and mufta bihi is tawaqquf.
This internal disagreement is important for fatwa.
4
Examine the furuat (applications)
Work through the examples provided: ambiguous animal, unknown plant, river of unknown ownership, stray pigeon, and giraffe. For each, determine the ruling based on tawaqquf.
Note that the giraffe is a famous example where the Shafi'i school follows ibaha.
5
Apply the maxim to a new case
Consider a modern scenario (e.g., a synthetic food additive with no clear evidence). Apply tawaqquf and identify what evidence would change the ruling.
This demonstrates the practical relevance of the maxim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشافعية إلى أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وفي البدائع المختار أن لا حكم للأفعال قبل الشرع، والحكم عندنا وإن كان أزليا، فالمراد به هنا عدم تعلقه بالفعل قبل الشرع، فانتفى التعلّق لعدم فائدته (انتهى). وفي شرح المنار للمصنف : الأصل في الأشياء الإباحة عند بعض الحنفية ومنهم الكرخي، وقال بعض أصحاب الحديث : الأصل فيها الحظر. وقال أصحابنا الأصل فيها التوقف بمعنى أنه لا بد لها من حكم، لكنَّا لم نقف عليه بالفعل. (انتهى). وفي الهداية من فصل الحداد؛ أن الإباحة أصل. (انتهى). ويظهر أثر هذا الاختلاف في المسكوت عنه، ويتخرج عليها ما أشكل حاله. الكل. فمنها الحيوان المشكل أمره والنبات المجهول سميته. ومنها : إذا لم يعرف حال النهر شل هو مباح أو مملوك. ومنها : لو دخل برجه حمام وشك هل هو مباح أو مملوك. ومنها: مسألة الزرافة؛ فمذهب الشافعي رحمه الله القائل بالإباحة، الحلّ في وأما مسألة الزرافة فالمختار عندهم حلّ .أكلها وقال السيوطي ولم يذكرها أحد من المالكية والحنفية ؛ وقواعدهم تقتضي حلّها والله تعالى أعلم.
The Shafi'is [attribute] to Abu Hanifa. In al-Bada'i: the chosen view is that actions have no ruling before revelation, and the ruling with us is eternal, but what is meant here is its non-attachment to the action before revelation, so the attachment is negated due to lack of benefit. In Sharh al-Manar by the author: The original ruling regarding things is permissibility according to some Hanafis, among them al-Karkhi. Some hadith scholars said: The original ruling is prohibition. Our companions said: The original ruling is suspension, meaning that a ruling is necessary for them, but we have not actually come upon it. In al-Hidayah, from the chapter of al-Haddad: Permissibility is the original. The effect of this difference appears in matters that are not spoken about, and that which is ambiguous can be derived from it. Entirely. Among them: an animal of uncertain nature and a plant of unknown identity. Among them: when the state of a river is not known whether it is permissible or owned. Among them: if a pigeon enters his tower and he doubts whether it is permissible or owned. Among them: the issue of the giraffe; the Shafi'i school, which holds permissibility, [says] it is permissible to eat. As for the giraffe issue, the chosen view with them is its permissibility. Al-Suyuti said: None of the Malikis or Hanafis have mentioned it; their principles dictate its permissibility. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a fundamental usuli maxim: what is the default ruling for things not addressed by divine revelation? The author presents three positions: (1) Permissibility (ibaha) — attributed to some Hanafis like al-Karkhi, and also stated in al-Hidayah from al-Haddad. (2) Prohibition (hadhr) — the view of some hadith scholars. (3) Suspension (tawaqquf) — the preferred Hanafi view as per Sharh al-Manar, meaning we acknowledge that a ruling must exist, but we do not know it definitively. The practical impact of this difference is seen in cases where the shari'ah is silent (maskan 'anhu). The author then gives examples: an animal whose nature is unclear, an unknown plant, a river whose ownership is unknown, a stray pigeon, and the giraffe. The giraffe is a famous example: the Shafi'is rule it permissible based on their principle of ibaha, while the Hanafis and Malikis have not explicitly stated a ruling, but their principles also imply permissibility. The author concludes with 'Allah knows best.' Note that the first part about 'actions before revelation' is a preliminary discussion; the core maxim here is the disagreement on the original state of things.

Key Terms
الأصل في الأشياء (al-asl fi al-ashya'): The initial legal presumption regarding material objects and actions prior to revelation addressing them

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the original ruling concerning things according to the preferred Hanafi view?
A) Permissibility (ibaha)
B) Prohibition (hadhr)
C) Suspension (tawaqquf)
D) All are valid with no preference

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's default for unaddressed matters is suspension (tawaqquf), not permissibility or prohibition.

Certainty Required for Prohibition in Breastfeeding Doubts
Clarifying that the rule of caution applies only when prohibition is certain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that certainty is required for prohibition in breastfeeding and identify when doubt prevents legal consequences.

Core Idea
Certainty is required to establish prohibition; doubt prevents the legal effect of breastfeeding.

In this lesson, we examine a key legal maxim: the rule of caution only applies when the basis for prohibition is certain. The Hanafi school requires certainty that breastfeeding actually occurred before declaring a marriage prohibited. We will explore several cases from later fatwa works that illustrate this principle and its practical application in marriage rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida
Identify the maxim: the rule of caution only applies when there is a confirmed cause of prohibition. If there is doubt, the prohibition is not established.
This is a sub-maxim of 'Certainty is not removed by doubt'.
2
Analyze the first scenario
Consider the case where a woman puts her nipple in a baby's mouth but it is uncertain whether milk reached the stomach. The ruling: no prohibition because of doubt.
Al-Walwalijiyya source.
3
Analyze the second scenario
A woman known to have breastfed a girl later claims she had no milk. Her statement is accepted, so her son may marry the girl.
Al-Qunyah source: the woman's claim is credible because the basis for prohibition is not confirmed.
4
Analyze the third scenario
A young boy and girl with mere suspicion of breastfeeding: marriage is permissible unless a reliable witness reports the breastfeeding.
Al-Khaniyya: suspicion does not prohibit; only reliable testimony establishes prohibition.
5
Consider the precaution after marriage
If reliable news of breastfeeding comes after marriage, the precaution is separation, indicating that certainty after the fact overrides the initial presumption.
This shows the weight of reliable testimony even after marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن هذه القاعدة إنما هي فيما إذا كان في المرأة سبب محقق للحرمة، فلو كان في الحرمة شك لم يعتبر; ولذا قالوا لو أدخلت المرأة حلمة ثديها في فم رضيعة ووقع الشك في وصول اللبن إلى جوفها لم تحرم لأن في المانع شكا، كما في الولوالجية. وفي القنية: امرأة كانت تعطي ثديها صبية واشتهر ذلك فيما بينهم، ثم تقول لم يكن في ثديي لبن حين ألقمتها ثديي ولم يعلم ذلك إلا من جهتها; جاز لابنها أن يتزوج بهذه الصبية .(انتهى). وفي الخانية صغير وصغيرة بينهما شبهة الرضاع ولا يعلم ذلك حقيقة; قالوا لا بأس بالنكاح بينهما هذا إذا لم يخبر بذلك أحد، فإن أخبر به عدل ثقة يأخذ بقوله ولا يجوز النكاح بينهما، وإن كان الخبر بعد النكاح وهما كبيران فالأحوط أن يفارقها .
Then know that this rule only applies when there is a confirmed cause of prohibition regarding the woman. If there is doubt about the prohibition, it is not considered. Therefore, they said: If a woman inserts her nipple into the mouth of an infant girl and doubt occurs about the milk reaching her stomach, she does not become prohibited because there is doubt about the preventing factor, as in al-Walwalijiyya. In al-Qunyah: A woman who used to give her breast to a girl and this became known among them, then she says, 'There was no milk in my breast when I put my breast in her mouth,' and that is only known from her side; it is permissible for her son to marry this girl. (End). And in al-Khaniyya: A young boy and a young girl between whom there is suspicion of breastfeeding, and they do not know the reality; they said: There is no harm in marriage between them, provided that no one informs about it. If a reliable trustworthy person informs about it, his statement is taken and marriage is not permissible between them. If the news comes after the marriage and they are both adults, the precaution is that he separates from her.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage explains that the earlier rule of caution applies only when the cause of prohibition is confirmed. If there is doubt about the actual occurrence of breastfeeding, the prohibition is not established. For example, if a woman puts her nipple in a baby's mouth but it is uncertain whether milk entered the baby's stomach, the baby is not considered a foster child. Similarly, if a woman who was known to breastfeed a girl later claims she had no milk, her statement is accepted regarding her own son marrying that girl. From al-Khaniyya: if there is mere suspicion of breastfeeding between a boy and a girl without certainty, marriage is permissible unless a reliable person reports the breastfeeding. If such a report comes after marriage, the recommended precaution is separation. This shows that the Hanafi school requires certainty for prohibition in marriage ties.

Key Terms
الرضاع (al-rada'): Breastfeeding that creates a foster relationship prohibiting marriage.
سبب محقق للحرمة (sabab muhaqqaq li-l-hurmah): Confirmed cause of prohibition, i.e., certainty that breastfeeding occurred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The baby becomes a foster child
B) The baby does not become a foster child
C) It is recommended to avoid marriage precautionarily
D) The mother must be questioned

Key Takeaway
Certainty is required to establish prohibition; doubt prevents the legal effect of breastfeeding in marriage rulings.

The Maxim: Original in Speech is Literal Meaning
Legal maxim with applied branch on nikah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة', explain its rank as a wusta qawida, and apply it to the interpretation of 'nikah' in Quran 4:22 to derive the ruling on the father's concubine.

Core Idea
Words default to literal meaning; only context shifts to metaphor.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir introduces a cornerstone of Hanafi legal hermeneutics: the presumption that words carry their real (haqiqi) meaning. This maxim governs all legal interpretation, from the Quran to everyday contracts. By examining its application to the word 'nikah' in the verse on marriage to paternal relations, you will see how a single linguistic presumption shapes an entire fiqh ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim and Its Rank
State the Arabic maxim: 'الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة'. Note that it is a wusta (intermediate) qawida, not a kubra (major) because it is not universal but applies to most speech.
The maxim is a default presumption, not an absolute rule.
2
Understand the Default: Haqiqa
Haqiqa is the literal meaning established by original linguistic usage. Unless there is a qarina (contextual indicator) pointing to majaz (metaphor), the literal meaning is binding.
Hanafi usul distinguishes haqiqa from majaz; the burden of proof lies on the one claiming majaz.
3
Apply to the Branch: 'Nikah' as Intercourse
The author states that 'nikah' literally means intercourse (wat'). Thus, in Quran 4:22, the prohibition on marrying 'what your fathers married' is interpreted as forbidding intercourse with the father's female slave, just as it forbids marriage to his wife.
This relies on the linguistic presumption that the term is used in its real sense.
4
Trace the Furu: Prohibition of the Father's Concubine
The ruling derived: the father's concubine (mazniyya) is haram for the son to marry or have intercourse with, exactly like the father's wife. This is because 'nikah' in the verse means intercourse, not contract.
The Shafi'i school, interpreting 'nikah' as contract, may permit the concubine.
5
Note the Dispute: Shafi'i Difference
The passage hints that a Shafi'i judge may rule for permissibility. This highlights how a core usul difference leads to divergent fiqh rulings. The Hanafi position relies on the maxim 'الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة'.
Both positions are valid within their respective schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة: الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة وعلى ذلك فروع كثيرة. منها : النكاح للوطء، وعليه حمل قوله تعالى : ولا تَنْكِحُوا مَا نَكَحَ آبَاؤُكُم من النساء [النساء : ۲۲] . فحرمت مزنية الأب كحليلته ولذا لو قضى شافعي بحلها لم
Principle: The original in speech is the literal meaning, and upon this are many branches. Among them: marriage is for intercourse, and upon this is carried the statement of Allah Most High: 'And do not marry those whom your fathers married among women' (al-Nisa 4:22). So the father's concubine is prohibited like his wife. Hence, if a Shafi'i judge rules for its permissibility, ...
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage introduces a legal maxim: 'The original in speech is its literal meaning.' This means that when a word is used, it is presumed to mean its real (haqiqi) meaning unless there is evidence for a metaphorical meaning. The author gives a branch: the word 'nikah' (marriage) is literally used for intercourse (wat'). Therefore, the Quranic verse forbidding marriage of those whom your fathers married is interpreted as forbidding intercourse with the father's female slave (mazniyya) just as his wife is forbidden. The passage cuts off, but it likely continues to discuss the difference with the Shafi'i school, which may interpret 'nikah' differently (as contract). The Hanafi school holds that the prohibition extends to the father's concubine because 'nikah' in the verse refers to intercourse.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqa): The literal or real meaning of a word, as opposed to metaphorical (majaz).
النكاح (al-nikah): Marriage; in Hanafi usul, the primary literal meaning is intercourse (wat').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The marriage contract
B) Intercourse
C) Dowry
D) Divorce

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة' requires words to be interpreted literally unless clear context indicates otherwise, as illustrated by the interpretation of 'nikah' as intercourse in Quran 4:22.

Applications of the Maxim 'Certainty is Not Removed by Doubt'
A conclusion listing rulings where the maxim is applied.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' to cases of doubt in taharah, salah, and sayd, identifying when the maxim governs and when exceptional caution is required.

Core Idea
Doubt does not disturb an established certainty, but where certainty is absent, caution prevails.

This passage from Ashbah wa al-Nazhair presents a conclusion listing several practical applications of the cardinal Hanafi maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt.' Each case illustrates a situation where a person faces doubt yet the original certainty (whether of purity, obligation, or prohibition) remains intact. As a mufti, you must recognize when doubt is irrelevant and when it necessitates precaution—a distinction that depends on the presence or absence of an established prior certainty.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Restate the qawida: اليقين لا يزول بالشك. Identify that it applies across all fiqh chapters whenever an established certainty exists.
The maxim is one of the five major qawaid al-kubra.
2
Categorize the cases by fiqh chapter
Group each issue listed: taharah (mustahadah, wetness, dead mouse, cat), salah (doubt about takbir, hadath, traveler status), sayd (game), and najasah (garment). Determine which have an established prior certainty.
Some cases involve a known prior state (e.g., water was pure, garment was clean).
3
Apply the maxim to each case
For each, explain how the maxim preserves the original ruling: e.g., the mustahadah's certainty of impurity remains despite doubt about the end of hayd; the traveler's original status remains despite doubt about residence.
The maxim maintains the status quo ante.
4
Identify where the maxim is overridden by istihsan or ikhtilaf
Note the cat case: Abu Hanifa's view uses istihsan (possibility of cleaning by saliva) rather than the maxim. Also note the sahib al-udhr case: the maxim applies to maintain excuse status unless doubt is about cessation.
In the cat case, Abu Hanifa prioritizes a possibility over the apparent impurity.
5
Determine the mufta bihi for each disputed point
Extract from the text: for mustahadah, 'al-sahih' is the relied view; for game, 'al-mu'tamad al-awwal' is the first condition; for cat, no tarjih given, so both views exist.
The mufta bihi may vary by region or later tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في خاتمة تشمل على فوائد قاعدة اليقين لا يزول بالشك) الفائدة الأولى : تشتمل على مسائل : الشك . ١ - المستحاضة المتحيرة يلزمها الاغتسال لكل صلاة وهو الصحيح. ۲ - إذا وجد بللاً ولا يدري أنه مني أو مذي ؛ قدمنا إيجاب الغسل مع وجود الشك. ٣ - وجد فأرة ميتة ولم يدر متى وقعت وكان قد توضأ منها؛ قدمنا الإعادة عليه مفصلاً. ٤ - قدمنا أنه لو شك هل كبر للافتتاح أو لا، أو أحدث أو لا، وكان أول ما عرض له استقبل. ٥ - أصابت ثوبه نجاسة ولا يدري أي موضع أصابته؛ غسل الكل على ما عن الظهيرية مع ما فيه من الاختلاف . ٦ - رمى صيدا فجرحه ثم تغيب عن بصره ثم وجده ميتا ولا يدري سبب موته؛ يحرم مع وجود الشك، لكن شرط في الكنز لحرمته أن يقعد عن طلبه وشرط قاضي خان أن يتوارى عن بصره وإليه يشير ما في الهداية والمعتمد الأول. ٧ - لو أكلت الهرة فأرة ؛ قالوا إن شربت على فورها الماء يتنجس كشارب الخمر إذا شرب الماء على فوره ولو مكنت ساعة ثم شربت لا يتنجس عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لاحتمال غسلها فمها بلعابها. وعند محمد رحمه الله يتنجس بناء على أصله من أنها لا تزول إلا بالمطلق كالحكمية. وهنا مسائل تحتاج إلى المراجعة ولم أرها الآن. منها شك مسافر أوصل بلده أو لا؟ ومنها: شك مسافر هل نوى الإقامة أو لا؟ وينبغي أن لا يجوز له الترخص بالشك. ثم رأيت في التاتارخانية : ولو شك في الصلاة أمقيم أو مسافر؛ صلى أربعًا ويقعد على الثانية احتياطا فكذلك إذا شك في نية الإقامة. ومنها: صاحب العذر إذا شك في انقطاعه فصلى بطهارته، ينبغي أن لا تصح .
In a conclusion that includes benefits of the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt': The first benefit includes issues of doubt. 1. A mustahadah (woman with irregular bleeding) who is confused (mutahayyirah) is obliged to perform ghusl for every prayer, and this is the correct view. 2. If one finds wetness and does not know whether it is mani (semen) or madhi (prostatic fluid), we have previously obligated ghusl despite the doubt. 3. If one finds a dead mouse and does not know when it fell, and had made wudu from that water, we have previously detailed the obligation of repetition. 4. We have previously stated that if he doubts whether he said the takbirat al-ihram or not, or whether he nullified his wudu or not, and it was the first time such doubt occurred, he should start over. 5. If his garment is hit by impurity and he does not know which spot, he should wash the whole garment, according to al-Zahiriyyah, despite the difference of opinion in it. 6. If one shoots at game and wounds it, then it disappears from his sight, then he finds it dead and does not know the cause of death, it is forbidden (to eat) due to doubt. However, al-Kanz conditions for its prohibition that he desists from searching for it, and Qadi Khan conditions that it disappears from his sight, and what is in al-Hidayah points to it, and the first is relied upon. 7. If a cat eats a mouse, they said that if it drinks water immediately after, the water becomes impure, like a wine drinker who drinks water immediately after; but if it waits an hour then drinks, it does not become impure according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) due to the possibility that it cleaned its mouth with its saliva. According to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), it becomes impure based on his principle that (impurity) does not go away except with pure water (mutlaq) like a legal impurity. And here are some issues that need review and I have not seen them now: among them, the doubt of a traveler whether he has arrived at his town or not; among them, the doubt of a traveler whether he intended residence or not; it is not permissible for him to avail the concessions (rukhas) due to doubt. Then I saw in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: If one doubts in prayer whether he is a resident or a traveler, he prays four rak'ats and sits after the second as a precaution; likewise, if he doubts the intention of residence. And among them: If a person with an excuse (sahib al-udhr) doubts whether his excuse has ceased, and prays with his purification, it should not be valid.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a conclusion (khatimah) with benefits from the famous maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' (اليقين لا يزول بالشك). The author lists several masail where doubt does not override existing certainty. 1. A mustahadah (woman with istihadah) who cannot distinguish her habit is considered in a state of doubt; she must perform ghusl for each prayer, as certainty of purity is required. 2. Finding wetness without knowing its type: since semen requires ghusl and madhi does not, the doubt over the type does not remove the original certainty of cleanliness? Actually the text says 'obligated ghusl' meaning doubt leads to the stricter ruling? Wait: The maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt' typically means if you were certain of purity, doubt does not make you impure. But here the text says 'we obligated ghusl with the doubt' — this seems to be an exception: the original state is uncertain, so they impose ghusl. However, the passage is about the maxim, so perhaps the example is that the doubt does not remove the obligation of caution? Actually the first example: mustahadah mutahayyirah — she has certainty of being in a state of impurity due to blood, doubt about when her period ends, so she remains in the state of impurity? The maxim applies to maintain the status quo. For the wetness example: if one is certain of having discharged something, doubt about type does not remove the need for ghusl? This requires careful review. The commentary should not go beyond the text. 3. Finding a dead mouse in water used for wudu: if the time of fall is unknown, the original state was purity, so wudu is valid? But the text says 'we have detailed the obligation of repetition' — meaning doubt over when it fell leads to repeating wudu? Again, might be an exception. 4. Doubt about takbir or hadath: if it's the first time such doubt occurs, one must start over as a precaution. 5. Najasah on garment: wash all because the location is uncertain. 6. Game: if cause of death is unknown, it is haram due to doubt, but with conditions. 7. Cat eating mouse and drinking water: Abu Hanifa says water remains pure if the cat waited, due to possibility of cleaning by saliva; Muhammad says it becomes impure. The traveler's doubts: if a traveler doubts whether he reached his destination or intended residence, he cannot avail rukhas (e.g., Qasr) because doubt does not remove the original status of being a traveler? Actually the text says he should not avail concessions due to doubt; then a reference from Tatarkhaniyyah says if in prayer he doubts whether he is resident or traveler, he prays four and sits after two as precaution. And for a person with a valid excuse (e.g., urinary incontinence) who doubts whether his excuse has ceased, his prayer with that purification is invalid because the doubt does not remove the excuse status.

Key Terms
المستحاضة المتحيرة (al-mustahadah al-mutahayyirah): A woman with istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding) who cannot distinguish between menstrual and non-menstrual blood due to forgetfulness or confusion.
صاحب العذر (sahib al-udhr): A person with a valid excuse, such as continuous urine leakage or istihadah, who makes wudu for each prayer time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The certainty of the original state persists in the face of doubt, except where the original state itself is uncertain or where istihsan provides a different ruling.

Shakk, Zann, and Wahm: Hanafi Epistemology for Fatwa
Clarifying terminology for doubt, preponderant belief, and weak belief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish shakk, zann, wahm, and ghalabat al-zann, and state the legal effect of each in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, only yaqin and ghalabat al-zann carry binding legal force; ordinary zann is treated as doubt.

The second benefit in the text defines three key epistemological terms used by Hanafi jurists: shakk (doubt), zann (preponderant belief), and wahm (illusion). Crucially, the author explains that in Hanafi legal usage, the term zann is often treated as a form of doubt (shakk) unless it reaches the level of ghalabat al-zann (overwhelming preponderant belief), which is then annexed to certainty. This distinction has direct consequences in rulings on iqrar, divorce, and nullifiers of wudu.

Guided Path
1
Define each term from the text
Identify shakk as equal sides of existence and non-existence, zann as preponderant side toward correctness, wahm as preponderant side toward error, and ghalabat al-zann as the preponderant side when the heart adopts it.
2
Recognize the Hanafi technical usage of 'zann'
Note that Hanafis treat ordinary zann as a type of shakk (hesitation) even though one side preponderates. This is a key technical departure from linguistic usage.
See the statement: 'الظن عند الفقهاء من قبيل الشك'
3
Identify 'ghalabat al-zann' and its legal weight
Ghalabat al-zann is annexed to certainty and forms the basis of rulings. The text gives examples from wudu nullifiers and divorce.
Examples: 'غالب الظن ملحق باليقين'
4
Apply the definitions to specific fiqh cases
Explain the ruling in iqrar (ordinary zann not binding), divorce (only ghalabat al-zann effects), and wudu nullifiers (preponderant belief treated as actual nullification).
5
Formulate the underlying qa'ida
Extract the maxim 'Ghalabat al-zann is annexed to certainty' and identify its scope and exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة الثانية : الشك تساوي الطرفين والظن الطرف الراجح، وهو ترجيح جهة الصواب، والوهم رجحان جهة الخطأ وأما أكبر الرأي وغالب الظن فهو الطرف الراجح إذا أخذ به القلب وهو المعتبر عند الفقهاء كما ذكره اللامشي في أصوله، وحاصله أن الظن عند الفقهاء من قبيل الشك لأنهم يريدون به التردّد بين وجود الشيء وعدمه سواء استويا أو ترجح أحدهما، وكذا قالوا في كتاب الإقرار لو قال له: علي ألف درهم في ظني. لا يلزمه شيء لأنه للشك . (انتهى). وغالب الظن عندهم ملحق باليقين وهو الذي يبتني عليه الأحكام يعرف ذلك من تصفّح كلامهم في الأبواب؛ صرحوا في نواقض الوضوء بأن الغالب كالمتحقق، وصرَّحوا في الطلاق بأنه إذا ظن الوقوع لم يقع وإذا غلب على ظنه وقع.
The second benefit: Shakk is equality of the two sides, and zann is the preponderant side, which is preferring the side of correctness, and wahm is preponderance of the side of error. As for 'akbar al-ra'y' and 'ghalabat al-zann', it is the preponderant side when the heart adopts it, and it is what is considered by the jurists, as al-Lamishi mentioned in his Usul. The sum of it is that zann in the view of the jurists is of the category of shakk, because they intend by it hesitation between the existence of a thing and its non-existence, whether the two sides are equal or one preponderates. Similarly, they said in the Book of Iqrār: If someone says 'I owe a thousand dirhams in my zann', nothing is incumbent upon him because it is (considered) shakk. (End). And 'ghalabat al-zann' in their view is annexed to certainty, and it is upon which rulings are founded. That is known from perusing their speech in the chapters; they explicitly stated in the nullifiers of wudu that the preponderant is like the confirmed, and they explicitly stated in divorce that if he merely zann (thinks) that it occurred, it does not occur, but if his zann is preponderant (ghalab  'ala zannih), it does occur.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definitions of key epistemological terms used in Hanafi usul. The author explains that 'shakk' (doubt) is equal probability of existence and non-existence. 'Zann' (belief) is when one side is preponderant, specifically the side of correctness. 'Wahm' (illusion) is preponderance of error. 'Akbar al-ra'y' or 'ghalabat al-zann' is the same as preponderant belief when it settles in the heart. Crucially, the author notes that in Hanafi fiqh, 'zann' is often treated as a type of shakk—it implies hesitation, even if one side outweighs the other. Evidence from the Book of Iqrār shows that saying 'in my zann I owe' does not create liability. However, 'ghalabat al-zann' is stronger and is treated as certainty for legal purposes: for example, in wudu nullifiers, the preponderant belief is treated as actual nullification, and in divorce, mere zann (non-preponderant) does not effect divorce, but ghalabat al-zann does. This distinction is important for fatwa rulings, as actions often turn on certainty or preponderant certainty.

Key Terms
الظن (al-zann): Preponderant belief towards correctness, but still considered a type of shakk in Hanafi usage.
غالب الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Overwhelming belief that is acted upon; annexed to certainty.
الوهم (al-wahm): Preponderant belief towards error.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the legal effect of a person saying 'I owe a thousand dirhams in my zann'?
A) He becomes liable for a thousand dirhams.
B) He is not liable because zann is considered shakk.
C) He becomes liable only if he says 'ghalabat al-zann'.
D) The qadi will decide.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, legal rulings are built on certainty or ghalabat al-zann; ordinary zann and less are considered doubt.

Istishab: Definition and Evidentiary Authority
Defining istishab and discussing its evidentiary weight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define istishab, explain the Hanafi position that it is valid for daf' only, and apply it to a pre-emption case.

Core Idea
Istishab preserves the status quo: it repels claims but cannot establish new rights.

Istishab (presumption of continuity) is a foundational legal maxim in Hanafi usul. This passage defines it and then explores its evidentiary weight (hujjiyya). While some schools accept it broadly, the Hanafi school, as refined by later masters like Abu Zayd and Shams al-A'imma, holds that istishab is valid only for repelling claims (daf'), not for establishing rights (istihqaq). The passage also presents a stronger view denying it altogether. Understanding this dispute is critical for muftis when applying istishab to real cases.

Guided Path
1
Capture the definition
Read the definition: 'الحكم ببقاء أمر محقق لم يظن عدمه'. Identify the two conditions: confirmed past existence and no suspicion of change.
2
Map the scholarly positions
Note the three views: (a) absolute hujja, (b) rejected entirely, (c) the Hanafi mufta bihi: hujja for daf' only.
The three eminent Hanafi scholars are Abu Zayd, Shams al-A'imma, and Fakhr al-Islam.
3
Understand the reasoning for daf' vs istihqaq
The Hanafi argument: original non-existence (al-'adam al-asli) is presumed to continue; existence does not imply continued existence. Thus, istishab can only repel a claim that would change the status quo, not vindicate a new claim.
4
Apply the principle: pre-emption case
If a partner claims shuf'a but the buyer denies his ownership, the buyer's statement is accepted based on istishab (original non-ownership), unless the partner brings evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة الثالثة : الاستصحاب؛ وهو كما في التحرير : الحكم ببقاء أمر محقق لم يظن عدمه واختلف في حجيته؛ فقيل حجة مطلقا ونفاه كثير مطلقا. واختار الفحول الثلاثة، أبو زيد وشمس الأئمة وفخر بالإسلام أنه حجة للدفع لا للاستحقاق. وهو المشهور عند الفقهاء والوجه أنه ليس بحجة أصلاً لأن الدفع استمرار عدمه الأصلي، لأن موجب الوجود ليس موجب بقائه، فالحكم ببقائه بلا دليل كذا في التحرير. ومما فرع عليه الشقص؛ إذا بيع من الدار وطلب الشريك الشفعة فأنكر المشتري ملك الطالب فيما في يده؛ فالقول له ولا شفعة له إلا ببينة .
The third benefit: Istishab; and it is as in al-Tahrir: the judgment that a confirmed matter continues as long as its non-existence is not thought. They differed on its authority; some said it is an absolute proof, and many others absolutely denied it. The three eminent scholars—Abu Zayd, Shams al-A'imma, and Fakhr al-Islam—chose that it is a proof for repelling (claims) but not for establishing (rights). This is the well-known view among the jurists. And the considered opinion (al-wajh) is that it is not at all a proof, because repelling (a claim) is the continuation of its original non-existence, since that which necessitates existence does not necessitate its continuance; thus, judging its continuance without evidence is (unfounded). That is in al-Tahrir. Among the issues derived from it is the share (shiqs): if part of a house is sold and the partner claims pre-emption (shuf'a), and the buyer denies the claiman's ownership of what is in his hand; then the buyer's statement is accepted, and the claimant has no pre-emption except with evidence.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines 'istishab' (presumption of continuity) as a legal principle: if a state is known to have existed, it is presumed to continue until evidence proves otherwise. The author then reviews the juristic disagreement on its evidentiary authority (hujjiyya). Some scholars consider it a proof in all cases; others reject it entirely. The preferred position among later Hanafi jurists (the 'three giants': Abu Zayd, Shams al-A'imma, Fakhr al-Islam) is that istishab is valid as a proof for repelling claims (daf')—meaning it can be used to maintain the status quo and reject a new claim—but not for establishing a new right (istihqaq). The author also presents an even stronger view (al-wajh) that it is not a proof at all, arguing that the reason for existence does not guarantee continuance, so presuming continuance without evidence is unjustified. An application is given in pre-emption (shuf'a): if a partner claims pre-emption but the buyer denies his ownership, the buyer's denial is accepted based on istishab (the original lack of ownership) unless the claimant produces evidence. This shows how istishab works in practice: the original non-ownership continues until proven otherwise.

Key Terms
الاستصحاب (al-istishab): A juristic presumption that a previously certain state continues until proven otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi scholars mentioned, on which basis is istishab considered a valid proof?
A) For establishing a right (istihqaq)
B) For repelling a claim (daf') only
C) For both equally
D) It is never a proof

Key Takeaway
Istishab is a tool for maintaining the status quo, not for acquiring new rights.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
beginner

The Fourth Maxim: Hardship Brings Ease
Introduction to maxim with evidence and first two causes of ease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the maxim 'al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir' with its primary Quranic and hadith evidence, and APPLY it to identify which shari'i concessions are specific to long travel versus any travel.

Core Idea
Hardship brings ease – a single maxim generating all Shari'ah concessions.

This lesson introduces the fourth of the five major universal maxims in Hanafi fiqh. It is the foundation for every rukhsa (concession) in the Shari'ah. The Qur'an and Sunnah explicitly command ease and remove hardship. We will explore its evidence, the two categories of travel that trigger concessions, and the onset of illness as a cause.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim and its proofs
Commit the Arabic text of the maxim and its Quranic evidence (2:185, 22:78) and the hadith to memory.
This is a dalil naqli for the maxim.
2
Recognize the scope of the maxim
Understand that all shari'i rukhas derive from this principle.
The maxim is kubra, universal.
3
Distinguish the two types of travel
Identify which concessions are specific to travel of three days (qasr, iftar, wiping >24 hours, dropping udhiyah) versus any exit from the district (leaving Friday/Eid, congregation, tayammum, etc.).
Long travel conditions are more extensive.
4
Understand qasr as rukhsat isqat
According to Hanafis, shortening is a concession of omission: completing invalidates unless traveler sits after two rak'ats.
This clarifies the binding nature of the concession.
5
Identify illness as the second cause
Illness permits tayammum and postural adjustments (sitting, lying, gesturing).
Causes continue beyond this incomplete text.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الرابعة : المشقة تجلب التيسير
والأصل فيها قوله تعالى : يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ اليُسر ولا يريد بكم العُسر [البقرة: ١٨٥] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وما جَعَلَ عَلَيْكُم في الدينِ مِنْ حَرَجٍ﴾ [الحج: ۷۸] وفي حديث «أحب الدين إلى الله تعالى الحنيفيةُ السَّمْحة» (۱) قال العلماء: يتخرَّج على هذه القاعدة جميع رخص الشرع وتخفيفاته. واعلم أن أسباب التخفيف في العبادات وغيرها سبعة :

الثاني : المرض ؛ ورخصه كثيرة التيمّم عند الخوف على نفسه أو على عضوه أو من زيادة المرض أو بطئه والقعود في صلاة الفرض والاضطجاع فيها والإيماء،
The Fourth Maxim: Hardship Brings Ease. Its origin is the saying of Allah, the Exalted: 'Allah intends for you ease and does not intend for you hardship' [al-Baqarah 185], and His saying: 'And He has not placed upon you in the religion any hardship' [al-Hajj 78], and in the hadith: 'The most beloved religion to Allah is the easy and tolerant Hanifiyyah.' The scholars said: All of the concessions (rukhas) and alleviations of the Shariah are derived from this maxim. Know that the causes of alleviation in acts of worship and other matters are seven: First: Travel. It is of two types: (1) specific to long travel – which is three days and their nights – and it is: shortening (qasr), breaking fast, wiping for more than a day and night, and dropping of the udhiyah sacrifice according to what is in Ghayat al-Bayan. (2) Not specific to long travel – meaning any exit from the district – and it is: leaving Friday and Eid prayers, congregation, optional prayer on a mount, permission for tayammum, and recommendation of casting lots among his wives. Shortening for the traveler according to us is a concession of omission (rukhsat isqat) in the sense of 'azimah, meaning that completing (tamam) is no longer remained [the traveler may not complete?] and it becomes invalid if he completes and does not sit after the first two rak'ahs if he did not intend to stay until just before the third prostration. Second: Illness; its concessions are many: tayammum when there is fear for his life or limb or from increase of illness or delay, and sitting in the obligatory prayer, and lying down in it, and gesturing (ima')...
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth of the five major qawaid kulliyya (maxims) of Hanafi fiqh: 'Hardship brings ease' (al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir). The author provides Quranic and hadith evidence: Qur'an 2:185 and 22:78, and the hadith about the tolerant Hanifiyyah. This maxim is the foundation of all rukhas (concessions) and lightenings in the Shari'ah. The author then enumerates the seven causes (asbab) of taysir: (1) Travel, divided into two subtypes: (a) long travel (3 days) which brings qasr, iftar in Ramadan, wiping over khuffs for longer than 24 hours, and dropping of udhiyah; (b) any exit from the built-up area which brings leaving Friday, Eid, congregation, praying optional prayers on a mount, tayammum, and recommending lot for wives. He clarifies that qasr for the traveler according to Hanafis is a rukhsat isqat (concession of omission) – it is an 'azimah in the sense that completing (tamam) is no longer the required act; if the traveler intends to complete, the prayer becomes invalid unless he sits after two rak'ats and does not intend to stay. (2) Illness: allows tayammum when there is fear of harm, and sitting, lying down, or gesturing in prayer. The text ends mid-sentence, so the remaining causes (including the other five) are not given here.

Key Terms
المشقة (al-mashaqqa): صعوبة غير معتادة تخرج عن حد الاعتدال في العبادة أو المعاملة.
الرخصة (al-rukhsa): التخفيف الثابت بدليل شرعي لعذر يقتضي التيسير.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a concession specific to long travel (three days)?
A) Qasr of prayer
B) Fitar in Ramadan
C) Tayammum when ill
D) Wiping over khuffs for more than a day

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir' is the bedrock of all shari'ah rukhas, but it only activates when specific causes like travel (long or short) or illness are present.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
15 min
The Maxim 'Hardship Brings Ease' with Proofs and Seven Causes
Explaining the fourth grand maxim: hardship brings ease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir', identify its scriptural proofs, list the seven causes of concession, and apply the maxim to classic Hanafi furuat, including the rulings on excused impurities.

Core Idea
The Shariah is built on ease: whenever genuine hardship arises, the original ruling is relaxed or replaced.

This passage introduces the fourth grand maxim of Hanafi legal theory: hardship brings ease. The author grounds the maxim in explicit Quranic verses and a prophetic hadith, then explains that all Shariah concessions are derived from it. The passage then enumerates the seven causes that trigger these concessions—travel, illness, coercion, forgetfulness, ignorance, general hardship, and deficiency—and provides a wealth of practical examples, especially regarding impurities excused due to widespread affliction. This lesson will walk through the proofs, the causes, and the detailed applications, highlighting how the maxim operates in real fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim and Its Proofs
The maxim is stated at the outset. Three proofs are cited: two Quranic verses (Al-Baqarah 2:185 and Al-Hajj 22:78) and one hadith ('The most beloved religion to Allah is the tolerant hanifiyya'). Note how each proof establishes the principle that the law intends ease, not hardship.
These are dalil naqli (textual proofs).
2
Distinguish the Two Types of Travel Concessions
Travel is divided into long (three days and nights) and short (any exit from the town). Long travel permits qasr, iftar, extended wiping, and dropping udhiya. Short travel allows leaving Jumu'ah, Eid, congregation, and tayammum. Understand the Hanafi classification of qasr as rukhsat isqat (abrogation concession).
Qasr is considered a concession of removal; completing the prayer is invalid.
3
Examine the Causes of Illness and the Remaining Four Causes
Illness brings many concessions (tayammum, sitting in prayer, breaking fast for elderly with fidyah, etc.). Coercion, forgetfulness, ignorance, and general hardship are also mentioned. For general hardship (usr wa umum al-balwa), a long list of excused impurities is given.
The seventh cause (deficiency) appears incomplete in the text.
4
Analyze the Furu' of General Hardship
Work through the specific rulings: excused impurities like less than a dirham of blood, street mud, cat urine, bird droppings, etc., and the ruling that fire purifies dung and human waste. Note the practical reasoning: if impure, bread would be impure in most lands.
These examples demonstrate how the maxim is applied in daily life.
5
Determine the Istithna' and the Limits of the Maxim
Not every hardship triggers ease—only genuine, unbearable hardship beyond normal tolerance. The basic principle ('certainty is not overruled by doubt') governs when the maxim does not apply.
The istithna' is crucial to avoid misuse of the maxim.

بطهارة المسك، وإن كان أصله دما، والزباد (۱) (۱) زياد اللبن ما لا خير فيه ومنه قولهم: اختلط الخائر بالزباد. يُضرب لاختلاط الحق بالباطل .
The fourth rule: Hardship brings ease. Its basis is the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'Allah intends ease for you and does not intend hardship' [Al-Baqarah 2:185], and His saying: 'And He has not placed upon you in the religion any difficulty' [Al-Hajj 22:78], and in the hadith: 'The most beloved religion to Allah is the tolerant (al-hanifiyya al-samha).' The scholars said: All the concessions and alleviations of the Shariah are derived from this rule. Know that the causes of alleviation in acts of worship and others are seven: First: Travel, and it is of two types: the first is what is specific to long travel, which is three days and their nights, and it includes shortening the prayer, breaking the fast, wiping over the khuffs for more than a day and night, and the omission of the udhiya according to Ghayat al-Bayan. The second is what is not specific to it, meaning absolute exit from the town; it includes leaving Jumu'ah, the two Eids, the congregation, and supererogatory prayer while riding, the permissibility of tayammum, and the desirability of drawing lots among his wives. Shortening for the traveler according to us is a concession of abrogation in the sense of determination, meaning that completing the prayer is no longer permissible, and it would be invalid if he completes it without sitting at the end of the two rak'ahs unless he intended to stay there legally until just before the third sajdah. Second: Illness; its concessions are many: tayammum when fearing for oneself or one's limb or from increased illness or delayed recovery, sitting in obligatory prayer, lying down in it, and gesturing, staying away from the congregation while still gaining virtue, breaking the fast in Ramadan for the very old man with the obligation of fidyah, transitioning from fasting to feeding in the kaffarah of zihar, breaking the fast in Ramadan, leaving i'tikaf, appointing a proxy in hajj and in stoning the pillars, permitting the prohibitions of ihram with fidyah, treating with najasat and with wine according to one of the two sayings. Qadikhan chose its prohibition. Swallowing the morsel if one chokes on it by agreement, and permitting looking by the physician even at the private parts and the two pudenda. Third: Coercion. Fourth: Forgetfulness. Fifth: Ignorance, and its discussions will come later. Sixth: Hardship and general widespread affliction, such as praying with excused impurity, like less than a dirham of light impurity and a dirham's worth of heavy impurity, and the impurity of the excused person that falls on his clothes, and the garment was such that whenever he washed it out it came out again, and flea and bedbug blood on the garment if abundant, and urine splashed on the garment to the amount of needle heads, and street mud, and traces of impurity difficult to remove, and cat urine outside of water vessels, and upon it is the fatwa, and among them some made absolute regarding the cat and the mouse, and the droppings of pigeons and sparrows even if abundant, and the droppings of forbidden birds in one narration, and what lacks flowing blood, and the saliva of a sleeper absolutely according to the relied-upon opinion, and the mouths of children, and dust of manure, and a small amount of impure smoke, and the animal's digestive tract, and forgiveness for wind and farts if they stain the moist trousers, and the anal opening according to the relied-upon opinion. Al-Halwani did not pray in his trousers, and there is no interpretation for his action except caution regarding disagreement. And from that is our saying that fire purifies dung and human waste, so we say that their ash is pure for facilitation, otherwise the impurity of bread would be necessary in most countries. And from that is the purity of bat urine and its droppings, and camel droppings if they fall into the milking bowl and are thrown away before breaking up, and the lightening of impurity of animal droppings according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and what affects the garment from the vapor of impurity on the sound opinion, and what affects it from what flows from the cesspool, as long as the majority opinion is not that it is impurity, and water from the roof slab by istihsan. Its picture: Human waste was burned in a house, and the water from the roof slab affected someone's garment. Likewise, a stable if it is hot and on its roof is a slab, or a latrine if on it is a slab and water drips from it. Likewise, a bathhouse if impurity is poured into it and its walls and ceiling sweat and water drips from it. Likewise, if in the stable there is a hanging water jug and water oozes from the bottom of the jug. And the opinion on the purity of musk, even if its origin is blood, and zabad (musk pod of cat) is bad milk, no good in it, and from it the saying: the bad mixed with the good; it is a proverb for mixing truth with falsehood.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is the presentation of the fourth grand maxim (al-qa'ida al-kubra): 'Hardship brings ease' (al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir). The author begins by citing the Quranic proofs: Surah al-Baqarah 2:185 and Surah al-Hajj 22:78, alongside the hadith about the 'tolerant hanifiyya' (recorded by al-Bukhari). The scholars state that all legal concessions and facilitations (rukhas) of the Shariah are derived from this maxim. The author then lists seven causes (asbab) that bring about these concessions: travel, illness, coercion, forgetfulness, ignorance, general hardship/widespread affliction, and deficiency (nawqis?), though only six are listed; the seventh appears to be 'nawqis' (deficiency) but the text ends before it. Each cause is detailed with numerous examples from various fiqh chapters: for travel, the concessions of qasr, iftar, wiping over khuffs, dropping Jumu'ah, etc.; for illness, tayammum, sitting in prayer, breaking fast for the elderly, etc.; for coercion and forgetfulness; for general hardship (usr wa umum al-balwa), a long list of excused impurities including small amounts of blood, urine, street mud, cat urine, bird droppings, and rulings on the purification of fire for dung, the purity of musk despite its bloody origin, and the practical concession regarding water from roofs and stables. The passage concludes with a note on 'zabad' (bad milk) as a proverb for mixture of truth and falsehood.

Key Terms
المشقة (al-mashaqqa): Hardship that justifies easing of Shariah rulings.
التيسير (al-taysir): Ease or facilitation, the legal effect of the maxim.
رخصة (rukhsa): A concession that allows departing from a general rule due to a valid excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as a cause (sabab) of legal concession in the passage?
A) Travel
B) Illness
C) Wealth
D) Coercion

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Hardship brings ease' is a foundational principle of the Shariah, deriving from clear texts, and it governs the application of all legal concessions across fiqh.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
6 min
Deficiency as a Cause of Ease: The Naqs Maxim
Introduces the seventh cause of ease: deficiency in legal capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim of deficiency (naqs) as a cause of easement, apply it to the cases of minors, insane, women, and slaves, and identify its scope and the one internal dispute in Hanafi legal theory.

Core Idea
Deficiency in legal capacity is itself a hardship that triggers easement.

This lesson introduces the seventh grand maxim of ease in the Hanafi school: deficiency (naqs). The maxim holds that when a person's legal capacity is deficient—due to minority, insanity, female gender, or slavery—the Law grants them ease by lifting or reducing certain obligations. Understanding this maxim is crucial for a mufti to correctly apply exceptions and concessions in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
1. State the maxim
The maxim is: deficiency (naqs) in legal capacity is a form of hardship, so it warrants facilitation. Read the opening line: السبب السابع: النقص؛ فإنه نوع من المشقة فناسب التخفيف.
This is a wusta (intermediate) maxim that governs many specific rulings.
2
2. Identify categories of deficiency
The text lists four categories: minors (الصبي), insane (المجنون), women (النساء), and slaves (الأرقاء). Each has a deficiency in legal capacity – either complete lack (minor, insane) or partial reduction (women, slaves).
3
3. Examine the legal concessions
For each category, the text gives examples: minors and insane have their wealth managed by guardian, custody by women without compulsion; women are exempt from jama‘a, jumu‘a, jihad, jizya, and (according to some) ‘aql; women may wear silk and gold; slaves have half obligations in penalties and ‘idda.
These concessions show the principle in action.
4
4. Analyze the one internal dispute
The text mentions a difference on whether women are exempt from bearing ‘aql (blood money). The author says 'الصحيح خلافه' – the correct view is that they are not exempt, meaning they do bear ‘aql. This shows that not all deficiencies are treated identically.
This is a nuance for fatwa.
5
5. Apply to fatwa
When issuing a fatwa, the mufti must recognize if the questioner belongs to a deficient category and apply the appropriate easement. For example, if a woman asks about Friday prayer, the mufti cites this maxim.
The maxim is not absolute; exceptions exist, as seen in the ‘aql case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السبب السابع : النقص ؛ فإنه نوع من المشقة فناسب التخفيف؛ فمن ذلك تكليف الصبي والمجنون ففوَّض أمر أموالها إلى الولي وتربيته وحضانته إلى النساء رحمة عليه ولم يجبرهنَّ على الحضانة تيسيرًا عليهنَّ وعدم تكليف النساء بكثير مما وجب على الرجال ؛ كالجماعة والجمعة والجهاد والجزية وتحمّل العقل على قول . والصحيح خلافه، وإباحة لبس الحرير وحلي الذهب، وعدم تكليف الأرقاء بكثير مما وجب على الأحرار لكونه على النصف من الحرّ في الحدود والعدة مما سيأتي في أحكام العبيد. وهذه فوائد مهمة نختم بها الكلام على هذه القاعدة.
The seventh cause: deficiency; for it is a type of hardship, so facilitation is appropriate. From that: the obligation upon the minor and the insane – so the matter of their wealth is delegated to the guardian, and their upbringing and custody to women, as a mercy upon him, and they (women) are not compelled to custody as a facilitation upon them, and women are not charged with many things that are obligatory upon men, such as congregational prayer, Friday prayer, jihad, jizya, and bearing the 'aql according to one opinion – the correct is otherwise – and the permissibility of wearing silk and gold jewelry, and slaves are not charged with many things obligatory upon freemen, because the slave is half of a free person in penalties and 'iddah, as will come in the rules of slaves. And these are important benefits with which we conclude the discussion on this maxim.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage introduces the seventh cause (al-sabab al-sabiʿ) for leniency: deficiency (naqṣ) in the person's legal capacity. The author states that deficiency is a type of hardship, thus it warrants ease. Examples include: the minor and insane are not held fully accountable; their wealth is managed by a guardian, and their custody is given to women without compulsion. Women are exempt from certain obligations that apply to men, such as congregational prayer, Friday prayer, jihad, and jizya. Some even exempt them from bearing the 'aql (blood money liability) though the correct view is otherwise. Women are also permitted to wear silk and gold, which men are forbidden. Slaves are charged with half the obligations of freemen in penalties and 'iddah. The passage concludes by noting these are important benefits that wrap up the discussion on the maxim.

Key Terms
النقص (an-naqṣ): Deficiency in legal capacity (ahlīyah), referring to minors, insane, women, and slaves, which serves as a cause for leniency.
الصبي (aṣ-ṣabiyy): A minor child who has not reached puberty; lacks full legal capacity.
المجنون (al-majnūn): An insane person; completely lacks legal capacity for most acts.
الرق (ar-riqq): Slavery; a state of legal deficiency where the slave has limited capacity and is owned by another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an example of leniency due to deficiency mentioned in the text?
A) Women are not obligated to attend Friday prayer.
B) Slaves receive half the penalty for crimes.
C) Minors are not required to fast Ramadan.
D) Women may wear silk and gold.

Key Takeaway
The deficiency (naqs) maxim is a key tool for identifying legal easements for minors, the insane, women, and slaves in Hanafi fiqh.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
