Hardship Does Not Override Explicit Text
Establishing the rule that hardship is considered only where no text exists

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim that hardship does not override explicit text to derive rulings in new cases, and identify when a fatwa may consider widespread affliction (balwa) only in the absence of a clear nass.

Core Idea
Where a clear text exists, hardship is no excuse — the text prevails.

In Islamic law, ease is a general principle, but it is not absolute. This lesson examines a critical limiting condition: the presence of an explicit text (nass). When a clear ruling exists from Quran or Sunnah, considerations of hardship do not override it. The source passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir demonstrates this through two well-known applications: the prohibition of grazing in the Haram and the classification of animal dung as intensely impure. A later gloss reconciles the famous maxim 'That whose affliction is widespread, its ruling becomes light' with this rule.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the qaida: hardship is considered only when there is no text. Understand its scope: it restricts the major maxim 'al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir' by adding a condition.
This is a sughra (minor) qaida within the Hanafi discipline of qawaid.
2
Examine the first furu': Haram grass
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prohibit grazing and cutting grass in the Haram except idhkhir, citing a clear text on the Haram's sanctity. Abu Yusuf permitted it due to hardship, but the majority refuted him because the text overrides.
This shows thamarah al-khilaf: the practical difference in fatwa.
3
Examine the second furu': dung impurity
Abu Hanifa classifies dung as intensely impure based on the hadith 'innaha riks'. Even though dung is widespread (balwa), the text prevents any relaxation, analogous to human urine.
Balwa does not affect rulings when a nass is present.
4
Understand the later gloss and reconciliation
Later scholars in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli explained that according to Abu Hanifa, there is 'no hardship in avoiding it', and according to the Sahibayn, 'no widespread affliction in its occurrence'—both relative to the general public. This confirms the maxim 'ma ummat balwatuhu khaffat qadiyatuhu' but only in the absence of a text.
The gloss does not create an exception; it clarifies the scope.
5
Synthesize the principle for fatwa
When issuing a fatwa, if a clear nass exists, the mufti must adhere to it regardless of hardship. If no nass exists, considerations of ease and balwa may lighten the ruling, provided they are truly widespread.
This is a key adab al-mufti principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة الثالثة :
·
المشقة والحرج؛ إنما يعتبران في موضع لا نص فيه، وأما مع النص بخلافه فلا، ولذا قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله بحرمة رعي حشيش الحرم وقطعه إلا الإذخر. وجوّز أبو يوسف رحمه الله رعيه للحرج وردَّ عليه بما ذكرناه، وذكره الزيلعي في جنايات الإحرام وقال في الأنجاس إن الإمام يقول بتغليظ نجاسة الأرواث، لقوله عليه السلام إنها ركس أي نجس ولا اعتبار عنده بالبلوى في موضع النص، كما في بول الآدمي فإن البلوى فيه أعم. (انتهى). وفي شرح منية المصلي: من المتأخرين من زاد في تفسير الغليظة على قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. ولا حرج في اجتنابه كما في الاختيار، وفي الغليظة على قولهما ولا بلوى في إصابته كما في الاختيار أيضًا. وفي المحيط وهي زيادة حسنة يشهد لها بعض فروع الباب. والمراد بقوله ولا حرج في اجتنابه ولا بلوى في إصابته على اختلاف العبارتين، إنما هو بالنسبة إلى جنس المكلفين فيقع الاتفاق على صدق القضية المشهورة وهي : إن ما عمت بليته خفت قضيته . (انتهى).
The third benefit: Hardship and difficulty are only considered in a place where there is no text; but when there is a text to the contrary, they are not. For this reason Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said that it is prohibited to graze and cut the grass of the Haram except al-idhkhir. Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) permitted its grazing due to hardship, but was refuted with what we mentioned. Al-Zayla'i mentioned this in the chapter on crimes of ihram. He said in al-Anjas: That Imam (Abu Hanifa) holds the intensification of the impurity of dung due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Indeed it is riks' i.e., impure, and he does not give consideration to widespread affliction in the presence of a text, just as with human urine, for the affliction therein is more general. (End). In Sharh Munyat al-Musalli: Some later scholars added in the interpretation of 'ghaliza' (intense) according to Abu Hanifa's opinion: 'There is no hardship in avoiding it' as in al-Ikhtiyar, and 'in the intense according to them, there is no widespread affliction in its occurrence' as also in al-Ikhtiyar. In al-Muhit: This addition is good, and some branches of the chapter testify to it. What is meant by his saying 'there is no hardship in avoiding it' and 'no widespread affliction in its occurrence' despite the difference in phrasing, is only relative to the general class of obligated persons; thus there is agreement on the truth of the famous maxim: 'That whose affliction is widespread, its ruling becomes light.' (End).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir articulates a key principle in usul al-iftā’: hardship and difficulty are considered only where there is no explicit textual ruling to the contrary. The author provides two applications: First, regarding the Haram of Makkah: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad ruled that grazing and cutting its grass is prohibited except for idhkhir (a type of fragrant grass), and they did not relax this due to hardship, whereas Abu Yusuf permitted it because of hardship. The second application is about the impurity of animal dung: Abu Hanifa considered it intensely impure based on the Prophet’s hadith calling it riks, and he did not allow the widespread affliction (balwa) to override the text, similar to human urine which is also intensely impure despite being common. The commentary from Sharh Munyat al-Musalli clarifies that later scholars explained that according to Abu Hanifa, there is no hardship in avoiding such impurities because of the text, and according to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the widespread affliction is not present relative to the general public. The discussion ends by affirming the famous maxim: 'That which is widely encountered becomes light in its ruling.'

Key Terms
مشقة وحرج (mashaqqa wa haraj): Hardship or difficulty that may justify a legal dispensation.
نص (nass): An explicit textual evidence from Quran or Hadith that provides a definitive ruling.
بلوى (balwa): Widespread affliction or common occurrence that might normally ease a ruling.
ركس (riks): A term from hadith meaning impure, specifically for dung.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best explains why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prohibited cutting grass in the Haram despite hardship?
A) The hardship was not severe enough.
B) There is a clear text (nass) prohibiting it, and the qaida states that hardship is not considered when a text exists.
C) Abu Yusuf's view was rejected by consensus.
D) Cutting grass is never permitted during ihram.

Key Takeaway
An explicit textual ruling (nass) overrides any consideration of hardship or widespread affliction; the qaida 'hardship brings ease' applies only where no nass exists.

The Maxim: When Matters Tighten They Expand
Introducing the maxim of inverse proportionality in legal rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'الأمر إذا ضاق اتسع وإذا اتسع ضاق', identify its rank as a wusta maxim, and distinguish it from the analogous maxims on initiation versus continuation.

Core Idea
When a matter tightens, it expands; when it expands, it tightens — a balance principle in Hanafi fiqh.

This lesson introduces a key Hanafi legal maxim that captures the dynamic nature of fiqh rulings: hardship brings leniency, but excessive ease can bring strictness. Understanding this inverse relationship is essential for a mufti to issue balanced fatwas that adapt to changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three formulations
Read the Arabic passage and note (1) the two separate maxims: 'إذا ضاق اتسع' and 'إذا اتسع ضاق'; (2) the combined version: 'كل ما تجاوز عن حده انعكس إلى ضده'.
The combined version shows that excessive strictness or leniency flips to its opposite.
2
Understand the dynamics
Grasp why tightness leads to expansion and vice versa. For example, when performing wudu is extremely difficult due to cold, the ruling expands to allow tayammum. Conversely, when ease is excessive, rulings may tighten to preserve legal objectives.
This is not a license for constant leniency; it is a balancing mechanism.
3
Relate to analogous maxims
The text mentions two analogous maxims: 'يغتفر في الدوام ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء' and its opposite. These address whether leniency is granted at the start or during continuation. Understand how they mirror the main maxim's inverse logic.
For example, a minor impurity in clothing may be excused at the start of prayer but not if it persists throughout.
4
Scope of application
This maxim applies across all chapters of fiqh where rulings change due to hardship or ease. It is a wusta (intermediate) maxim, more specific than the kubra (major) maxims but more general than sughra (minor) ones.
It is often used in istinbat and fatwa to determine the balance between strictness and leniency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة الرابعة : ذكر بعضهم؛ ؛ أن الأمر إذا ضاق اتَّسع وإذا اتسع ضاق، وجمع بينهما بعضهم بقوله كل ما تجاوز عن حده انعكس إلى ضده. ونظير هاتين القاعدتين في التعاكس قولهم : يغتفر في الدوام ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء وقولهم : يغتفر في الابتداء ما لا يغتفر في البقاء، وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى ذكر فروعهما.
The fourth benefit: Some of them mentioned that when a matter becomes tight it expands, and when it expands it becomes tight. And some combined both with their saying: 'Everything that exceeds its limit reverts to its opposite.' And analogous to these two mutual maxims is their saying: 'What is excused in continuation is not excused in initiation' and their saying: 'What is excused in initiation is not excused in continuation.' And there will come, Allah willing, mention of their branches.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This benefit presents two related maxims that deal with the dynamic of legal rulings: (1) When a matter becomes constrained (e.g., difficulty increases), the ruling becomes expansive (relaxed). (2) When a matter becomes expansive (e.g., ease prevails), the ruling becomes constricted (strict). A third formulation combines both: when something exceeds its proper limit, it turns into its opposite. The author then notes two other analogous principles: that something may be excused in the duration but not at the start, or excused at the start but not in continuation. These maxims reflect the nuanced nature of fiqh, where rulings adapt to context. The author promises to discuss their applications later.

Key Terms
ضاق (daqa): To become tight or restricted, referring to a situation of hardship that triggers a relaxation.
اتسع (ittasa'a): To become expansive or lenient, referring to the relaxation of a ruling.
انعكس إلى ضده (in'akasa ila diddihi): Reverses to its opposite, meaning that excessive strictness leads to leniency and vice versa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which maxim is analogous to 'when matters tighten they expand'?
A) المشقة تجلب التيسير
B) اليقين لا يزول بالشك
C) ما عمت بليته خفت قضيته
D) يغتفر في الدوام ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'When matters tighten they expand, and when they expand they tighten' is a foundational wusta qawida governing the dynamic adaptation of fiqh rulings to circumstance.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Maxim: Harm Must Be Removed
Stating the fifth major maxim with its hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fifth major Hanafi legal maxim, identify its primary hadith evidence, and apply it to a concrete legal scenario.

Core Idea
Harm must be removed: a universal legal principle based on the Prophetic command 'no harm and no reciprocating harm.'

This lesson introduces one of the five major legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kubra) in Hanafi fiqh: 'al-darar yuzal' (harm must be removed). This maxim is derived from a well-known hadith that prohibits both initiating harm and retaliating with harm. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti when issuing fatwas on torts, property rights, and public welfare.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim text in Arabic
Write and recite: 'الضرر يزال'. This is the fifth major maxim and a cornerstone of Hanafi legal theory.
Memorizing the exact wording is essential for fatwa drafting.
2
Identify the hadith evidence
The maxim is based on the hadith 'لا ضرر ولا ضرار' (no harm and no reciprocating harm). Note its transmission: reported by Malik as mursal, and by al-Hakim, al-Bayhaqi, al-Daraqutni, Ibn Majah, and Ahmad with connected chains.
The hadith is accepted by all madhhabs; its mursal version is strengthened by multiple routes.
3
Understand the dual prohibition
The hadith prohibits two concepts: darar (initiating harm) and dirar (reciprocating harm). The maxim covers both: not only must you not cause harm, but you also cannot take revenge by causing harm in return.
This distinguishes the maxim from a simple command to avoid harm.
4
Recognize the scope and key applications
The maxim applies across all fiqh chapters: torts, property, neighbor disputes, public welfare, and liability. It obliges individuals and authorities to remove existing harm and prevent new harm.
It is a universal principle, but it interacts with other maxims (e.g., necessity).
5
Note the balance with other maxims
The maxim is not absolute; it is qualified by principles like 'harm is removed as much as possible' (al-darar yuzal bi qadr al-imkan) and necessity (darura). A mufti must weigh competing harms.
This is a common point of misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الخامسة : الضرر يزال
أصلها قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: (لا ضرر ولا ضرار (۱) أخرجه مالك في الموطأ عن عمرو بن يحيى عن أبيه مرسلاً. وأخرجه الحاكم في المستدرك والبيهقي والدارقطني من حديث أبي سعيد الخدري، وأخرجه ابن ماجه من حديث ابن عباس
وعبادة بن
الله الصامت رضي عنهم.
(۱) رواه ابن ماجه في كتاب الأحكام باب ۱۷ والموطأ في كتاب الأقضية حديث ٣١. وأحمد في
مسنده (۳۲۷/۵).
The fifth maxim: Harm must be removed. Its origin is his saying (peace and blessings be upon him): 'No harm and no reciprocating harm.' Reported by Malik in al-Muwatta from Amr ibn Yahya from his father as a mursal. And reported by al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Daraqutni from the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, and by Ibn Majah from the hadith of Ibn Abbas and Ubada ibn al-Samit (may Allah be pleased with them). (1) Reported by Ibn Majah in Kitab al-Ahkam, bab 17, and al-Muwatta in Kitab al-Aqdiya, hadith 31, and Ahmad in his Musnad (5/327).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth major legal maxim in the Hanafi tradition: 'Harm must be removed' (al-darar yuzal). The author provides the hadith basis for this maxim: the prophetic saying 'La darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no reciprocating harm). Although the hadith is transmitted as mursal from Malik, it is also reported with a connected chain by al-Hakim, al-Bayhaqi, al-Daraqutni, and others. The maxim means that any harm (darar) should be eliminated or prevented, and also that one should not respond to harm with harm (dirar). This is a foundational principle of Islamic law, underlying many rulings in various chapters, such as the prohibition of causing damage to neighbors, the duty to remove obstacles from the road, and the rules of liability.

Key Terms
ضرر (darar): Harm or injury to oneself or others.
ضرار (dirar): Reciprocating harm or causing harm in return.
يزال (yuzal): Must be removed, indicating an obligation to eliminate harm where possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Quranic verse: 'And do not throw yourselves into destruction'
C) Hadith: 'No harm and no reciprocating harm'
D) Rational reasoning

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-darar yuzal' is a fundamental principle of Hanafi fiqh derived from the prophetic command to prevent harm and not to retaliate with harm.

The Maxim: When Narrow Expands and When Expands Narrows
Introduction of a correlative maxim and its counterpart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim, identify its inverse nature, and explain its relationship with the two parallel maxims about inception and continuation.

Core Idea
When a ruling becomes too stringent, it is relaxed; when relaxed, it becomes stringent.

This passage introduces a foundational Hanafi maxim that governs the flexibility of rulings. Understanding this maxim helps a mufti navigate cases where the usual ruling becomes too burdensome. The author also links it to two related maxims about differing standards at inception versus continuation.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim
Repeat the Arabic: 'إذا ضاق الأمر اتسع وإذا اتسع ضاق'. Note the inverse word pairs.
2
Understand the inverse relationship
Recognise that the maxim states a direct inverse: constraint leads to leniency, ease leads to strictness.
3
Link to the combined formulation
Some scholars combine both directions: 'كل ما تجاوز عن حده انعكس إلى ضده' (Everything that exceeds its limit reverts to its opposite).
This captures the same idea in a single sentence.
4
Connect to parallel maxims
The author mentions two related maxims: (1) 'What is excused in continuation is not excused at the beginning' and (2) 'What is excused at the beginning is not excused at continuation'. These also involve inverse temporal rulings.
These will be detailed in later lessons.
5
Anticipate applications
The author promises to bring furuat (branches) later. Expect examples from taharah, salah, and other chapters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة الرابعة : ذكر بعضهم؛ أن الأمر إذا ضاق اتَّسع وإذا اتسع ضاق، وجمع بينهما بعضهم بقوله كل ما تجاوز عن حده انعكس إلى ضده. ونظير هاتين القاعدتين في التعاكس قولهم : يغتفر في الدوام ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء وقولهم : يغتفر في الابتداء ما لا يغتفر في البقاء، وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى ذكر فروعهما.
The fourth benefit: Some mentioned that when a matter becomes narrow it expands, and when it expands it becomes narrow. And some combined them with their saying: Everything that exceeds its limit reverts to its opposite. And analogous to these two maxims in their inverse relationship are their sayings: What is excused in continuation is not excused at the beginning, and their saying: What is excused at the beginning is not excused at continuation. And their branches will come, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This short passage introduces a key fiqh maxim: 'When a matter becomes narrow it expands, and when it expands it becomes narrow.' This means that rulings are often inversely related to the scope or difficulty of the situation. The author notes that some scholars combine both into one statement: 'Everything that exceeds its limit reverts to its opposite.' He then compares this to two other well-known maxims: (1) 'What is excused in continuation is not excused at the beginning' and (2) 'What is excused at the beginning is not excused at continuation' — which are also inverse pairs. The author promises to mention their applications later.

Key Terms
ضاق الأمر اتسع (ḍāqa al-amr ittasa'a): A maxim meaning that when a situation becomes constrained (e.g., harder to fulfill), the ruling becomes more lenient; and when it becomes unconstrained, the ruling becomes stricter
يغتفر في الدوام ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء (yughtafar fī al-dawām mā lā yughtafar fī al-ibtidā'): What is excused in continuation is not excused at the beginning
يغتفر في الابتداء ما لا يغتفر في البقاء (yughtafar fī al-ibtidā' mā lā yughtafar fī al-baqā'): What is excused at the beginning is not excused at continuation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are unrelated
B) They are inverse (على التعاكس)
C) They are identical
D) They are contradictory

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi legal system uses an inverse relationship between constraint and leniency, ensuring rulings adapt to circumstances.

The Major Maxim: Harm Must Be Eliminated and Its First Corollary
Statement of the fifth major maxim and its first corollary with examples and interschool comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Harm must be eliminated' to new cases and identify when the sub-maxim 'Necessities permit prohibitions' is limited by greater harm.

Core Idea
Harm must be eliminated, but not by causing equal or greater harm.

This lesson presents the fifth major maxim of Hanafi fiqh: 'Harm must be eliminated', based on the prophetic hadith 'La darar wa la dirar'. The maxim governs a wide range of rulings from sales to criminal law. A key corollary is that necessities can permit prohibitions, but this is strictly limited by the principle that harm cannot be removed by equal or greater harm. The text includes many practical applications and a discussion with the Shafi'i school about the boundary of this sub-maxim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its textual evidence
Locate the wording of the fifth major maxim (الضرر يزال) and the hadith that forms its basis (لا ضرر ولا ضرار). Note the Hanafi interpretation: no initiating harm and no retaliating harm.
The hadith is narrated through multiple chains; its meaning is established in the school.
2
Examine the scope of the maxim through examples
Review the listed furuat: return due to defect, pre-emption, qisas, etc. Identify how each application removes harm. Pay special attention to the mulberry tree example from al-Bazzaziyya—it shows the maxim operates even in mundane disputes.
The examples span ibadat (hudud, kaffarat) and muamalat (shuf'a, khiyar).
3
Analyze the first corollary: Necessities permit prohibitions
State the sub-maxim (الضرورات تبيح المحظورات) and list the examples given: eating carrion when starving, using wine to swallow a morsel, uttering disbelief under coercion, destroying property, taking from a debtor, and repelling an aggressor.
This sub-maxim is widely used but has built-in limitations.
4
Evaluate the Shafi'i condition and the Hanafi response
The Shafi'is add a condition that the necessity must not be diminished (e.g., a dead prophet cannot be eaten). Although the Hanafis do not state this as a separate condition, they give examples that achieve the same result: coercion to kill does not permit killing, and a body buried without shroud should not be exhumed.
Both schools agree that not all necessities override prohibitions; the difference is in terminology.
5
Identify the exceptions to the main maxim (istithna'at)
The text shows two cases where harm is not eliminated: when doing so would cause greater harm. These are governed by the maxim 'Harm cannot be eliminated by like harm' (الضرر لا يزال بمثله). List the examples from the text: coercion to kill and burial without shroud or washing.
The istithna'at ensure the primary maxim is not applied absolutely.

القاعدة الخامسة : الضرر يزال
أصلها قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: (لا ضرر ولا ضرار (۱) أخرجه مالك في الموطأ عن عمرو بن يحيى عن أبيه مرسلاً. وأخرجه الحاكم في المستدرك والبيهقي والدارقطني من حديث أبي سعيد الخدري، وأخرجه ابن ماجه من حديث ابن عباس وعبادة بن الصامت رضي عنهم.
(۱) رواه ابن ماجه في كتاب الأحكام باب ۱۷ والموطأ في كتاب الأقضية حديث ٣١. وأحمد في مسنده (۳۲۷/۵).
۷۲

وسره في المغرب بأنه لا يضر الرجل أخاه ابتداء ولا جزاء. (انتهى). وذكره
أصحابنا رحمهم
الله في كتاب الغصب والشفعة وغيرهما.
ويبتني على هذه القاعدة كثير من أبواب الفقه . فمن ذلك؛ الردّ بالعيب وجميع أنواع الخيارات والحجر بسائر أنواعه على المفتى به والشفعة فإنها للشريك لدفع ضرر القسمة وللجار لدفع ضرر الجار السوء (بجيرانها تغلو الديار وترخص). والقصاص والحدود والكفَّارات وضمان المتلفات والجبر على القسمة بشرطه ونصب الأئمة والقضاة ودفع الصائل وقتال المشركين والبغاة. وفي البزازية من کتاب الكراهية باع أغصان فرصاد والمشتري إذا ارتقى لقطعها يطلع على عورات الجيران؛ يؤمر بأن يخبرهم وقت الارتقاء ليستتروا مرة أو مرتين، فإن فعل وإلا رفع إلى الحاكم ليمنعه من الارتقاء. (انتهى).
وهذه القاعدة مع التي قبلها متحدة أو متداخلة، وتتعلق بها قواعد :
الأولى :
الضرورات تبيح المحظورات، ومن ثم جاز أكل الميتة عند المخمصة، وإساغة اللقمة بالخمر، والتلفّظ بكلمة الكفر للإكراه، وكذا إتلاف المال وأخذ مال الممتنع من أداء الدين بغير إذنه ودفع الصائل، ولو أدى إلى قتله.
وزاد الشافعية على هذه القاعدة بشرط عدم نقصانها ؛ قالوا ليخرج ما لو كان الميت نبيًّا، فإنه لا يحلّ أكله للمضطر، لأن حرمته أعظم في نظر الشرع من مهجة
المضطر . (انتهى).
ولكن ذكر أصحابنا رحمهم الله ما يفيده؛ فإنهم قالوا: لو أكره على قتل غيره بقتل لا يرخص له، فإن قتله أيْمَ لأن مفسدة قتل نفسه أخف من مفسدة قتل غيره. وقالوا لو دفن بلا تكفين لا ينبش منه؛ لأن مفسدة هتك أشد حرمته من عدم تكفينه الذي قام الستر بالتراب مقامه. وكذا قالوا: لو دفن بلا غسل وأهيل عليه التراب؛
صلي على قبره ولا يخرج.
The fifth maxim: Harm must be eliminated. Its origin is his saying (peace be upon him): 'No harm and no reciprocating harm.' Recorded by Malik in al-Muwatta from Amr ibn Yahya from his father as a mursal report, and by al-Hakim, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Daraqutni from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, and by Ibn Majah from Ibn Abbas and Ubadah ibn al-Samit (may Allah be pleased with them). And it is interpreted in al-Mughrib as one should not cause harm to his brother initially nor in retaliation. (End). Our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) mentioned it in the books of ghasb, shuf'a, and others. Many chapters of fiqh are built upon this maxim. From that: return due to defect, all types of options, interdiction in all its types according to the mufta bihi view, shuf'a (pre-emption) for the partner to repel the harm of division and for the neighbor to repel the harm of a bad neighbor (with neighbors, houses become expensive and cheap), qisas, hudud, kaffarat, guarantee of damages, compulsion for division with its condition, appointment of imams and judges, repelling an aggressor, fighting polytheists and rebels. And in al-Bazzaziyya from the book of karahiyya: If someone sells branches of a mulberry tree, and the buyer climbs to cut them and sees the private parts of neighbors, he is ordered to inform them at the time of climbing so they may cover themselves once or twice; if he does so, otherwise the matter is raised to the judge to prevent him from climbing. (End). This maxim and the one before it are united or overlapping, and there are related sub-maxims: The first: Necessities permit prohibitions. Hence it is permissible to eat carrion when starving, to swallow a morsel with wine, to utter a word of disbelief under coercion, similarly destroying property, taking the property of one who refuses to pay a debt without his permission, and repelling an aggressor even if it leads to killing him. The Shafi'is added to this maxim a condition: that the need does not diminish; they said: to exclude the case where the deceased is a prophet, for it is not permissible for the desperate person to eat him because his sanctity is greater in the eyes of the law than the life of the desperate person. (End). However, our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) mentioned what supports it (the condition): They said if someone is coerced into killing another person, and killing is not permitted for him, then if he kills him, he is sinful because the harm of killing himself is lighter than the harm of killing another. They said if someone is buried without a shroud, he should not be exhumed because the harm of violating his extreme sanctity is greater than the lack of shrouding which is substituted by the earth covering him. Similarly, they said if he is buried without washing and soil is thrown upon him, prayer is performed over his grave and he is not removed.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth major maxim of Hanafi fiqh: 'Harm must be eliminated' (الضرر يزال). It is based on the famous hadith 'La darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no reciprocating harm). The author explains its interpretation: one should not initiate harm nor respond with harm. This maxim is foundational for many rulings including: return due to defect (khiyar al-ayb), pre-emption (shuf'a), qisas, hudud, kaffarat, and repelling aggressors. A specific example from al-Bazzaziyya illustrates a practical application regarding mulberry tree branches and privacy. The maxim overlaps with the previous one about hardship. Then the first sub-maxim is introduced: 'Necessities permit prohibitions' (الضرورات تبيح المحظورات). Examples: eating carrion when starving, using wine to swallow a morsel, uttering disbelief under coercion, destroying property or taking from a debtor, and repelling an aggressor even if killing results. The Shafi'is add a condition that the necessity must not be diminished (e.g., a dead prophet cannot be eaten). Although the Hanafis do not state this exact condition, they provide examples that support the same principle: coercion to kill does not permit killing, and a body buried without shroud or washing should not be exhumed because the harm of violation outweighs the benefit. This shows that the permissibility of necessities is limited by the greater harm of violating sanctities.

Key Terms
الضرر (al-ḍarar): Harm or damage to a person or property; the law commands its removal
الضرورات تبيح المحظورات (al-ḍarūrāt tubīḥ al-maḥẓūrāt): Sub-maxim: Necessities make prohibited things permissible, but limited to the extent of necessity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi examples in the text, what is the ruling on exhuming a body buried without a shroud?
A) It is obligatory to exhume and shroud
B) It is permissible
C) It should not be exhumed because the harm of violating sanctity is greater
D) It is allowed only if the family requests

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Harm must be eliminated' is a master principle in Hanafi fiqh, but its application is always balanced by the rule that harm cannot be removed by causing equal or greater harm.
